OUT OF TH€ HURLY BURLY,

the attendant hurry-scurry of the celebrations, retired, at the earliest possible moment, our
own P. 0., and contributed his quota to the illuminations by lighting up a

THE BED PAGE OF AUSTRALASIAN NOTES AND QUERIES.
[It is desired to collect information from all manner of
readers and observers on all mnnner of ourious and in-
teresting subjects. A not-exolusive preference will be
given to notes and queries especially Australasian, or kin
with Australasian, in their origin and reference—parti-
cularly to those dealing with aboriginal life and language,
natural history, white-Australasian language, customs,
exploits and typical developments, characteristic old-
time anecdotes, and so on. Brevity of statement is
essential. Writers must observe the ordinary correspon-
dence conditions. The help of Bclletin readers every-
where is asked in recording some of many valuable facts,
impressions, or experiences which may otherwise be lost
to our country. Notes may be forwarded on any interest-
ing topic—not necessarily relating to something already
in print. Queries may be suggested; and any relevant
information will be welcome in reply, even if not precisely
answering. Contributions must be endorsed ‘Notes and
Queries,' and if dealing with matters previously referred-
to the number of note or query and date of issue must be
added. All references to books should state title, author,
publisher, date of publication, place where published,
and pages quoted from ; and all records of personal
experience should give date and place of occurrence
(province, district, miles from nearest post-town), and
other helpful details.]
3.— Whistling Spears.
Seeing this query, a party of us adjourned to a
convenient blacks’ oamp, and interviewed the
■pear-throwers. ‘Noise? Of course, the spears
make a noise,’said the old blacks, proceeding to
imitate it with their lips. A bleary-eyed old chap
took from the side of his humpy a medium-length
■pear, poised it in his hand, and threw it. ‘That
decides it; no sound,’ said one of us. ‘ Nothing
of the sort,’ said another. ‘I heard it. Let_us
■tand where it will pass us.’ This we did.
Another spear was thrown higher and further,
and all ears heard the whizzing vibration as the
spear passed. Again was the spear thrown a short
distance—sound almost imperceptible. Again
higher and further —sound definite, just as the
blacks imitated it. General conclusion was that
though a writer was .within the bounds of truth
when he sent his spears whizzing, it would be as
well not to emphasise the sound by too many
noisy adjectives. Then were some boomerangs
thrown : their noise was quite beyond doubt. One
of us declared the game drawn, saying : ‘Some-
times you hear spears, and sometimes you don’t.’
But the black fellows said : ‘Sposem you no hear
him, you wornba.’ Womba means deaf or stupid.
—K.S.
All sound is caused by atmospheric vibration.
In a vacuum there is no sound. Without friction
there is no vibration. If a bullet were mathema-
tically smooth the coefficient of friction=0 .\
sound also=0. Nothing is perfectly smooth.
Therefore everything passing through the air
causes sound. The rifle-bullet makes much sound
because the grooves cause a serrated section trans-
versely—just as boys nick a top to cause a hum,
or just as a circular-saw whizzes. A well-made
feathered arrow will not whiz so loudly as one
with oblique plumage.— Scotty the Wrinkler.
I can confirm A. Meston’s statement that the
‘fast-driven woomera spear’ flies in deadly
silence. No sound whatever attends its passage
through the air.— Mallee Cockle.
4. — Marsupial Reproduction.
I send three specimens of the embryo of the
kangaroo—one just born and placed on the teat;
one in the first stage of gestation in the uterus ;
and one in the uterus which would have been born
to atmospheric life in a day or two only tor what
happened. The kangaroo is born like all other
mammals, and is placed on the teat by the mother.
I have found them in pouch before they have got
on to teat. All this was proved by Professor
Owen 40 years ago; but it appears there are un-
believers still. The platypus controversy has also
long ago been settled. The female lays eggs, and
is also a mammal without teats, but with large
mammany elands. Both kangaroos and platypi
are monotremes (i.e., as in birds, the ducts of the
urinary, genital, and alimentary systems terminate
in a single opening).—H.G.R., Tenterfield.
8.— Ants.
In the Q. district lying between Isaacs and
Conors Rivers, on the Belyando River and a large
part of the desert, and on the upper Dawson and
the Brown, Comet and Nogoa rivers, ants throng
into the blacks’ camps. The fact has almost
originated a proverb. One frequently bears there
■uch remarks as ‘You’d think this was a blacks’
oamp, the way the ants swarm,’ and ‘thick as
ants in a blacks’ camp.’ Two kinds of ants infest
the camps—the common red meat-ant, and a
much smaller one that we call the crawling ant.
It has a tremendously heavy head and abdomen
for its size, smells very strongly of formic acid
when crushed, and is nearly stingless.—C.A.M.
I never knew that ants drew the (colour) line at
the aboriginal. The ‘ nigger ’ of my acquaintance
has tc take trouble to exclude ants from his camp
by heaping round it a ring of loose sand an inch or
two high, or digging a trench-enclosure an inch
deep. Ants hate climbing on loose sand.— R.
Williams, Gilgunnia.
In Westralia I have seen natives, after picking
their oamping-ground, look about for the small
black ants, and on top of these nests (not ant-
hills) they light their fires. One fire does three
blacks, and a camp of one hundred blacks would
require thirty-three fires—which would account
for some dispersion of ants, anyway. Have abo-
riginals in other provinces the same habit ?—H.H.
To prevent the common meat-ant from getting
at the jam, we tied four thicknesses of paper over
the top of the tin. Every morning there was a
fresh hole through the paper, which was put down
to mice, until the ants were observed going through
for all they were worth. They made a hole
about a quarter of an inch in diameter in a very
short time, tearing the paper with their jews.
Percy Wickham, Australia United Mine, W.A.
The Bulletin has been assured, on excellent authority,
that they breed a white ant at Normanton, N.Q., which
Is so fond of jam that it eats right through the tin.
9. — Sheoak.
Why is ‘colonial beer’ called sheoak? Was
the berry ever made into beer ? or the bark ? or
the wood ? or foliage ? It was Polly Lawson, of
Ballarat, shanty-keeper and lady, who first intro-
duced name and poison to — Scotty Shedrinkler.
‘ M.C.’ (13/6/97) tells how bush lads chew sheoak nuts
* because they are sour.’ This paper chewed some by way
of experiment—many palates would find them bitter.
Also, they moisten the mouth at first and dry it after-
wards. Analogical application to ‘colonial ale’ might
easily be made as the bush lads grow up.
10.— Aboriginal Legends.
The Waradgery blacks (country back of the
Lachlan, N.S.W.), used to ascribe the origin of
man and animals to a supreme creator. How far
this idea was their own I do not know. It may
have been borrowed from the whites. They gave
this curious legend as to the first entrance of
Death into the world : —When the blacks came to
Australia they found it peopled by a race of super-
natural beings called wanamg, who could pile up
mountains, turn the course of streams, and per-
form other similar miracles. On the appearance
of the blacks the wanamg left the world ; but
before going they drew the attention of their suc-
cessors (who had not yet begun to die) to a bat
which lived in a certain hollow tree. The blacks
were warned that if they disturbed this bat
terrible results would follow. For a long time
the bat was left alone; but one day a woman (of
course) incautiously struck a stick against the tree
and the bat left its hiding-place. Immediately
afterwards the ravages of Death commenced.
Mallee Cookie.
The Waradgery blacks, in the country between
the Lachlan and Darling Rivers, have a story of
of how the crow became black different from that
printed April 24. In substance it is as follows
Waggan, the crow, wa3 a knavish and tricky black-
fellow. Moolyan, now the eagle, was another blackfellow,
a bold hunter and warrior. Moolyan’s uncle (not named)
had offended Waggan, who crippled him by a stratagem,
and left him to parish in the bu3h. Moolyan’s dog found
the uncle’s corp3e, and led Moolyan to the spot. Feeling
sure that this was Waggan’s doing, Moolyan vowed
revenge ; and shortly afterwards, when Waggan had gone
into a thick patch of spinifex in search of game,
Moolyan set the spinifex on fire all round him.
Satisfied that he had avenged his uncle, Moolyan sat
down to roast and eat some game he had caught. "While
doing so he heard a loud ‘ Caw ’ from the branches of the
tree above him, and, looking up, saw Waggan, who had
changed himself into a bird. He was quite black from
the smoke and ashes of the spinifex, but he was unhurt.
Moolyan at once changed himself into a far bigger bird.
Before doing so he broke off the jagged ends of his spears
and fastened them to his fingers, and when he ohanged to
a bird the spears became talons. Thus armed, he started
in chase of Waggan, but never caught him. And so
began the unending war between the eagle and the crow.
It seems strange that the color of the crow should
require accounting for, seeing that, according to
both tales, he was originally a ‘nigger.’— Mallee
Cookie.
13. —Vegetable Caterpillars.
I send separately one or two specimens of these
found about here. The plant is known as the
bulrush caterpillar-native name, awheto. It is
found at the root of the rata, and the finest speci-
mens of the metamorphosed body are about 3 to
3| in. long. From this the stem rises to 6 or 10
inches. The body is under the surface of the
ground, and a great portion of the stalk as well.
The natives eat these dried caterpillars, and also
use them when burnt as color for tattooing. The
vegetable growth proceeds from the nape of the
neck, and it is inferred that when burrowing in
the vegetable soil the seeds get between the scales
of the caterpillar in its sickening state. —F.
F. Wate, Rotorua, M.L.
15. — Black Maria.
The following was printed in an issue of the
‘World,’ New York, some few years since:—
‘ During old colonial days, Maria Lee, a negress,
kept a sailors’ boarding-house in Boston. She was
a woman of gigantic size and prodigious strength,
and was of great assistance to the authorities in
keeping the peace, as the entire lawless element
of that locality lived in awe of her. Whenever an
unusually troublesome person was to be taken to
the station-house the services of Black Maria, as
she was called, were likely to be required. It is
said that she took at one time, and without assist-
ance, three riotous sailors to the lock-up. So fre-
quently was her help required, that the expres-
sion : ‘Send for Black Maria,’ came to mean—
‘Take the disorderly person to gaol.’ It is easy
to see how the name became fixed to the means of
transportation.’—T. O’Callaghan, Melbourne.
‘ V.' and others write to similar effect.
French slang for a police-van is ‘ panler k salade ’ —
apparently referring to the mixed contents.
16.— Waltzing Matilda.
The origin of this lies no doubt in some lonely
bushman’B association of his partner-swag with
the female partner that he Jongs for. A swag has
a suggestion of a dummy female figure ; and not
long ago ‘Bulletin’ correspondents pointed out
that a Dutch ‘ wife ’ was a bolster —a similar long,
rotund object. And I have known one old
tramper to carry the parallel so far that _ he
strapped his swag in at the middle for a waist,
and coaxed a little bit of clothing to stick out at
the end for a head, and found (doubtless) a, lot of
comfort in the companionship. Having got
‘Matilda’ —a typical name for the sex in comic-
song country—‘waltzing’ comes easily from the
idea of continued motion-often in a circle of
stations.—X.X.X.
There are several ways of carrying a swag,
Maoriland fashion, collar fashion, and Australian
—the usual way. A new-chum once asked an old
swagman which was the best way to carry it?
Well, said the old fellow, when I was in the army
I used to hump my drum in front of me, but since
I’ve been in the squatting line, I carry it behind
me ; whichever way you carry it you’ll get there
just the same. ‘Get where?’ asked the new-
ohum. ‘Between Hay and Booligal.’—H.H.
17. — Habits of Persons Lost in Bush.
As far as my knowledge extends only those
perishing of thirst throw off their clothes when
lost in the bush. Insanity overtakes them before
death, and they drink from, and bathein, imaginary
lakes and streams—tearing off their clothes to let
the water touch their parched skin. The ‘bushed ’
horseman travels in a circle either way, but I
believe the footman always turns to the left (not
the right)—I think because the left leg is the
shorter.—R,. Williams, Gilgunnia.
18.— The Maginnis.
The ‘Maginnis,’ or ‘crooked Maginnis’ is a
well-known wrestling hold. Standing behind an
opponent you grasp his left wrist with your left
hand, put your right arm over his left shoulder,
and crook the elbow firmly under his chin, so that
your right hand is almost on his right shoulder.
He is then at your mercy. It is a garrotting hold,
debarred in decent wrestling. The formula is
very old: the modern name comes from its
habitual use by a New York garrotter named
Maginnis early in this century. ‘Putting the
Maginnis on him’—applied in Australia to the
plan of tying a girth or whip-lash tightly under a
horse’s belly to make him buck—is an obvious
application of the wrestling phrase.—A.E.O.B.
20.— Aboriginal Bock Carvings.
In my note of 10/6/97, in the paragraph des-
cribing the situation of the rocks containing car-
vings, Fig. 4 should be Fig. 3, and vice versa. For
‘plate XXI’ of the Journ. Anthrop. Inst., read
‘ plate XVI.’ Figs. 2, 4, 5, and 8 are in the
county of Northumberland, and the remainder in
the county of Cumberland. — R. H. Mathews.
27. — A boriginals.
The W.A. natives that I have seen never stoop
to pick anything from the ground which can be
lifted by the foot. A stick they pick up with
their toes ; a billy-can they lift with the instep ; a.
stone they toss in the air with their big toe and
catch with their hands. A white woman may
stoop to conquer : the local black never stoops—
her foot conquers.— Hugh Harding, Cue, W.A.
Aboriginals have a peculiar sense of smell. In
the desert country of Westralia (I speak more
particularly of the district near Cue) they cannot
bear the smell of eucalyptus—because no eucalyp-
tus grows there ? I poured some eucalyptus out
of a bottle on a gin’s foot. She lifted her foot to
her nose k la acrobat, made a face, and said—
* ’Tinkin pfella! ’ These aboriginals also say
‘ white pfella ’mell nasty ; wearem too much
clothes.’ I have tried them with different scents,
but they invariably turn up their noses. Yet they
like the smell of the bad fat with whioh they
smear their bodies. —TlNDOG.
28.— Phenomenal Water-Springs in Drought.
Many newspapers of recent date have referred
to these. I quote account from ‘Sydney Stock
and Station Journal ’ of May 22, 1897 —
In parts of the Western country a surprising, though
not unprecedented, phenomenon has noently occurred.
In the districts about Bathurst and Orange streams that
dry up in any ordinary summer recently commenced to
flow, and are now running in full strength. Wateiholes
are full, and springs that usually cease to flow when-
ever the weather is at all dry are bubbling up with vigor.
In the districts to which this sudden supply has come it
was particularly needed. The citizens of Bathurst had
been placed on a limited allowance of water for consump-
tion, and now tire very works which had been undertaken
in connection with increasing the supply in future have
been stopped by the inflow of water. Professor David,
geologist at the University, who was appealed to for ex-
planation, staled that the. probable cause of the sudden
flow was that all the back country had been denuded of
grass, and that the water which the grass would absorb
has sought an outlet and come into old channels and
springs. In tire same way, he pointed out, after land had
been ringbamd the water in the nearest creek flow's
much more freely. Assuming this theory to be correct,
the flow would be distinctly attributable to the destruc-
tion of the vegetation checking the evaporation, and so
leading to tire water which otherwise would pass into the
air being discharged in the ordinary way through springs.
What amazing flapdoodle! Trees, of course,
not only draw surface water from the ground for
a considerable depth, but often hoard in their
roots large quantities—which are given out again
when the trees are killed by ringbarking. But
grass-roots extend, in average droughty country,
to a much less depth, and can contain compara-
tively little moisture—partly because they are
mere fibres, and partly because they only tap the
surface layer, where there is least water to draw
from. Besides, the theory is topsy-turvy. If
there were any water for dead grass to set free,
that same water would Veep the grass alive. Pre-
sumably the grass dies because it hasn’t got any
moisture round its roots —if it had any it wouldn’t
die—and the watercourses can’t rob a corpse
which has not a drop of property in the world.
—II. B.
[The following much more scientific explanation by
R. L. Jack, Q. Govt, geologist, in reply to a correspondent,
was published in ‘The Queenslander’ —‘Yours of 19th
May informs me that “some of the springs in the lime-
stone ridges on the eastern side of Ipswich, and also some
at Redbank Plains, about six miles distant., are now run-
ning strongly, and overflowing the successive holes in
their course, whereas, until recently, many of these in_
termediate holes were almost dry.” Mr. Clement L.
Wragge says, regarding similar phenomena observed in
New South Wales, “speaking with some caution, I can
only regard it as due to seismic waves caused by con-
vulsions or disturbances, most probably in connection
with the artesian supply being forced upward by the
twitching and contortions of the ground far below the
surface.” The 'Adelaide Advertiser’ of 14th May men-
tions as one of the direot effects of the recent earthquakes
that a well at Walkerville, South Australia, which had
been dry for twenty years, was suddenly filled with
water to the brim ; and many instanc es are given
of fissures opening in roads and throwing out
water and sand. These instances .and many others
recorded on similar occasions leave no room for doubt
that springs may rise or increase in volume from the
causes to which Mr. Wragge points. There is no
evidence, however, that the shocks extended to Ipswich,
or, at least, that they were perceptible. It may be
pointed out that the Western country furnishes many
weli-authenticared instances of springs breaking out or
increasing during periods of drought, in places and at
times when there seems no ground for suspecting sub-
terranean disturbance. This may be due to the length of
time required for heavy rain at the “intake” to affect
distant springs or artesian wells. Given a long pipe, and
supposing (as is always the case in water-bearing strata)
the pipe to be more or less obstructed, it may take
years for a heavy wet season or a prolonged drought
to affeot springs or wells which derive their
“heads of water” from distant sources. When
our information with regard to the Western wells
is complete, I should not be surprised to find that there
may, in some instances, be ten years of interval. It will
some day be possible to construct two diagrams, one
representing the rainfall at the “intake,” and the ocher
the pressure at the artesian outlets. The first diagram
will be like the edge of a saw, with teeth of varying
heights standing for good wet seasons, and teeth
knocked out for droughts. The second will be of the
same pattern, the teeth standing for periods of high and
the gaps for periods of low pressure. When observation*
have extended over a sufficient number of years for the
establishment of an undoubted parallel between the two
diagrams, a great deal of light will be thrown on the
phenomenon of intermittent springs.'] *
29. —Duplicated, Geographical Names.
A cause of misunderstanding which the Federal
Government should remove is the duplication of
geographical names. To begin with rivers, there
is a Mary river in Q. and W.A. ; Glenelg, Vic.
and W.A. ; Boyne, two in Q. ; Lachlan, N.S.W.
and Tas.; Styx, N.Q. and Tas. ; Gawler, S.A.
and Tas.; Macquarie, N.S.W. and Tas.; Nive,
Q. and Tas. ; Fish, N.S.W. and Tas. ; Swan,
W.A. and Tas. ; Lachlan, N.S.W. and Tas. ;
Murchison, W.A. and Tas. ; Fitzroy, Q. and
W.A.— Eknest Favenc.
30. — The Echidna.
I can quite agree with German Professor Semon,
who says the echidna doesn’t eat ants. About
18 months ago I found an echidna on a ledge
in an old shaft, with about two feet of water at
bottom. I.fished him out and carried him to an ad-
jacent red-ant bed, having first stirred up the ants.
The alleged ant-eater must have been hungry after
his spell below, yet he positively refused to feed,
though the ants swarmed all over him. After
making an attempt to burrow into the nest, he
watched a chance and scurried away at a very
fair pace, leaving my curiosity satisfied re ant-
eating.—E.D.E.

Various Verses,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“THE VOICE FROM OVER YONDER.”
“ Did she care as much as I did
When our paths of Fate divided?
Was the love, then, all onesided—
Did she understand or care ? ”
Slowly fall the moments leaden,
And the silence seems to deaden —
And a voice from over yonder answers sadly :
“ I’ve been there.”
“ Have you tramped the streets of cities
Poor? And do you know what it is—
While no mortal cares or pities -
To have drifted past ambition ;
To have sunk below despair ?
Doomed to slave and stint and- borrow ;
Ever haunted in your sorrow
By the spectre of To-morrow ? ”
And the voice from over yonder answers sadly :
“ I’ve been there.”
“ Surely in the wide Hereafter
There’s a land of love and laughter ?
Say : Is this life all we live for—
Say it ! think it, if you dare !
Have you ever thought or wondered
Why the Man and God were sundered?
Do you think the Maker blundered ? ”
And the voice, in mocking accents, answered only :
“ I’ve been there.”
HENRY LAWSON.
THE GRASS GROWS QUICKLY OVER
GRAVES.
Where are the loves of yesterday ?
Where are the leaves of yesteryear ?
Where are the zephyrs that did play
When summer was alive and dear?
Sweet sang old breezes to the ear,
But of them faithless memory saves
Not one small fainting chord to hear :
The grass grows quickly over graves !
Where are gone goblin, wraith, and fay ?
And where gone Merlin, hoar and sere ?
Where all we loved when we were gay,
And child-eyes could in strange worlds peer—-
Strange worlds of ghosts and kelpies queer,
Where brownies sang to fairy staves?
Far are those worlds that once were near :
The grass grows quickly over'graves !
Where is last autumn's yellow hay?
Where are the lilies of the mere ?
Where are the flowers of early May—
Purples that were as wedding gear?
To king or queen dead drop a tear,
While half-raised banner droops or waves,
And then the new king rise and cheer :
The grass grows quickly over graves !
Friend, ere the Fates have spun our cere,
And Charon ferries o’er Styx’ waves,
Long, lilt, live, love, while still we’re here !
The grass grows quickly over graves !
Vic.
STEELE GREY.
SIC TRANSIT.
Some great, grand song of Life to sing*
Now grant us, Muse divine,
On whose wide-roaming rainbow wing
Pale stars of Music shine !
Some flight beyond the common themes
Of common modern lays,
Some echo of those charmed dreams
Bards dreamt in buried days !
Ah, where’s the light that flashed along
Thy steel, Sir Cavalier ?
Ah, where’s the wild war-chorus strong,
Thou whiskered halberdier?
All flown ! They sell the name ye won
To buy a year of peace,
That ten fat men, from sun to sun,
May watch their hoards increase.
Your swords adorn the new-gilt walls
Of some gross plutocrat;
The shadow from your visor falls
Upon his sleek pot-hat.
His nervous son drones out his life -
A rank, unwholesome weed ;
He sells his girl to make a wife
For your betitled breed.
Bold bravoes in your cobwebbed frames,
Brave knights and courtiers gay,
Behold the “ Golden Peace” that tames
Our souls this later day !
Gay gallants of that ancient time, ■
I’d sing thee if I could ;
But gallant deeds ill fit to rhyme
In this dull age of wood.
And, —but the madrigal that long
Did make the singer’s fate,
The sonnet and the pastoral song
Are somewhat out of date, —
Fayre ladye in your ivied keep '
The moonlight streaming o’er,
Fain would I ’cross the greensward creep
To be thy troubadour.
N.S.W.
E. J. BRADY.
BRIDAL DREAMS.
By dim dream-ways, lit with pale mystic light—
Light shed we knew not whence or how - we sped
With flying feet o’er many a sweet flower-bed
Nestling within the opiate wings of night.
The flowers moved and murmured at our flight,
Then dozed again and closed their white or red ;
Their scented dreams caressed us as we fled,
As though Love bade them add to our delight.
Hand locked in hand, we gained a strange, vague
lea
Beside a mere that knew no earthly breeze,
Where lilies lived brief lives of languorous ease
(Giving therefor their dim white grace in fee); —
These had sweet things to say of her and me,
As down we sank and all our dreams did cease.
Vic.
LIONEL COLONNA.

Special Notice.
The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length , or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission
To Amateurs in Black and White.

other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
‘will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion—i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn —will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

SATURDAY, JULY 17, 1897.
A Comedy of Spasms.
The crusade against betting has suddenly
fizzled out in New South Wales, and in its
stead there is a violent outbreak of zeal in
the matter of Sunday trading, and the retail-
ing of bananas and ginger-pop on the Day of
Rust. There is no local law, so far as The
Bulletin knows, dealing with the subject,
but the Sunday banana is understood to be
illegal under an ancient English statute,
passed in the days of a monarch of bad char-
acter now deceased. It was decided very
long ago that the said musty statute applies
in New South Wales, but for years the
police have turned a blind eye on the Sunday
trader and passed by on the other side. In
so doing they admittedly overlooked a glar-
ing breach of the law, but in some matters
the local “cop” is allowed to unmake laws
at his pleasure, and no one in authority ever
called on the force to account for its whole-
sale neglect of duty. A week ago, however,
a sudden spasm of virtue overwhelmed the
unripe banana and descended on the ginger-
beer like an avalanche, and the I’eason of
this awakening is explained by Acting-
Premier Brunker as follows :
The action I took was upon a petition received from
about 2000 small shopkeepers, who complained that the
Italians were keeping their shops open on Sundays to
their detriment, and interfering with their business. I
called the attention of the Inspector-General of Police to
the petition. In fact, I sent him the petition, and there
is no doubt he is acting upon it.
* • * »
This leaves things in an eminently un-
satisfactory position. Apparently the inter-
ference of the Government comes in, not
because Sunday-trading is illegal and it is
the Government’s duty to see that the law is
enforced, or to promptly amend it if it is
the kind of law which isn’t fit for enforce-
ment. Nor is it because Sunday-trading is
sinful, and it is the duty of a GoD-fearing
Ministry to see that the Day of Rust is
kept holy. It is purely a matter of trade
jealousy. The good fruit-merchant and the
bad one both have bananas and mandarins
and pie-melon left on their hands on Satur-
day night. The bad man gets rid of a lot
of the surplus stock on Sunday, and thereby
avoids a serious loss; the good man has
the festering mass on his hands on Monday,
and makes a serious loss. The bad man gets
seven days’ profits per week; the good man
only six days’. The good man doesn’t sell so
much ginger-beer and pine-apple as he might
do on Saturday night, because people don’t
lay in a stock knowing that they can buy
from the bad man on Sunday ; also the good
man doesn’t find the rush on his liquors that
there should be on Monday morning because
the public, instead of being compelled to
save up its Sunday thirst, has been quench-
ing it on the bad man’s premises. There-
fore he asks that the law should insist on
the bad man’s bananas decaying on Sunday
so that the two may start fair with corres-
ponding piles of stale vegetables on Monday
morning. There is no question of public
convenience or public morality in the matter;
it is entirely based on the good man’s fiendish
desire to see the bad man’s banana get stale.
And Acting-Premier Brunker acknowledges
that he moved in the matter solely because
the aggregate good man asked him to help in
the pious work of making the bad man’s
Saturday night banana unsaleable.
* * * •
This isn’t the sort of attitude which was to
be expected of a GoD-fearing Ministry.
Sunday-trading is distinctly illegal. As the
Ministry made no effort whatever to abolish
the law it seemingly regarded it as the right
thing that it should be illegal. The conduct
of the police, who took it upon themselves
to suspend the law, was a disgraceful piece
of presumption and neglect of duty, and the
Government should have fallen heavily on
the force for its misconduct. Either the
holy Brunker disgracefully neglected his
duty up till now in not looking after the en-
forcement of a law which he held to be just,
or else he is now meanly enforcing a law
which he regards as unjust in order to secure
the votes and support of 2000 small shop-
keepers. If the law is unjust then the
Brunker Government should have the
courage to introduce a bill for its abolition.
If it is just it should be enforced every Sun-
day against every offender, not put in spas-
modic operation at casual intervals. It should
operate all the time ; not merely when
Brunker or any other person happens to
feel that way. Also, it should be enforced
because it is the law, and not because any
man desires, for his private interest or grati-
fication, to know that any other man’s apples
and mandarins are going bad.
* * * «
In such a matter the public convenience
should not be considered in the least. The
public makes the laws—in a clumsy, slow
and circuitous way, it is true, but still it does
make them. It also unmakes them. If the
community has been so foolish as to make, or
so tired that it couldn’t take the trouble to
unmake, a statute prohibiting it from getting
any refreshment on its one regular holiday,
the said statute should be enforced to the
uttermost until the community gives orders
to the contrary. The only way to get rid of
foolish or obsolete laws is to enforce them ;
the only reasonable use of good laws is to
enforce them. In the one case, they should
be enforced so as to keep the community
fully aware of their foolishness ; in the other,
so as to give the community the full benefit
of their wisdom. The worst thing that can
be done with a ridiculous statute is to leave
it on the statute-book, yet tacitly allow it to
fall into disuse. Then it serves as a means
by which casual bigots may harass their
neighbors, and venal police and informers
may blackmail them, and by which acci-
dental Brunkers may make themselves
instruments of spasmodic oppression.
* * * »
The Sunday-closing problem is, in some
respects, on the same level as the recent
cash-betting trouble. They are both regu-
lated by British usages which New
South Wales inherited in a purely
casual sort of way. For many years
cash-betting on racecourses was held to
be legal because, though it is unlawful to use
a public place for betting purposes, a British
Court had decided that a racecourse was not
a place in any legal sense. Therefore, cash-
betting on racecourses was permitted in
N.S.W., the Gop-fearing Government not
interfering. Then a British Court resolved
that a racecourse was a place after all, and
cash-betting became illegal in N.S.W., and
those who practised it were heavily fined,
the GoD-fearing Government again not inter-
fering. Next, still another, and a higher,
British Court upset this last decision, and
cash-betting on racecourses again became
legal in N.S.W., the Gon-fearing Ministry
taking this aspect of the case as uncon-
cernedly as the other two. Its first position
distinctly implied that it held betting to be
a thing to which no objection could be taken
—unless it was going back on its conscience
and its principles for some unworthy object ;
yet when betting suddenly became illegal
it didn’t attempt to pass a law 7 of its own to
legalise the bookmaker. Its second position
implied that it regarded the bookmaker as a
wicked person who required to be put down
for the moral health of the community, but
when he suddenly came on top again it
manifested no intention of putting him down
on its own account. It is impossible to get
even an expression of opinion on the subject
out of the spineless combination which calls
itself the GoD-fearing Ministry of N.S.W.,
and it seems to have absolutely no personal
conscience in the matter.
* ♦ * *
Its position on the Sunday-closing question
is For something like three
years it allowed the Sunday banana-man to
remain open with impunity, thereby dis-
tinctly implying that the Sunday-closing law
was a bad one and should be abolished. Yet
it made no attempt at its abolition. It now
takes measures to close him up, thereby im-
plying as distinctly that its previous conduct
was wicked or corrupt or grossly negligent,
or all three, yet it offers no explanation of
its past doings. And, by its own showing,
it doesn’t act as it is doing because the first
day of the week should be kept holy, but
simply because 2000 small shopkeepers either
ask it to do something that is wrong, or want
it to do something which it never before had
the courage to do even though it was right,
and which it seemingly does now, not because
it is right, but because it is better not to
aggravate 2000 small shopkeepers. What is
the Brunker Ministry’s own opinion on the
moral aspect of the matter ? Or does that
sneaking aggregation possess any opinion 1
The Suifield Case.
When Ann Chambers had been brutally as-
saulted by an educated man of high character
and intelligence who was not acquainted with
her, had never spoken to her, and had only
twice seen her; —who chose for the place
of his assault a spot under her employer’s
open bedroom windows, where her shrieks could
be plainly heard, and assistance was certain to
come to her at once—and who chose for the time
of his assault ten o’clock in the evening, just when
she was expected home, before her employer had
got into bed—although he could have waited for
her at many convenient spots on the mile-long
lonely bush road which she had just traversed ;
who was so full of sexual desire that he thus
risked his liberty or life —after permitting the
woman to clearly identify him —although (as the
Crown suggested) he kept a mi stress, and although
he could have gratified his passions in Larubton
or Newcastle without risk and with comparative
pleasure and reciprocity and who did all this,
and walked or ran three-quarters of a mile in his
bare feet to do it, although two women swore he
never left his room, and although neither himself
nor his suit of clothes was stained or injured,
despite the fact that he had a violent struggle on
wet grass with a sturdy 22-year-old woman whom
he left bruised and bleeding terribly, with her
clothes torn in a manner which only great force
could have accomplished ;
When all these remarkable things had taken place,
and Mr. Croudace and his son rushed downstairs
and found Ann Chambers “lying down crouched
up and partly on her side; with her hair dis-
hevelled, no hat on, and seeming terribly afraid ;
with her hands raised as if in fear, and terribly
shaky ”; then Croudace said, “ What is the
matter, Annie?” And the woman, just freed from
the grasp of a brutal assailant —who, at that
moment, was a few hundred yards away fleeing
for his life as fast as he could consistently with
keeping his bare feet unscratched and unbruised,
to show to the police next day—naturally replied,
“ Oh, that Suffield 1 ”
No, she did not.
Then she must have replied, “ Oh, that man ! ”
No, she did not. She replied, “OH, THAT
BULL! ”
Croudace, J.P., swears it; his son swears it.
They looked ; and there was the bull —“ about 30
yards from her, on the back road to the house,”
says Croudace, J.P.
* * * *
This bull was short-horned ; and the plot of
ground where Ann Chambers lay was “ its
favorite camping-ground,” says the gardener at
Croudace’s. “ Five nights out of the seven it
would camp there.” Croudace, J.P., says “it
was perfectly quiet unless ill-used. ” Let us see.
Croudace, J.P., says he “ heard that a boy
named Kennedy was injured by the bull”—after
some boys had ill-used it. The gardener says the
bull “ rushed him several times, and once nearly
caught him; sometimes he had to go round the
back way home ” —making a considerable detour —
“in order to avoid it.” The gardener denies that
he ill-used the bull. The gardener also says the
bull knocked a baby’s chair to pieces. Did the
baby’s chair ill-use the bull? And, singularly
enough, while Suffield was waiting for trial, the
bull attacked Mrs. Cole, an elderly, grey-haired
lady, as she was-coming home from church near
by ; inflicted injuries bearing some resemblance to
those inflicted on Ann Chambers, and would pro-
bably have killed her had not a young man beaten
it off with a paling—just as Croudace, J.P., and
his son might have had to beat it off Ann
Chambers with the sticks with which (anticipating
something of the kind ?) they provided themselves
before coming out. Next day the bull was shot
by Croudace’s orders —because Mrs. Cole ill-used
it? Why does its owner shoot a “perfectly
quiet ” bull when an elderly lady ill-uses it ?
* * * *
The bull, then, was very likely to attack Ann
Chambers ; she led the Croudaces to believe it had
attacked her; and as they stood there, stick in
hand, and saw her condition, and saw the bull,
they accepted her statement without question.
Consequently, Croudace, J.P., did not say, “ Non-
sense, Annie ; the bull is perfectly quiet; it would
not hurt you unless you ill-used it.” He said,
“ Has the bull hurt you, Annie?” She replied,
“I am hurt all over.” Says Croudace, J.P.,
“My son and I then examined her carefully about
the back and shoulders, expecting to find the
thrust of the bull’s horns ’’—all unconscious that
the bull was grossly libelled, and that the respon-
sible ruffian in the bare feet was getting further,
and further away every moment. Then Croudace
said, “ The bull has not horned you ; you are not
hurt, Annie; do you think you can get up and
walk ? ” They lifted her up to walk her in, and
she fainted on the way.
* * * »
And all this time, from a period before the ruffian
left her, Ann Chambers was unconscious. (So
she says.)
She was unconscious when the Croudaces
found her “seeming terribly afraid”—do uncon-
scious people seem terribly afraid ? She was un-
conscious when “her hands were raised as if
in fear, and terribly shaky ” —do unconscious
people raise their hands as if in fear, and shake
them terribly ?
She was unconscious when Croudace, J.P., asked
her a distinct question—“ What is the matter,
Annie?” and got a distinct, natural answer—
“Oh, that bull!” She was unconscious when
Croudace, J.P., asked her a second distinct
question—“ Has the bull hurt you?” and got a
second distinct, natural, consecutive answer, “ I
am hurt all over.” Do unconscious people then
talk, and answer questions, and answer them
sensibly and categorically ?
“We lifted her to her feet,” says Croudace,
JiP.-»-to her feet, not in our arms—“and sup-
ported ” —not carried —“ her through the fowl-
yard gate, from which she was lying a few yards
away; I can scarcely suppose her conscious of
what she was saying ” —no, not then —“ at the
time I said ‘lt is all right, Annie, the master is
with you;’ after going a few yards I felt her
growing heavier, and found she had fainted ”
then she had not fainted and was not unconscious
before .
* * * *
Now comes son Croudace to corroborate his
father. “ She seemed terribly frightened and
excited ” —how can an unconscious person seem
excited ? . . “My father then asked her to get
up and try to walk ; she said she could not. ”
(Here is a third clear, consecutive answer to a
distinct question.) “We then assisted her to her
feet ” —although unconscious—“and after going a
few feet she fainted ” —although unconscious.
For, said Ann Chambers at the trial, describing
the assault, “he (Suffield) was then kneeling down
in front of me, and I do not recollect anything
after that, and Ido not recollect anything more till
Monday morning when I found myself in bed at
Croudace’s house.” Dr. Stapleton, who saw her
about 11 o’clock on the night of alleged assault,
said “ she appeared upset ; she was not unconscious
or insensible; and, I believe, was capable of
answering questions put to her and of recognising
persons.” And any doctor who knows his business
will know that, if the Croudaces spoke truth, Ann
Chambers must have remembered their coming
before she lost consciousness, and probably
remembered something of the conversation which
took place while her brain was still clear enough to
answer rationally, and before she had faded into
the semi-consciousness which culminated in com-
plete unconsciousness when she was being taken
in to the house.
• • * *
The Bulletin believes that any unbiassed per-
son reading this commentary on the sworn evi-
dence will certainly find that the bull attacked
Ann Chambers, and that she remained conscious
after the attack until she was assisted to her feet
by the Croudaces. Then the blood left her head,
and she fainted —naturally enough. When she
was laid down again, the blood returned to her
head, and she recovered the consciousness sworn
to by Dr. Stapleton. But her brain was cer-
tainly registering impressions until she was raised
to her feet; and as certainly her brain gave out
those impressions as memory—clear or faint—
when she was almost in a normal condition next
day.
Yet Ann Chambers —primed to avoid incon-
venient questions ? —swore she recollected nothing
from the time when the man was kneeling in front
of her until the next morning—a good, sweeping
non mi recordo / /
* * * *
It comes to this, there was some lying on the
Crown side. And if it was Ann Chambers who
lied, please remember that it was upon her testi-
mony, upon her uncorroborated testimony, upon
her testimony alone, in defiance of many contra-
dictions, all the probabilities, and an alibi sworn
by two persons, that Thomas Suffield was sent to
rot in prison for ten years, whereof he has already
rotted six.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Justification of the Tote.
The English Court of Appeal, with its learned
tongue in its cheek, and a suspicion of learned
“ cheek ” in its tongue, has ruled that betting
within racecourse enclosures is legal. A race-
course enclosure is a public place in every sense
of the term save a betting sense, which is the
sense discovered, for the first time last week,
by certain Judges who had been asked to pro-
tect turf gambling from the law as it stood.
The law as it stood, or seemed to stand,
threatened vengeance upon all such as assemble
for betting purposes in any public place. Said
law was the result of a loud English outcry
against the growing evil of horse-race wagering
some 40 years ago. All classes agreed that the
evil in the ’fifties called for legislation, and an
Act, bringing turf-betting odds within the same
category of evil as roulette-betting odds, was
hung upon the statutes for bookmakers to peck
at. Street-betting on a wholesale scale was
immediately suppressed by the new Act. After
a while the private offices of “ turf commis-
sion-agents ” were raided by the police on the
legal ground that an office where anyone
may invest his ready cash upon a horse by letter,
is a public place for betting. Cheap clubs of
a purely betting character were likewise sup-
pressed. The iaw ruled that such clubs, not
being constructed on decent social lines, nor
having any restrictions as to membership, were
naught else than public enclosures for betting.
A man paid his entrance-fee as easily as he
could buy a ticket for a Tattersall’s Ring at the
races, and went into the “ club ” to gamble on
horse-races. Down with such clubs, said the
law. And yet all this while the law was never
enforced against the public practice of doing on
a racecourse the thing that had been forbidden
in streets, offices, and certain club buildings.
Betting was declared illegal ; magistrates re-
fuse! to recognise “ welshers ” as thieves—
but the “sport of kings,” which is rooted
in gambling, proceeded as recklessly as
ever for the amusement of lords and in-
fluential capitalists. Smaller people partici-
pated in the sport, and were in truth the main
support of it, but not on their account did the
police leave racecourse enclosures unmolested.
Albert Edward, Prince of Wales, and com-
pany w'ere the explanation of why public
betting continued to flourish. Last year a
League of snuffling pietists called attention to
this preposterous anomaly by prosecuting a
bookmaker for betting on a public racecourse,
and sundry J udges naturally ruled that a
“ ring,” or any other part of a public resort, is
a public place. This decision, needless to sav,
had to be nullified by some means, for the par-
ticular sake of Albert Edward and co. A
common sense interpretation of a wholesome
law was not to be endured by the British aris-
tocracy. So a new test-case was brought to
another court—the Court of Appeal—and in
this case five Judges ruled that betting in race-
course enclosures is legal. And the latest
English ruling, argues Ikey Mo in Sydney,
will govern Australian betting legislation.
Glorious news ! Racecourse betting is legal,
therefore any racing club may run its own
totalisator. Where is the need for a Totali-
sator Bill ?


The Sailors of a Leaky Ship.
Six or seven weeks ago the barque Glenmark
left Port Pirie, S.A., with a cargo of sulphide
ore for Europe. A week or two later, during
bad weather, in which some 50 or 60 tons of
cargo were jettisoned, she was compelled to
put into Hobart for repairs. The crew took
proceedings against the captain for negligence
at sea. Cruelty, incompetence, and gross care-
lessness were all alleged during the progress of
the case, but the charge of negligence (in re
sidelights) was proved up to the hilt, and the
captain was fined. As no previous Ho bail
record exists of a captain being fined at
the instance of (omiiioii sailors for a simi-
lar offence (a fact, proverbial among sea-
faring men, by the way), it is safe to as-
sume. apart from the evidence, that the
ease against the master was fairly strong.
Then the Marine Board surveyors inspected
and reported the vessel overloaded, badly
, trimmed, and leaky-decked. die captain was
ordered to lighten cargo and patch up his
decks. Some 200 tons of ore was bagged up and
stacked on shore. The M.B. surveyors then
reported a “sag” of about 2in. in the deck
amidships, faulty rivets, and signs of working
in various joints and plates, and informed the
captain that they couldn’t think of allowing
him to proceed round the Horn at this season
of the year unless the cargo was further reduced
and more repairs effected. Then they would
see about it. As a result of the fine for negli-
gence, the captain is alleged to have made
things particularly warm for his victorious
crew while engaged in carrying out the in-
structions of the Marine Board. So warm, in
fact, that at last all hands (15) revolted and
refused duty. They u'ere promptly arrested,
billed before the P.M., and sentenced to
seven days’ gaol. At both court proceed-
ings the crew stoutly maintained that no
matter what happened, they “ would never
go to sea in that ship again,” as, in
their united opinion, mutiny, bloodshed, and
total wreck would assuredly ensue. The cap-
tain’s proven negligence and the Marine Board’s
indefinite detention of the ship, together with
their own lively experiences between Port
Pirie and Hobart, look like pretty fair justifi-
cation for this decision ; yet, as they signed an
agreement when they believed the ship sound
and seaworthy and competently captained, the
penalty for refusing to allow the captain to
“make it warm ” for them is seven days’ gaol !
Under the circumstances an ordinary employer
would have sent his dissatisfied workmen about
their business, but sea-captains are evidently
not built that way. Perhaps the P.M. had no
alternative to making felons of these protesting
sailors, which seems doubtful. What is not
doubtful, however, is that the Van Dobson
Land magistracy has reasserted its right to the
reputation temporarily lost by the unfortunate
fine for negligence imposed upon an employer.
The Marine Board has a backing of insurance
companies ; no insurance company suffers
through the gaoling of sailors.

A Windeyer in the West.
Dear Bulletin, —
Some of thy experience as a juryman last week before
Warden Finnerty in his capacity as chairman of Quarter
Sessions at Coolgardie. I, in conjunction with several
Kalgoorlians, was dragged down to Coolgardie to serve on
the jury. One fellow-townsman who had been bidden,
but was unavoidably absent in Perth, had his name called
and was fined £5 ; later on his name was twite pulled out
of the box, and he was again fined £5 each time —that is,
three times for the one absence. Presently a man was
arraigned on a charge of stealing- a bike. His Worship
greeted him as an old acquaintance: “What! you here
again forstealing a bike ! You seem very persevering in that
direction.” (The man—aforeigner—answered “Nosav.ee”
or words to that effect.) Later on a jury couldn’t agree.
They were called in and sharply reprimanded. His Wor-
ship “couldn’t understand how so simple a case,” &c.,
&c.—same old yarn. The foreman asked for five minutes.
“ I’ll give you five seconds,” roared the Bench, and out
they were scurried. During the winding up of wordy
rubbish preparatory to adjournment for lunch, the jury
sent in word that they had agreed. The “ five seconds,”
however, had expired, the chairman refused to hear
them, and adjourned the court for two hours. But the
jury’s revenge was at hand ; for, on resuming, though his
Worship had summed up dead against the prisoner) they
returned an enthusiastic “ Not guilty ”!! His Worship
gave them a bit of his mind in vivid Windeyeresque.
But this time the jury were masters of the
position, and didn’t seem a bit hurt at the
chairman’s surprise “how twelve presumably intel-
ligent men,” and so on. During the whole sittings
the chairman’s attitude seemed so persistently aggressive
towards the various juries that they eventually brought
in verdicts mostly against his direction. When the jury
returned to. Court in the.last case, they added a rider
calling the Attorney-General’s attention to “ the uniform
discourtesy displayed by the chairman towards the various
juries,” and recommending that a real Judge should pre-
side in future. Flaherty— er, I mean Finnerty— said he
would send their message and also give Ms view of matters.
Foggarty— that is, Finnerty— is what I heard a leading
dry-blower call “ a very harbitrary gent.”— Lictor, Kal-
goorlie, W.A.
The N.S.W. Labor Party’s Decadence.
“ Primus” to The Bulletin :
Dear Bulletin,— The N.S. W. Labor-party (not the Labor
members but the organisation which professes to have
put these in Parliament) is apparently in a bad way.
Wlivii, in 1891, the working classes, sick of the two old
semi-Tory parties and smarting under the unionistie
defeats of 89 and ’9O, set up the Labor Electoral League,
that body included all sorts—Freetraders, Protectionists,
Single-taxers, Socialists, Unionists and free laborers,
horny and soft-handed sons of toil, and not a few small
employers of radical views and democratic sympathies.
A heterogeneous party of 36 was returned to Parliament,
and ere long there was a secession of Protectionists
“ more fiscal than labor.” This was but a reflection of
what was taking place outside. The movement for re-
form, which'at first was broad enough to embrace all sorts
and conditions of workers, became the prey of various
sectaries who strove to capture it for their own narrow
purposes. The Socialists, by means of wire-pulling and
tli': packed Conference of 1893, succeeded in con-
demning the Protectionist members of the original
Labor-party to undying hostility, and ukased as
“bogus” the Freetrade and Single-tax members who
refused to utter the shibboleth of Solidarity. Notwith-
ing the energy expended on them, the working-class elec-
tors in ’94 voted mainly for the old politicians or the more
Democratic Freetraders and Protectionists then in the
field. Comparatively few “ Solid ” members were returned
to the House, and when Premier Reid precipitated a disso-
lution in ’95, several of these only saved their seats through
the Government not running a candidate in their elec-
torates. In this year’s Federal election, Labor, which
thought to sweep the polls with its batch of 10, did not
get one man in, and now the prospect for ’93 is very
gloomy. Instead of the Labor-party outside broadening
its policy so as to get more support, it at its last con-
ference adopted Socialism as its main plank, which means
that the organisation henceforth consists of Socialists
only who are prepared to lose every Labor seat rather
than “ sacrifice principles.” As the Socialists consist of
two camps, it is likely one will declare the other bogus, and
the so-called Labor movement will end in utter collapse.
The trouble arose in no small measure through the system
m which the Labor Electoral League is built. It consists
of representatives of the various Labor Leagues, and a
league may consist of 500 men, or it may be organised for
the occasion in some wilderness, and consist of the
delegate, who appoints himself, and his brother, who
helps to appoint him, and the names of a very few
entirely uninterested people who allow themselves to be
put down as members to oblige a friend. There is very
iittle real limit to the manufacture of dummy leagues,
and the dummy leagues vote down the real ones, and
take the management out of the hands of the majority,
and commit the party, in their own irresponsible way, to
all manner of impracticabilities. The delegate who is
responsible to a body of genuine constituents must advo-
cate what Labor really wants; the one who is really
responsible to no one save himself and a gum-tree can
shriek at large and commit the party to any fad he
pleases. And the Labor-party is going down-hill. Where
else could it go with such management?— Pkimus.

The Cyanide Patent;.
“ J.” to The Bulletin :--
It is averred that when the application of the cyanide
people to amend their patent comes before the N.S.W.
Examiner of Patents the Attorney-General and the
Minister for Mines will personally oppose the application.
But why should these officials so ostentatiously step out
of their way to act as advocates for one of the parties ?
Presumably the Examiner of Patents is quite qualified to
judge whether an amendment proposed in any patent is
one which may be granted in terms of the Act. Then
why in this particular case must Messrs. Want and Smith
thrust themselves forward ? The mining industry is a
very important one, but not more so than any other, and
I fail to see why two members of the Government should
consider it part of their duty to champion this especial
one. If the mining community feel that their just in-
terests are endangered, by all means give them every
facility to put their ease, but not at the expense of
the general taxpayer. If the proposed amendment is
one not contemplated by the Act, reject it, but let
the official who is supposed to have special qualifications
for investigating those cases be the man to say so,
unfrightened by the legal thunder of Mr. Want, and un-
illumined by the brilliant intellect of Mr. Sydney Smith.
A curious contention has been raised by those who oppose
the cyanide people. They say that because of a flaw in
the original specifications thousands of miners are
using the cyanide process, and that if the patent
is amended in the direction sought these miners
will have to pay a royalty. In direct language
they admit that they have “ taken advantage” of
this flaw, and they claim that they must continue
to be protected against the man whose brains discovered
the process ! Arid the highest legal official in the State,
so i t is said ms prepared to back them up because they
represent a large proportion of the working community.
If the inventor of the pneumatic tyre had made a similar
slip in his patent, and desired to rectify it afterwards,
would the Attorney-General and, say, the Colonial Secre-
tary, feel called upon to defend the great body of bicyclists
from the claim of the inventor? And if I leave my front
door open and a passing burglar helps himself to my
valuables, will he be acquitted on the plea that he merely
“ took advantage”of my carelessness?
A Supreme Court vacation is on in Victoria.
Although there is no provision in the rules of
court for this particular one, the Judges take it
regularly every year, and litigants have to wait
their pleasure. If ordinary public servants
took a fortnight’s holiday without leave, they
would “ get the run ” in no time, and if a
County Court Judge took it, he would receive
secretarial raps over the judicial knuckles and
be cautioned against doing it again ; but then
his salary is not a charge on the consolidated
revenue as his Supreme Court brother’s is. The
original idea of this vacation was to afford
facilities for conferences with the Judges of
other provinces, but it hasn’t come off yet.
Anyhow, it doesn’t matter much this year, as
there is practically no business for the Judges
to get through.

Dear Bulletin, —Why do you advocate the
exclusion of colored races from N.Q. ? Is it
because you always stick up for the weaker
side? Read the following extract from
Mareeba Express :
A rumor having been current in Cairns that the
kanakas purposed making it warm for Europeans visiting
tiie races at Fretwell’s, Cairns, yesterday, the police went
out to the course, so it is alleged, armed with revolvers.
Probably owing to this forearming nothing eventuated of
a disagreeable nature.
The survival of the fittest—good Lord ! Are
the white trash not digging their own graves
by getting the force armed with revolvers to
protect their white persons from being ejected
from one of their own racecourses by
“kanakas?” Truth is stranger than fiction.
Mareeba Express is quite wrong in saying
“ Europeans.” Europeans in other black
countries usually take good care that they are
treated with respect and fear. Here the boot
is on the other foot. Therefore, Cairnsiles or
N. Queenslandites is the word, not Europeans.
The warlike islander evidently sees the defer-
ence with which the white Cairnsite treats the
Chinkie, and the islander, knowing hpw much
beneath him physically the Chinkie is, con-
cludes that the white is ditto, and evidently in-
tends to treat ’em accordingly. Why not
advocate “ Separation ” with a strictly-defined
boundary, and leave them with sufficient police
to protect them until by mere intermarriage
they are absorbed in the dominant race?—
Grilled Two.
—-
The failure of Chamberlain and Co. to extract
any concessions worth mentioning from the
seven Premiers suggests that the price will have
to be raised. A few years ago a very useful
Australian could be bought with a K.C.M.G.-
ship, but the bestowal of that “honor” on
MTntyre, John Lackey, Renwick, Jenkin
Coles, Zeal, and such cattle made it hopelessly
vulgar. The idea of trying to buy a man of
Isaacs’ calibre by offering to put him on the
same level as MTntyre and John Lackey was
an absurdity which showed grievous want of
tact and an utter ignorance of the relative sizes
of things. If it had been proposed to
raise Isaacs to the peerage as the Lord
d’Moses, or something like that, he might
have been bought, supposing he was for sale,
but the price offered was not high enough.
Similarly when the British Government chucked
seven Privy Councillorships indiscriminately at
the Premiers with a shovel it made a bad
failure. Honors that are distributed with a
shovel are little appreciated, and now that it is
understood that a P.C.-ship is the fair thing
for the commonplace Hugh Nelson and the
beautifully-oiled Braddon, and the heavy,
stodgy Forrest, the other men want some-
thing better. The idea of extracting valu-
able concessions from Kingston and Turner
by raising them to the level of Nelson would
only make them feel a desire to knock
out Chamberlain’s historic eye-glass and
jump on it. To the British Government, look-
ing at tilings from a distance of 16,000 miles,
Ren wick and MTntyre and Seddon and
Kingston and Isaacs and Reid all look the
same size, and that is just where the failure
comes in. The indiscriminate gaud which is
scattered around may buy Renwick and
MTntyre, who are of no use, but just because
Renwick and MTntyre have got it Turner
and Reid accept it with a grunt of dissatisfac-
tion, and either wallow reluctantly and un-
gratefully or else refuse to wallow at all. /
Editor Bulletin, —The holy men of the Coun-
cil of Churches do well to be running to the
Brunkers and Ghoulds and Cooks and Gar-
rards for aid against the introduction of the
Continental Sunday, since that wholesome re-
creative institution with its gaiety and bright-
ness threatens their monopoly with ruin. Only
why aren’t they game to say straight out that
St. Plate is blasphemed and the Sacrament of
the Most Blessed Collection outraged by the
People’s claim to enjoy themselves on Sun-
days? Instead, they hypocritically pretend
that Christianity is endangered by such
arrangements for the augmentation of popu-
lar happiness as the founder of Chris-
tianity prospectively approved, when He
declared that the Sabbath was made for man,
and scorched with His indignation the canting
Pharisaism that asserted the contrary. Christ
redeemed the primeval institution of a day of
recreation from the narrow restrictions set
about it by self-seeking sacerdotalism. Yet in
N.S. W., a so-called Christian land, greedy Sab-
batarianism, assuming the sanction of Christ’s
name while distinctly violating the tenor of His
precepts, is in league with Authority (scandal-
ously servile to its puritanic influences) to make
Labor’s Day of Refreshment one of sourness,
monotony, and gloom. Down with such clergy !
—J.S.
A few years ago, a paper was founded in
Sydney with the coin of the working Demo-
cracy, but gradually the small shareholders were
frozen out, and the paper is now owned by a
few big men, so it prints the following :
If so, ami if it be attempted, British Radicalism and
British spurious and ignorant philanthropy are but reap-
ing what they have sown. In deference to benighted
English faddists it has of late years been fashionable to
attempt to govern India by English methods and parish
machinery.
The present position of the N.S.W. Protec-
tionist party—in Opposition and also opposed
to the Labor-party, whereas the reverse is the
case in the other Australasian provinces—must
be, more or less, ascribed to its daily organ.
Ever since the Newcastle strike of 1888, in
which the Cowlishaw interest was affected,
the Star has been hostile to the workers ;
deriding their claims and gloating over their
defeats.
The much-discussed Robie case is a diffi-
cult one in any aspect. Woman’s - rights
claimants will, if logical, probably resent any
attempt to restrict a woman’s freedom to
suffer unmolested the effects of “Rough on
Rats.” Others, too, protest against any
attempt to enforce criminally as a legal duty
what is no more than a moral duty. The
old debating-society illustration of the drown-
ing man and his companion able to swim but
refusing to help when he could do so without
danger to himself, comes up in this connection,
as do the sad surroundings of Francis Adams’s
last moments. Coroner Candler wanted a
decided case on a man’s duty to prevent his
wife taking her life, but, it seems, “ there’s no
sich thing.” So “ Daniel Deronda ” has to be
pressed into service, and a case where a brute
of a mother was held not guilty of anything in
particular for allowing her unmarried daughter
to die in childbirth for the want of a midwife’s
assistance, which said mother could well have
afforded. Later cases, however, seem to show
a greater tendency in the courts to find a legal
duty if they possibly can, and if a duty exists,
criminal responsibility usually follows wanton
neglect of it.
Preparing for Premiers.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Scene: Office of High Official in charge of Colonial
affairs in London. High Official discovered
glaring at table covered with lists of visiting
potentates, programmes of amusements, lists
of precedence, cablegrams, &c., &c. A waste-
paper-basket full of K.C.M.G. ribbons and
orders stands by the table. On the table a
handbag full of Privy Councillorships. High
Official rings bell angrily. To him enters
subordinate official, the Honorable Somebody,
a very tired-looking youth.
High Official : “ Look here, this is a nice state
of things. I’m only just back from Monte Carlo,
and I’ve got to set to work and clear up all this
business. Now, have you got a list of the people
we are responsible for ? ”
Tired Youth : “ Ya-a-as.”
High Official : “ Well, who is there ? There’s
the Indian Viceroy, of course, and the Premier of
Canada —we know all about them. And then
there’s the chappie from China, my brother-in-
law, he’s all right. But what about these Aus-
tralian brutes? How many are there? Two, I
suppose—Premier of South Australia and Premier
of North Australia—eb ? There must be a North
Australia if there’s a South Australia ! Where's
your list —how many are there ? ”
Tired Youth : “ There’s seven.”
High Official : “ SEVEN ! ! Good God, are
the whole population Premiers over there ? There’s
not seven places for them to be Premiers of !
You must have made a mistake. Get the map ! ”
(They get the map and pore over it discontentedly).
High Official (triumphantly): “ There you
are ! What did I tell you ! There’s only five
colonies, even if each place has a Premier, which I
don’t believe. There’s Queensland, New South
Wales, Victoria, South Australia and West Aus
tralia. Now, how the devil do you make seven
out of that ? ”
Tired Youth: “/ don’t know. One of the
clerks made out the beastly list/’
High Official.: “ Which clerk ? ”
Tired Youth : “ I don’t know. How should 1
know one beastly clerk from another ? ”
High Official : “ Well, you must find out.
Seven ! There must be two frauds among ’em.
Nice we’ll look if we let two infernal pickpockets
loose among those Indian Rajahs all over dia-
monds. How are you going to identify ’em when
they come ? There’s one fellow I could swear to,
anyhow—a big, hairy, orang-outang of a man
about seven feet high. He was here before. I’ll
swear to him anywhere. What was his name
again—Gibbs or Gibson, or something like that.”
Tired Youth : “Dibbs, I think. Always re-
minded me of money, I know.”
High Official : “Well, perhaps it was, but I
‘think it was Gibbs. Anyhow, is he coming.”
Tired Youth : “I don’t know. How should I
know ? I suppose he is.”
High Official (at his wits’ end): “Well, for
goodness sake send someone here that does know.
You’ll get me into nice trouble, going on like this.
Send for a clerk that knows about it, and, mean-
while, we’ll have a go at this list of precedence.”
(Tired youth rings bell for clerk, and returns to
table to look over list of •precedence.)
High Official : “ See here, the truth’s this—
We’ve got orders from headquarters to soap these
confounded self-governing colonies all we can.
But, if we send their Premiers in to a function
before the Indian Princes —my goodness, the
Indians will stick ’em in the back with a tulwar,
or something. Then there’s the Indian, China,
Straits Settlement, African, and Crown colonies’
lot. Which is to come first, and which last?
Have you any idea ? ”
Tired Youth: “/don’t know. There’s a clerk
knows all these things.”
(Enter Clerk.)
High Official: “Look here; is there any
table of precedence in the office ? ”
Cleric : “Yes, my Lord ! ”
High Official : “ Thank God ! Who made it
out ? ” *
Clerk : “ Lord Titmarsh, when he was in office,
my Lord.”
High Official : “ Bless him and praise him !
See that it is followed with literal accuracy ; literal
accuracy, you understand—and I’ll take all the
credit if it goes right, and Titmarsh can take all
the blame if it goes wrong. So far, so good. Now
there’s another thing. How many Premiers are
coming from Australia ? ”
Cleric : “ Seven, my Lord ! ”
High Official (to Tired Youth): “ See ! He’s
made the same mistake you did. If I wasn’t here
to look after you fellows you’d run the empire to
the devil. Seven, indeed!” (To Clerk):“ Do-
you know, sir, there are only five colonies in Aus-
tralia ? Look at the map ! ”
Clerk : “ Yes, my Lord ; but there’s a Premier
of Tasmania and a Premier of New Zealand.”
High Official: “Oh, good gracious! They
say America is mostly colonels ; this place appears
to be mostly Premiers. Now, what do you know
about ’em ? How are you going to be sure that
some fraud doesn’t pass himself off as a Premier —
some anarchist, with a bomb in his trousers-pocket,
and blow us all kite-high ? ”
Clerk : “ We have photographs of them all, sir,
and a private and confidential cypher report from
the Governor of each colony as to their political
leanings.”
High Official : “Let’s have a look. Who’s
this fat, bald-headed man, that looks like a tallow-
merchant ? ” “
Clerk : “The Hon. G. H. Reid, my Lord, Pre-
mier of New South Wales.”
High Official (angrily): “ There you go again !
I tell you a man named Gibbs is Premier of New
South Wales —great, long, hairy man, quite dif-
ferent from this fellow. I met Gibbs often. This
is a fraud. I’ll take my oath. Don’t he look it—
look at his face, all jowl and jelly.”
Clerk : “ There has been a change of Ministry,
my Lord, and this is the present Premier.”
High Official : “ Well, he’s not much to look
at, anyhow. What does his Governor say about
him? Who is his Governor, anyhow? Hampden
—Oh, I was in the House with Hampden. Dry
sort of fellow, not such a fool as he looked. What
does he say about him ? Let’s have a look.
(Reads report mumblingly.) “ ‘ Truckles to
Labor-party . . . time-server . . . not
last long . . . change may be for the worse
. . ". no force of character . . . afraid of
the Labor-party . . . dare not take K.C.M.G.
. . . better be bought with a P.C.-ship.’
I like that —a P.C.-ship indeed for a ruffian
like this —an Anarchist without the courage of
his villainy. We bought Gibbs with a K.C.M.G.
Let this ruffian have a K.C.M.G. or nothing.”
Tired Youth (waking to in terestin proceedings):
.“ I read those reports. There’s one chappie there
rather a good sort. All the rest are awful rotters.
Read his report. Nelson I think was the name.”
High Official mumbles over Nelson’s report.
“ ‘ Fights Labor-party ... fearless . . .
can’t last long . . . has worked well for Im-
perialistic ideas . . . very courageous man
. . . will support Anglo-Japanese treaty.’
Ah ! that’s the sort of man. What’s he to get ? ”
Tired Youth : “Headquarters say they are
all to get P.C.-ships.”
High Official : “All ! Good heavens. Well,
if we can’t degrade that man Reid in any way see
that he gets the suite of rooms in the worst part
of the hotel, and give him a hard seat at all func-
tions. And, by-the-by, what about taking them
round? Who’s to do it? (To Tired Youth.)
You'll have to do it : I’ve got the Canadian and
Indian lot to look after.”
Tired Youth : “ Oh, I’ll send one of the clerks
and get them tickets for everything that is going
in the way of concerts and public receptions, and
so on. I suppose they can’t go to anything really
select.”
High Official (very slowly and deliberately):
“ I should think not. Take ’em to the British
Museum and the waxworks, and see that the name
of some unattached lord or other is always asso-
ciated with theirs. It will be put in their cables,
and help to damn Reid in the eyes of his friends—
and that’s about all, isn’t it ? ”
Tired Youth: “ And supposing they kick up a
row, don’t you know, if they’re not asked to any
of the really swaggah things? ”
High Official : “Mv dear boy, if they try
anything of that sort on, have them bayonetted by
the soldiers the moment they show their noses at
the gate. Fill up these P.C. forms, and see that
you don't fill ’em up wrong. And now I’m off to
the Club. Just be decently civil to these people,
but don’t go too far, because, you know, by this
time next year they will probably be back in their
shops selling sugar—l’m certain that man Reid
sells sugar, by the look of him. Au revoir 1 ”
Cable item : “ All preparations have been made
for the reception of the Australian Premiers in
London, and they will be the principal items of
the Jubilee.”
