Athbn.*um Hall Carl Hertz.
“ Mrs. Quinn’s Twins,” which is running
at Sydney Royal this week, is somewhat better
than its title. The name vaguely suggests two
large, sticky infants squalling in the alley by a
New York tenement house; also it creates the
idea that the play is a child-drama, somewhat
after the fashion of Little Eva, only that there are
two Evas, both pathetic, and each with a ragged
hole in a sock that isn’t so clean as it might be.
The twins prove instead to be two comical male
characters aged about seven, whom Roberts cap-
tures with difficulty and washes publicly. They
have no direct concern with the drama, but they
burst into it whenever it begins to lag, generally
with a large dog running in front of them and a
large, draggled mother racing distractedly behind,
and make a cheerful diversion. At other times
they sit round and accumulate dirt audibly. The
drama through which the twins and their anguished
mother and the dog sprint so continuously is a
little Irish pastoral of the usual sort. There is
the bad young squire who is hopelessly mortgaged
and who wants to marry the rich daughter of the
good old squire, begorra. Also, there is the
young hero in the fatally-beautiful tail-coat to
whom the girl has given her heart under difficulties,
and amid much interruption from the twins ; and
the background is filled in, as usual, with the in-
former and the peasantry that can’t pay its rint.
The praste and the pig with the circuitous tail
and the eviction have been left out, partly to
make room for the musical element of the show,
and partly because the piece is intended for
comedy, and the pig and the praste only fit in
where tragic intensity is the thing aimed at.
* * • *
There is no plot in particular, barring the
fact that the girl and the hero become reconciled
at last with the old man, and the twins, despite
great efforts to keep them clean, are left as
smudgy and as anxious to fall into the lake while
running after the big dog as ever. Still the play
drifts along very pleasantly. Maggie Moore fills
the part of Mrs. Quinn admirably and with a
brogue that is above reproach, and Harry Roberts
is all that could be desired as the hero ; also, Miss
Gill makes a moderate heroine in a nice white
frock, and the twins—represented by Ethel Bash-
ford and Nellie Finlay—are miles above the aver-
age of the ordinary child actress. Miss Laura
Roberts also does her little character very well;
and the other people, who are mostly plain and
ordinary, have comparatively little to do, and
really don’t matter, and they are only moderately
shocking even at their worst. Some good scenery
enables the twins to fall down and bark them-
selves amid congenial surroundings.
** * *
One production rushes after another so
rapidly at Sydney Opera House that it is difficult
to keep pace with things, and last week “Mari-
tana ” got in, and stayed one night, and departed,
before this paper had time to mention the circum-
stance. This week “Faust” is the bill, and,
taken all round, it is a very respectable produc-
tion. The scenery doesn't profess to have been
specially painted for this occasion only, but
Farley’s Mephistopheles would compensate even if
the back cloth was made of bagging. Pretty Maud
Lita, who seems to be acquiring more confidence in
her voice, acts Marguerite very moderately and
sings it very effectively, the “Jewel Song” in
particular bringing down what house there was on
the first two nights ; and when she is on her straw
bed awaiting execution with over two gallons of
fair hair streaming down her back she makes a
really nice picture. Then the back of the dungeon
dissolves, and three angels bear her off to para-
dise, while Jules Simonsen is being damned in the
wings. Jules, in an acting sense, is a most
lamentably ineffective person, though in a musical
sense there is something to be said for him, and,
considering that he had a benefit the other day
prior to his immediate departure for some place,
his presence in the nice clothes and very neat
legs of Faust is a surprise. Miss Lambert
is a rather good Siebel, and, except for one
military chorister who seemed on Monday night
to have a fit standing, things go very smoothly.
Even Fred England, who has, so far, been rather
a weak point in the cast, gets through Valentine
well enough, and the anomaly of a dying man
singing a column of small print in his last agonies
makes Valentine rather a difficult character to
wrestle with.
** * *
Melville Stoltz’s co., whose season at
Sydney Lyceum ends next Thursday, is booming
even a little more violently than at its first visit.
The Jordans have introduced some new feats, and
outrage the laws of gravity in one or two fresh
directions ; and Virginia Aragon does sundry im-
Kossibilities on her wire which were omitted from
er earlier programme. A most peculiar cyclist,
bearing the name of Olaf Schrader, is a late addi-
tion to the co., and Olaf on his bike is sufficiently
remarkable to pass muster even in such company.
The pedestrian humorists in general are as painful
as they were before, but the demand for their
sudden death remains unsatisfied. Possibly they
are being reserved for a worse fate. Still the
pedestrian humorists are only a casual circum-
stance, and if the audience takes them under
chloroform the rest of the show will fully repay it
for its trouble.
“ A Night Out” is still doing large busi-
ness at Sydney Her Majesty’s, and seems good
for a considerable stay. It is finely mounted, and
a large amount of legal tender has been spent on
it anyhow, and legal tender, when spent lavishly,
covers a lot of things.
** * *
The Avolos have caught on so well at Syd-
ney Tivoli that there will apparently be no neces-
sity to turn on any new attraction at the meter for
some time. They are described in the bill as the
“ only artists in the world who throw a double
somersault from six perpendicular poles”; the
fact of three men throwing two somersaults from
six poles sounds very like an arithmetical conun-
drum. So many stout, elderly politicians have
been turning somersaults in England of late
before the blessed Throne and dropping a shower
of their principles in mid-air, that it takes a rather
remarkable somersault to rouse much popular
interest just now, and the fact that a jaded publio
swarms to look at the Avolos, just after seeing the
extraordinary spectacle of a stout Premier upside-
down by cablegram, says a lot for Rickards’s trio.
** * *
Bland Holt’s long season, at Melbourne
Royal, deserved a more glorious farewell pro-
duction than “ The Cotton King,” which took the
stage on Saturday, and is expected to maunder
along its beaten track for a fortnight. “ The
Cotton King ” turns out to be a dull repetition of
the old, old melodrama wherein a gentlemanly
villain perpetrates hollow frauds upon his friend,
the hero, and makes several inane attempts to kill
or capture the hero’s girl. The swallow-tailed
plotter gets some assistance from an unsober
accomplice, who wears his shirt open at the neck,
and _ suffers occasional pangs of remorse. A
“ ruined ” spinster contributes lamentation and
woe ; also, Bland Holt is an irrelevant mill-hand
beloved of Kitty, said Kitty being Mrs. Holt;
and Corlesse, as the doting father of the
ruined one, is laid to rest in “a quiet corner
of God’s acre ” between the second and third acts.
His small, illegitimate grandchild is understood to
have been planted in the same spot. Charles
Brown figures once more as the festive Kitty’s
E articular Juggins, and Miss Flora Anstead is the
ero’s mother-in-law elect. The fact that every-
body concerned in baking this chestnut has been
exactly the same person, similarly circumstanced
in many former dramas, wouldn’t affect the pro-
spects of “ The Cotton King ” if it were a fast and
furious entertainment leading up to a battle or
steeplechase. It isn’t a full-sized Bland Holtian
success in any respect.
* * * *
The one sensation scene was said to have
been short of rehearsal on Saturday night, but no
improvements in the working can make it a new
variety of sensation, anyway. The heroine wanders
into the printing room at a cotton-mill and gets
trapped under an elevator by her bitter foe, who
rings for the lift to descend and blot the lady out.
A more amusing thrill was furnished years ago by
Charles Holloway, in an iron foundry, where the
villain made an elaborate attempt to crunch his
victim with a steam hammer. Apart from the
elevator, v. hammer question, “ The Cotton
King ” is a bundle of ancient devices ineffec-
tively displayed, so ineffectively that the gods
laugh sometimes when they are expected to take
things seriously. The characters talk more than
is necessary, the situations cast their unmistakable
shadows before, and the comic relief is weaker
than usual. A lot of bright, rural scenery and
solid carpenter’s work, likewise some respectable
acting by Walter Baker, Miss Watson, Albert
Norman and John Cosgrove, are wasted upon
“The Cotton King,” and The Bulletin feels
bound to reprimand the estimable manager who
could send up such a drama for a fortnight only.
He ought to have given it ten years in solitary
confinement.
** * *
“The Sign of the Cross,” at Melb. Prin-
cess’s appears to suit the souls or the curiosity of
most people, excepting ordinary, frivolous dress-
circle habitues. The attendance there at any
time is largely composed of Brethren who
have found salvation. The typical Brother,
shedding his oily beam upon this bea-utifu’l
play, is a droll study in self - gratulation.
He bangs his cold, damp paws together, and
beats the carpet with his redeemed feet, when the
patriarchal Christian (A.D. 64) says, “ The King-
dom of God is at hand,” for there is much
Y.M.C.A. comfort in the thought that the King-
dom must certainly have arrived and got to a high
state of perfection during the past 1833 years.
And, again, when the giddy Pagans (A.D.
64) complain that their modern waltz-
melody stands no chance against the iin-de-siecle
hymn tune of Early Christianity, the d.c. pietist
revels in the Pagan’s discomfiture. He refers to
the musical “fight between Pluto and Christus ”
as a noble dramatic conception, quite forgetting,
for the moment, that Messrs. Moody and Sankey,
likewise General Booth, bemoan the Devil’s luck
in capturing all the best tunes. And again, the
Brother aforesaid demonstrates with his four
limbs upon hearing, from the wicked stage, that
Pontius Pilate found no evil in the Nazarene. At
this point the enthusiast is apt to glance triumph-
antly around the theatre as though he would like
to know whether Infidelity can find anything to
say after that.
* * * *
“The Sign of the Cross” is a play to be
seen often, for the sake of the humor which lies
under its solemn surface. It grows upen the pre-
sent writer’s sense of the ridiculous, when con-
sidered in conjunction with press notices. As a
mere melodrama, apart from its blessed mission,
“The Sign” is not funnier than the works of
Sutton Vane, but as a show adored by the critics
it is a screamer. Consider the last scene, where
Marcus makes an offer of marriage to Miss Mercia.
The damsel, taken by surprise—glad surprise—
«Kclaims, gushfully: “Your wife! ’ Practically
she is asking “Do you really mean it?’’and the
situation suggests a proposal by Bland Holt to
Mrs. Holt, which is usually answered by
the lady falling upon her lover’s chest, and
inviting him to “say it again.” Mercia is await-
ing her turn to get eaten by lions, the bones of her
Early Christian comrades are being crunched in the
adjoining amphitheatre, she has comforted a small
boy five minutes before with the news that they
are all going to Heaven together, but, when Mar-
cus pops the question, she feels almost over-
whelmed by the honor, and fires off a gratuitous
confession that she has loved him from the first
time she saw his legs.
* • * *
Talking of Marcus and his scanty apparel,
Julius Knight was kept out of the bill by
a severe cold, two or three evenings last
week, and Gaston Mervale took his place.
The show lost weight in consequence. Miss
Ferrar likewise had to nurse her throat last
Saturday, and Mrs. Maesmore Morris walked
Srettily through the leading lady’s part. “ The
ign of the Cross ” is not likely to suffer any ill-
effects from these mishaps. Its success is founded
on the Rock of Ages, or something equally durable
for theatrical purposes, and Nero will figure as a
shocking example of Unbelief until further notice.
Meanwhile the beauteous Berenis is a powerful
antidote .to Nero’s influence. If Paganism—to
paraphrase Marcus—made many such women as
Berenis, it was almost a pity to crush it with a
religion that has produced Mrs. Chant.
** * *
The ill-fated Alexandra management seems
to be tottering. On Monday the house was closed
in consequence of “ unforseen circs.” ; on Tuesday
night it was to re-open with “Madame Angot,”
but without the assistance of Miss Fanny Liddiard.
A benefit for Howard Vernon is announced for
Friday (16th), and The Bulletin commends the
first Australian benefit of that valuable artist to
the patronage of the local world.
** * *
“ The Hermit’s Bell,” a trivial old operatic
story set to charming music, was performed at the
Bijou last week by a very amateur company.
There are only three or four important characters
to be struggled with, and the struggles of the
prima-donna (Miss Nellie Chapman) and a melliflu-
ous tenor (Alfred Pett) were quite satisfactory.
Also George Leopold, who ceased to be an amateur
when he was very young, helped to keep the show
going. The rest of the performance was over-
shadowed by the baritone’s nose, which bold
Hebraic feature will live for ever in the memory of
all who beheld it in profile standing out against
the painted sky. A false nose of equal propor-
tions, on the face of a low-comedy bill-discounter,
would be denounced as a wild exaggeration of
human probability.
** * *
On Saturday next (17th) Frank Thornton
is dne at the Bijou with a revival of “ Char-
ley’s Aunt. ” Melbourne was glad to see Thorn-
ton on every previous occasion, and this time the
city is expected to like him more than ever, for
strictly financial reasons. “ Charley’s Aunt ” will
be played at popular prices. Moreover, the early
doors are to be taken off their hinges, figuratively
speaking, in order that the man who comes early
to the rush may not complain of having to pay
extra for his enthusiasm.
*# * *
At the Athenaeum Hall, on Saturday, a
great multitude came along to see some more Carl
Hertz —or perhaps the crowd sprang from a sec-
tion of the community that hadn’t seen the
magician aforetime. Anyway, Hertz, and his
cinematograph, appear to be filling a long-felt
want. He gives the class of entertainment at
which the lion and the lamb can sit beside one
another and feel a united interest in the fate of
somebody else’s gold watch. And when the
Cine, begins, and the lights go out, and the
strange girl on the other side draws closer to your
shoulder—it is good to be there.

Prof. Norton B. Smith, “the Emperor of
all Horse Educators,” is just now taming fiery
steeds while you wait at the Rotunda, Bourke-
street, Melb. The Professor intends to take the
big cities of this horsey country in turn, and he
fives every promise of taking them all by storm.
'rof. Sample, long ago, made a deep impression
on the Australian buckjumper, yet he, as far as
one can remember, was several degrees below the
educational standard of the Emperor now in our
midst.
At Poverty Point.
The Firm derived a fine advt. yarn from the last night
of “Sign of the Cross” at Sydney. A middle-aged
woman, making her first visit to any theatre, on this
solemn occasion managed to get her unsophisticated arm
broken in the crush for gallery tickets, and was promptly
removed to the hospital by her daughter. The old girl’s
calamity didn’t check her determination to gaze upon the
Early Christians. Half-an-hour later she came back to
the theatre with her limb in a sling, and her daughter in
tow; the gallery was now chock-full, so the management
fixed up the interesting enthusiasts in its own private
box, and watched over them like a heavenly father, and
sent them home in a cab. Next day “ mother” returned
to the hospital as an in-patient, and her arm, at latest
dates, was mending slowly. It is an absolutely true story,
strange to say. The convalescent, who feels very like* a
Christian martyr herself, is full of a beautiful new faith
in the stage, and sings praises to J. C. Williamson’s name.
A travelling Australian writes from Munich under date
May 24 “We got here from Vienna yesterday, and
have seen several breweries, which are fine. There are
art-galleries here, but we are tired of art-galleries. I went
to Lenbach’s studio this afternoon, and saw this year’s
portrait of Bismarck. He paints one every year. It is
magnificent. His studio is a palace, and he has about a
dozen of the finest modem portraits painted—one of
Johann Strauss and another of Bjorn sen, the no\elist
being l transcendent. We have seen all the circuses, and
Dan Fitzgerald, who is here, is slowly making a selection
of acts which will probably open the eyes of the youth of
his native land—Hagenbeck, Busch, &c.’
There is talk of taking the “ Lelamine " show to Sydney
at the particular request of Gov. Hampden and sassiety
in general. In fact, Ferley, who acted as business fire-
brand at the recent Melb. revival of Lord Shaftesbury and
Co., has been asked to take up an advance agency for
“Lelamine” and set the harbor ablaze. Meantime,
though, nobody seems to have figured out the cost or
carrying an amateur opera mob 600 miles.

his trumpet, was stuck in front of Melb. Princess s.

the Daily Telegraph. Cherubic Williamson ! Seraphic
Musgrove ! Cruel of J.O.W. to refuse the theatre to the
Salvation Army.

night, stepped to the footlights and took one of the

do it every night, for all I know. Another step in dis-

H. : “The dialogue of ‘The Sign of the Cross’is bald, re-
calling Sheridan, on the purple patches of Richard Cum-
berland : ‘ Lumps of marl on a barren moor, encumber-
ing what it is not in their power to fertilize.’ An actor's
play. The bites in Barrett’s sentences are plundered
from Holy Writ, except one from Carlyle: ‘ What is Life?
A gleam of Time between two eternities.’ Whereupon the
amphitheatre gives the conveyancer a round of plaudits.”
The boss villain of “ The Cotton King ” strikes a new
idea in dark designs. He promises £5O to the husband of
a woman, who is suffering from an infectious sickness, if
he (the husband) will induce the heroine to sit beside the
patient and catch her complaint. Considering the odds
against anybody getting fatally infected whilst engaged
on so ordinary a job, the villain’s offer seems wildly reck-
less. A hospital-nurse would run the risk for two guineas
per week, and beer-money.
Fanny Liddiard wastes a lot of sweetness on the desert
air at Melb. Alexandra, alas ! More buxom than of yore,
Miss L. is also more dashing, and quite as pretty as ever,
whilst her mellow voice is just now at its best, and she
sings charmingly. A show with such a winsome lead-
ing lady, backed up by some bright male talent and a-
capital chorus, couldn’t have failed to do decent business
at another house —the Bijou, say. But, then, the Bijou
costs £6O a week for rent.
Lauri joins the Rickards co. shortly.
Those supernumerary Christians in “ The Sign of the
Cross ” are absurdly uninteresting items, and their final
departure gives a sense of relief to the audience. One
would like to think that the alleged lions were really and
truly waiting for ’em round the corner.

created by the scene in the dungeon of the ampitheatre,

ment isn’t cheerful.
Frances Saville is said to have made a remarkable hit as
Juliet. An enthusiastic listener writes that he has at last
discovered the Juliet of his dreams. As Violetta in “ Tra-
viata” also she was particularly successful—likewise as
Marguerite in “ Faust.” At Nice, where she appeared in
these characters, the audiences rose en masse and cheered
her, while the gilded youth smothered her with valuable
souvenirs.

season. The co. did just fair business in the sacred city,

Scot Inglis and his wife departed for New York last
Saturday, with no flourish of trumpets or farewell benefit
or any of the customary frill. Scot Inglis is the best
actor that Australia has produced up to date, and the
fact that he has found it advisable to look for his fortune
in another country should serve as a valuable discourage-
ment for Australia, junior. His career as a public
favorite lasted two years. The first occasion when he got
a chance to show a city audience really what he could do
was in July, 1895, when he played the cat-like clerk in
“A Bunch of Violets,” and completely overshadowed the
ill-fated Dacre as the boss of the Widow’s Mite Life In-
surance 00. (Limited).
ODE FOR AN AUSTRALIAN TANKARD.
“ Henri of Navarre Brownlow the other day married
at Perth Miss Rhoda Hay, the charming 1 Tasmanian
brunette, who formerly slew the Eastern world with her
smile.”
She slew the world. 0 subtle, extreme pain
Of so soft dying ! I, from heaps of slain,
Smiled grimly as I marked my fellow’s fall,
Knowing the wound was vital. Yet, withal,
Not one among us wished for life again.
And so, with glance of gently sweet disdain
Of the poor homage of each lovelorn swain,
With smile, and quip, and laughter musical,
She slew the world !
Now she lies low ! Eros, of pleasure fain,
Smote this, his doughtiest warrior—and those twain
Dusk liquid orbs which held our hearts in thrall
Beam with new light, unfeigned, potential . . .
Soon age. Then young lips curl, satirical:
She 1 —slew the world!
F. MORTON

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
On Wednesday last week Brother Brunker,
M.L.A., as temporary head of N.S.W. Government, in-
formed an inquiring member that “he would see, by
looking at the Treasurer’s cash statement for 1896-97,
that there was no deficiency.” Said statement was pub-
lished in the N.S.W. Government Gazette, No. 618, dated
Ist July, with Brunker’s signature attached as a guarantee
of spotless truthfulness. It said distinctly (page 4654)
that the year’s revenue was £9,309,269; and (on page
4673) that the “total payments chargeable to revenue
1896-97” were £9,476,145, and the “ payments chargeable
to previous years ” £4069 ; total, £9,480,214. And if that
doesn’t leave a deficit of £170,945, then this paper asks
Cow-merchant Brunker in a loud tone of shriek what it
does leave? The man who can sign and publish such
figures, and then assert that there is no deficiency, is a
nice person for any respectable married cow to be sold by.
Victoria is about the slowest province of the
seven to issue its complete revenue and expenditure
accounts. The revenue figures came along all right on
2nd July, but the expenditure is still a mystery, and
whether there is a surplus or a deficit no one knows.
Most probably there is a deficit. So far as the informa-
tion goes, the income is £6,629,612, which is £170,930
more than that for the previous year On the other
hand, it is £49,000 less than Turner expected. The
brightest feature is that the income-tax yielded £11,200
more than last year, so apparently the national income
is looking up considerably.
* * * *
Vic. Premier Turner’s luck doesn’t seem to be
worth a cent since he got his knighthood. His revenue
returns were £49,000 below the estimate, the whole short-
age being in the probate department. Something like
£lOO,OOO duty is to be paid on the two big estates of Mrs.
Silas Harding and Baronet Clarke, and if the money had
only arrived in time the surplus would have been some-
thing beautiful. But they didn’t come in time. An un-
reliable legend has it that when closing hour arrived on
the last day and no messenger with two huge cheques
could be seen sprinting up the street, Acting-Treasurer
Isaacs gave way to his feelings and broke nearly 30s.
worth of furniture and kicked his hat into the middle of
next session.
* * * *
The South Australian Government has, it would
appear, definitely accepted the offer of one Govett, an ex-
journalist, to act as hawker of the mortgage bonds of the
State Land Bank in England for five years, and has
thereby done a wildly-foolish act. Hitherto, these bonds
have been sold in S.A. ; they carry 3J per cent, interest,
and, as they fetched a good deal over their face value,
the bargain seems good enough. The yearning for a still
higher price led to the idea of selling in England, but,
instead of the business being done direct, Govett is to get
all the bonds issued for the next five years. When S.A.
3i per cent. Government securities are selling in London
at £l2O, he is to pay £lOB 7s. 6d. for the State Bank
bonds ; and the price goes down on a sliding-scale until,
with Government 31 per cent, securities at £lO4, Govett
is to get the 31 per cent, securities of the State
Land Bank at £lOO. This is how the bargain is re-
ported in the Adelaide papers and it lays over most
things in the way of Australian finance up to date.
First, Govett is an ordinary Australian journalist, not
a huge capitalist of the Hooley order. The names
of the people who are behind him in the scheme are
not given, therefore they can adhere to the agreement
so long as it enables them to buy the bonds at less
than their market value and sell them at a profit—
in other words, so long as it is a dead loss to South Aus-
tralia. When it no longer pays—in other words, when it
ceases to be a loss to S.A.—there seems really nothing to
prevent these unknown people dropping out if they feel
that way.
It doesn’t appear that the syndicate, whatever
It may be, puts up any substantial security that it won’t
drop the whole matter when it pleases, or go broke, or
disappear. The syndicate, whatever it may be, must get
the bonds at a lower price than the Government could
sell them at direct, for it must have a margin of profit for
itself somewhere. Selling in London means delay, loss of
exchange humping the money to Australia, more
exchange sending the interest to England, more exchange
later on sending the principal back to England when it is
repaid, stamp-duty to the Bull Government, loss of
income-tax on the interest, and loss to S.A. in general
through the local capitalist being deprived of a good
investment. Also, as likely as not, the agreement may
pan out so that the State Bank will get less for its
bonds in London than it has been .getting in
Adelaide. And, apart front all these things, the
Government which begins dabbling in syndicates and
delegating its duties to private capitalists is always to be
regarded with suspicion. The plea urged in the‘present
case is that the Govett syndicate is willing to give £lOB
7s. 6d., whereas the best local price is only £lO2 18s. But
the £lOB odd is dependent on S.A. State securities rising
to £l2O, and they are a very long way off that price, and
may never get there. At present rates (£111) the price
for bank bonds would be about £lO4, and after allowing
for all casual expenses and losses it isn’t at all certain
that that is worth any more than £lO2 18s. locally. A
very moderate drop in the market would bring the price
paid by the Govett syndicate down to £lO2 or so. Alto-
gether the scheme is not only foolish, but there is an
odour about it which will hang around Holder’s repu-
tation for a long time to come.
* * * *
In Queensland the State is the sole issuer of
paper-money. The scheme was met at first by much
opposition, and many financial authorities and eminent
persons who ran dubious banks proved over and over
again that it was absolutely impossible and ridiculous;
but apparently all these troubles have simmered down.
The State issue has come to be accepted as a matter of
course. At the end of May there were £972,609 of State
paper in circulation, and the coin reserve held against
it was £592,942, making it the best-secured note-
issue in Australasia. Even with this evidence at their
doors, however, the private-enterprise crowds in N.S.W.
and Victoria keep on proving the utter impossibility of a
State paper currency just the same.
* * » *
Some further amendments in the reconstruction
scheme of the Modern Permanent Building Society (Mel-
bourne) make it still more interesting. The depositors,
who represent about £476,000, are to have the option of
taking preference shares, which will get a dividend in
the remote future—l6s. worth of preference share for
every £1 of deposits. If they don’t want preference
shares they can get deposit receipts at the rate of 9s. 4Jd.
worth of deposit receipt for every £1 of deposits. The
receipts are to bear 3 per cent, interest (equal to less than
lj per cent, on the original deposit), and are Anally re-
deemable in 1908. The society starts with the idea of
writing down the deposits to 15s. in the £, but the miser-
able 3 per cent, deferred person, as above-mentioned, gets
only 9s. 4Jd., the other ss. 7Ad. being carried to a reserve-
fund. If the reserve-fund is a reality, this means that
the society has assets enough to give its depositor 15s. in
the £, but proposes to do him out of ss. 7id. And if it
really can’t give him more than 9s. 4jd in the £ because
its assets won’t go any further, then the reserve-fund is
apparently bogus and a good part of the preference-share
capital has no existence.
Considering that the society saves ss. 7|d. in the
£ by giving deposit receipts in lieu of preference shares, it
might be supposed that it would prefer the former device.
But, instead, it is intimated that only <£1507000 worth of
depositor shall be entitled to the blessed privilege of get-
ting 9s. 4£d. in the £ at 3 per cent, payable by instal-
ments spread over ten years, and those who want this
mode of settlement are invited to come early and avoid
the rush. The present shareholders, whose money is all
gone, are to get deferred shares for 10s. in the £ of their
nominal capital, and, after the preference shareholders
have had 5 per cent, dividends for three years, and a
sufficient reserve fund has accumulated to make up the
other ss. in the £ of their deposits, these deferred people
are entitled to get something per cent, if there is any thing.
If the reconstruction goes through, the interest bill will
be reduced from £13,500 a year to about £2200. Last
year’s loss was £11,400, but this was really loss through
depreciation of property sold. When the depreciation is
all provided for by writing down capital and deposits,
then the Modern, on last year’s figures, should be able to
give the reduced depositor 5 per cent, instead of 3 as
some compensation for his lost principal, and still have
a good margin. What Tub Bulletin mostly admires
about the scheme, apart from its mild assurance,
is the deadly accuracy of the person who figured
out the 9s. 4Jd. and the ss. 7£d. Only people who live in
a great, free country like this can fully appreciate the
advantages of getting 9s. 4Jd spread over ten years at
3 per cent.
* * * *
The Royal Bank of Queensland finishes a good
half-year with a net profit of £7463 as compared with
£6515, £6445 and 16033 for the three previous periods—
an eminently satisfactory result. A dividend of 2J per
cent, absorbs £5156 ; dividend duty on same, £258; £2OOO
is added to reserve making it £35,000, and the remaining
£49, with previous balance, makes £3920 at credit of
profit and loss. The improvement in profits arises through
the repayment of a large part of the reconstructed i'% per
cent, deposits and a consequent saving in interest. The
Royal’s balance-sheet looks generally satisfactory, especi-
ally as regards its reserve of cash and liquid securities
(£232,832) which equals 31 per cent, of all its liabilities to
the public, and it is feeling so good that the remaining
instalments of the call made in 1893 have been postponed
indefinitely. On that disastrous occasion the Royal called
up £187,500 (£2 10s. per share), of which £104,286 has
been already collected. The policy of adding a quarter
of the profits to reserve fund is steadily building up a
solid line of defence. Said reserve, by the way, is con-
stantly set forth in the balance-sheet as being “ invested
in Government securities,” but as the reserve is £35,000,
and the bank’s entire holding of Government securities
is only £31,959, and has been stationary at that figure for
12 months, the assertion seems to need explanation.
* * * *
The scheme for forming an English Assets Co.
to take over the remains of the Federal, City of Mel-
bourne, Mercantile, and English and Australian Mortgage
banks, appears to be meeting with acceptance. Yet its
only features seem to be that it will involve the cosh of a
London office in addition to the Australian ones, that six
irremoveable British directors will proceed to draw good
salaries out of the business, that the management will be
shifted 16,000 miles away from the assets, and that there
will be a huge sum to pay for stamp-duty on the transfer
of the assets, legal expenses, promotion expenses, cable-
grams, and stationery. Also the new co. proposes to
issue shares and debentures and so on, which means more
stationery and stamps, but as these won’t make the assets
any more valuable or more saleable, the only improve-
ment they can possibly make is that they will be handy to
fall back on as pipe-lights.
* * * •
The great Hooley, who wants to buy up all the
Australasian meat-freezing works with the pure and dis-
interested motive of getting better prices for the producer,
is accused of a very ugly transaction in London. The
charge (as per Times weekly edition) runs as follows : A
certain bike company was in low water, shares selling
below 4s. Hooley bought vast numbers of the shares.
Then he offered to buy the whole concern for £385,000,
but the agreement contained a clause, which seems to
have been kept very dark, giving him the option of back-
ing out of the purchase on forfeit of £25,000. When it was
known that the tremendous Hooley was buying the con-
cern the public felt so dead sure that there was a stupen-
dous fortune in it that the shares boomed from 4s. to £4.
Hooley sold, made, it is said, £lOO,OOO or so, paid his
£25,000 forfeit, and dropped out, and the shares went
down with a loud thud to the old wretched figure. And
the people who tell this yarn say that they were
cronkly “stuck” with the philanthropist’s shares at £4,
and want damages.
* ' * • *
Dear Bulletin,—ln noticing the 54th annual
balance-sheet of the Mutual Life Insurance Company of
New York, in your last issue, you quote the item “ In-
terest accrued and due, £468,140,” and add, “ The Mutual,
like several Australian offices, follows the bad practice of
lumping these together, and the amount of arrears is not
stated.” I need scarcely tell you that in the case of this
company the practice is only adopted for brevity’s sake.
The item, £468,140, is made of “ Interest accrued,
£424,101,” and “ Interest due, £44,039.” You also allude
to what you term the “very heavy expense rate equal
to ... a little over 20 per cent, on total assets.”
[Total “ income ” was what was meant. “ Assets ” was a
hideous misprint, the responsibility of which is hereby
put on the compositor, as usual.— Ed. B.] Assuming that
you mean “ total income,” I would ask you to remember
that before you can term this a “very heavy expense”
you must consider the new business obtained there-
by. Of new insurance actually paid for there was
added in 1896 no less than £26,448,008. It is
the influx of new lives in a company’s books which tends
to keep down the rate of mortality, thereby enhancing
the bonus profits of the policy-holders. Surely that ad-
vantage is worth paying for. The new premiums of the
Mutual Life of New York for 1896 amounted to £2,071,732,
bearing the proportion to renewals of 358 per cent. The
new premiums of the leading Australian office, to which
you refer, amounted to £104,727, bearing the proportion
to renewals of 88 per cent. only. In comparing expense
ratios, the volume of permanent premium-paying insur-
ance secured is a most important factor, and I think you
will find, when that factor has due significance given to
it, that there is no life insurance office whose manage-
ment is more profitable to its policy-holders than the
Mutual Life of New York.—Z. 0. Rbnnib, General
Manager. #
Sydney has a beautiful and capacious harbor,
and, according to her “great dailies,” foreign trade has
stimulated her shipping. Yet, of the large shipping co’s
trading with the port, how many are locally owned ? Not
one ! Melbourne, Maoriland, Adelaide, have each an
interprovincial fleet, but not Sydney, since the A.S.N. Co.
went bung. There is a moral in this anomaly.
• * • •
Elliott’s Investors’ Guide, issued in Melb., is
a very creditable pamphlet, giving market reports and
particulars of the chief investments of the day, as well as
an almost complete list of the mining co.’s of Australia,
with their share-capital, calls, divs., and market prices,
accompanied with a description. It has a dig at Mt.
Lyell: “There is no doubt the mine is a great property,
but we consider £lO a share too high a figure for the pre-
sent. The issue of reserved shares at equal to a discount
of £5 is no doubt reckoned as a div., but that cannot be
repeated very often, and only results in reducing the
amount of div. per share in the future. We would not
like to say that the mine will not ultimately return £lO a
share to holders, but repeat we look upon the price as
too high for prudent people to pay, having in view the
present position of the co., usual risks attending such
ventures, and the possibility of the minerals produced
falling in value. The future is being anticipated too
much. It is rumored that the last jump in share-quota-
tions has been caused by buying-orders from London,
consequent on London dealers being largely over-sold.
On the rising market few people are inclined to sell, but,
when the new issue is allotted, we anticipate that, in
the ordinary course of events, many people will wish to
realise their profits out of the issue. We reckon that the
shares will ease, unless London supports the market.
London buyers are an unknown quantity, and they rush
things up to prices that Australian speculators would not
dream of.” Since this warning was issued, Mt. Lyell’s
have reached £l2.
* * * *
The New Pinnacle Group Co., which took over
the property on Jan. 14, held its first statutory meeting
in Sydney early in July. The co. is financially weak. Of
£6250 subscribed £985 only is now available after all
creditors are paid. Ore sales for the period covered—
about five months—represent £lOB9, and it was estimated
that ore worth another £1125 was ready for despatch or in
transit, while che mining expenses, which don’t include
assay and sampling, horse-feed, rent of leases, directors’
fees, &c., reached £2345. Still, no doubt, a good deal of
dead work had to be done in straightening up the mine
after its ransacking by tributors.
* * * *
The voluminous report issued by the New Pin-
nacle directors is, altogether, none too cheering. It
doesn’t give the impression that the men in charge know
either what they have got hold of, or what to do with it
now they have got it. First, Mr. John Mundy, “a gentleman
of large and varied experience both in these colonies and
elsewhere,” having reported upon the mine, has been
appointed “ to act as consulting and advising engineer,
and generally to direct the mining operations, visiting
the mine for the purpose as often as may be necessary.”
Mr. Mundy may be all that is said, but he is unknown in
the Barrier sulphide industry ; and non-resident engineers
have failed every time. Then “ the solution of the sul-
phide problem has made the Pinnacles ore a marketable
product. . . . The question of dressing the ore up to a
sufficiently high grade and the consequent saving in
carriage, &c., appears to be the only matter likely to
retard the success of the company.” Why, that was the
very thing which “ retarded the success ” of all the com-
panies before the sulphide problem was solved. Up to
date that is all the solution amounts to. Apparently what
the New Pinnacles Co. proposes to do is to potter along
breaking out the best of the ore in sight, hand-dressing it,
and sending away the best of the product without any
attempt to mechanically concentrate or otherwise over-
come the special metallurgical difficulties which Pinnacles
ore presents. It is a poor policy in connection with a
mine of such great possibilities.
* * * *
B.H. South prospects continue indifferent : hard
ore, consequent small mill-runs, and poor silver contents
are the trouble. The ore worked lately goes only 6oz.
silver and 29 per cent, lead, with 16 per cent. zinc. Even
the zinc is too low to make the zinc tailings worth any-
thing. And the B.H. North figures are just a trifle worse
still; the ore is not so hard, though. But if it isn’t so
hard it is rarer.
* * * •
The concentrating-mill at the Silver Hill Junc-
tion has been started. A mere toy.
The sulphide parcel sold by the Junction to the
Smelting 00. of Australia (John Howell’s Ulawarra works)
is second-grade stuff—3ooo tons of it. The mine is look-
ing well; but the creep is causing so much inconvenience
and delay that, in order that shareholders may be spared
as much of the pinch as is possible, every available ton of
material on the floors is being realised.
• • * *
Isn’t it about time the Junotion North made
some provision for adequately dealing with its lower-grade
ores? For some of the co.’s oie is low-trrade.
• * • *
Four months ago it was mentioned in a “ Oat ”
par. that the game of the Yuba people was a London sale.
Meetings of shareholders in Adelaide now authorised the
directors to negotiate. The claim lies in a locality proved
to be the most unreliable in the whole Barrier district,
and nothing has been exposed in the mine itself to justify
the belief that it is better than its neighbors. And it is
also just as well to remember that pretty well throughout
the present co.’s career the mine has been worked with an
English sale in view.
* • * •
Birthday mine, Parkes, N.S.W., 170 tons 91oz.
This being the first crushing at the mine’s own mill,
attention is directed to the gold absorbed by plates.
Tailings assay nearly sdwts., but the manager says “ this
will be rectified in future crushings.” How ? The yield
is quite good enough from so big a reef. It would also
be well to know whether the reef, like most others in the
district, consists of blocks of stone with much waste rock
intervening, or is a continuous ore-body? That con-
sideration makes a heap of difference.
• * • •
Ross, Ross & Co. financiers, 352 Collins-street, Mel-
bourne, notify that money at very low interest is now
available on C.P. or Freehold Land. Owners writing to
this firm can always rely on prompt and straightforward
business.*
• * * •
The directors of the Great Boulder in London
“arrived at a very amicable settlement with the gentle-
man who came over from Adelaide.” They consented
not to take full advantage of the percentage-on-profits
allowed them by the articles, and had fixed a maximum
of £3OOO a year. They also allowed the appointment of
an advisory board in Adelaide, and one or two other
smaller things, and Dixon, the representative of the dis-
satisfied Adelaide shareholders, expressed himself pleased,
and promised to assure them that their confidence in the
board need not be disturbed. No reference seems to have
been made to the character of the mine when this profit-
sharing arrangement was first made. That would be too
cruel at such a gorgeous time. It is sufficiently indicated
by the price of the shares for long after the mine was
floated. Lane said at the meeting that “many Australian
papers said they were afraid to sink, but the develop-
ments at the 300 ft. proved that the Great Boulder was a
main fissure vein and was going down.” But it does not
prove that they were not afraid to sink. They were a long
time about it, anyhow. And they had good reason to be
afraid when they remembered the experience of other
Westralian mines in that direction. If sinking had been
Lane’s chief object with the Boulder it might be down a
thousand feet by now.

As usual ! “ Tributors at Penrose’s claim, Yul-
gilbar, N.S.W., have put through a parcel of 23 tons 14ewt.
of stone for the magnificent yield of 150 oz. It is under-

* * * *
“Haycroft’s ore-reduction works, W.A., have
shut down, under instructions from London. The reason
for this step is not disclosed.” But probably they have
been running at too great a profit, and producing too
much gold.
* * * *
The three famous W.A. mines. Lake View,
for the month, 5000 oz. ; Great Boulder, fortnight, 35850 z.,
and Ivanhoe, ditto, 14740 z. Over 2000 oz. of the L.V.
yield came from failings by cyanide, which is going to play
an important part in W.A., particularly at Hannan’s. The
other two mines have big heaps awaiting treatment; that
of the G.B. is estimated at £130,000.
* * * *
“ The reconstruction of the Brown Hill mill is
proceeding slowly.” Perhaps it is better to go slow this
time. Two years ago the plant was rushed up at a cost
of some £15,000, and it shut like an oyster after two or
three trial runs. It was a combination of dry crushing,
cyanidation and amalgamation, and an awful failure,
which, naturally, many shareholders attributed to the
mine and got out in the slump that followed. How many
men have been ruined by experimental machinery ?
* * • •
“ An alluvial rush has set in 37 miles from Kal-
goorlie. Only a few men are on gold.”—W.A. wire. A
change here, from the time when every rush was re-
ported as sensationally rich. Rushes don’t seem to be
fashionable nowadays.
* * * •
The copper-market has unexpectedly weakened
lately, and several revived prospecting ventures in the
N.W. of S. Aus. and on the Barrier have been abandoned.

A Queensland tradesman the other day gar-
nisheed a friendly society’s “ sick pay ” to a temporary
cripple. No magistrate should be permitted to garnishee
any such allowances made by benefit societies. The
creditor who attempts such an inhuman action is a
ghoul; the beak who places the legal power in his hands
is a patron of vampires.

metal mines on same date, 4‘3 per cent, were females. A
pretty big proportion of the latter are fighting starvation
on Cornish tin works, now shockingly depressed through
Straits competitibii.

SOCIETY
Wood, M.L.A. (South Australia), was par-
ticularly anxious that the Government should
have„ Joshua Beard “hanged by a Christian
man,” and not by a person with a criminal record
or a mere hired butcher. There is a lot of humor
m the idea of being hanged gratis by a sincere
professing Christian. What kind of apostle does
Wood suppose is likely to go in for that kind of
work? And supposing some devout preacher
could have been induced to hang Beard, and ho
got through with the job all right, and stooped
his respectable top hat over the trap to see
whether Joshua was strangling satisfactorily down
below, and then wiped his hands on his respectable
coat-tail and departed, how much better would
anyone be ? There is a sort of feeling that a
poem about the advantages of being hanged
cheaply by a sincere Christian would come in very
well here, but the lyric isn’t to hand.
* • * •
Another legend comes to the surface con-
cerning Joshua Beard, of Streaky Bay (S.A.), the
man whom Wood, M.L.A., desired should be
hanged by a Christian. The police have already
traced the bodies of two men who went out with
Beard, and were never afterwards heard of till
they were dug up in a decomposed state. But
sundry old inhabitants with only one reliable
TWO DAUGHTERS OF MAORILAND. —From copyright photo, by Hes, now of the
Tasma Studio, Hobart.
back-tooth among them profess that Joshua was
in the same line of business over 20 years ago,
though they can only locate his alleged victim
vaguely as a man with spectacles. Very little
is known about the matter ; it appears to have
been generally understood at the time that Beard
was responsible for the spectacled man’s dis-
appearance ; also that his property—one rug with
blood on it and a cake of tobacco —was afterwards
seen in good old Joshua’s possession. But no one
was sufficiently interested in the supposed corpse to
take action, and until a skeleton with goggles on
rises to explain matters at the general resurrec-
tion, it isn’t likely that anything will be known
for certain.
* • • *
Victoria and N.S. Wales differ as to the
disposal of the body of a judicially-hanged. If
there are friends to claim, N.S.W. hands the
body over to them, taking precautions that it
shall be duly buried within a stated time. In
Victoria the body remains the property of the
State, and is quicklimed within gaol walls. In
N.S.W. the hangman may go out and get drunk
as soon as he gathers up his rope, but in Victoria
he must coffin the corpse and drop it in the grave.
* * * *
Most of the newspaper-proclaimed “smart
captures ” by detectives are smart only because
the “information supplied,” which has made the
capture the simplest of matters, is carefully sup-
pressed by the d’s. Sometimes the facts become
public, to the consternation and discomfiture of
the force. Lately a gang of Perth (W. A.) burglars
removed a safe containing £7O from a house in the
main street, almost before dark, and opened it in
the bush. An hour or two later the rifled safe had
been found, and two of the thieves were in
custody, one of them having been captured with
the swag on him. The detective-office supplied
the reporters with an account of the glorious
achievement robbery at 7, detectives soon
apprised, got to work, soon found clue, followed
it actively, searched bush, found men just as
safe had been opened, recovered money—instance
of remarkable promptitude and efficiency of force.
But, alas ! beneath this par. in one of the Perth
dailies appeared a second, headed “ Another
Account.” It related how the burglars had hailed
a cab and driven off with the safe, having invited
cabby to stand in. Cabby had dissembled, and,
after setting them down at a lonely spot out of
town, and being told to wait, had driven back to
town and related the story to the detectives. Six
d’s then mounted the cab and were driven to the
scene of the safe-opening, and descended on the
way and ambushed. Cabby went on and met the
two burglars, who had meanwhile opened the safe,
returning to town. He calmed th or suspicions
with a yarn about having been engaged by a pass-
ing fare, and they entered the vehicle, to be
pounced upon by the six d’s a short distance along
the road, Hie “ Another Account ” was the true
one, but it. vexed the d.-office sorely, and the par-
ticular newspaper which published it, it is threat-
ened, must find its own burglary news in future.
Here is also “another account.” The d’s had
been expecting that, particular burglary, which a
“fizgig” had informed them was to be perpe-
trated, but a little later, that particular week.
When the two captured burglars were examined—
A GERMAN EXERCISE.
“Robert, have you brought my boots and some hot water (wasser)? ”
“ Yes, my lord, I have brought some hot (heiss) water and some lemon-peel,
some sugar, and some (etwas) Scotch whisky, but I have forgotten
the boots (Ich habe der stieffeln vorgessen).”
“ Good! If I can have whisky, I can do without boots (stieffeln)!”
10, one of them was the fizgig! He had been
trying to turn an honest penny by putting his
fiends, the enemy, off the scent.
* * » •
The clever capture in Melbourne of the
pious Scotch solicitor who was wanted on a charge
of defrauding the British Linen Co. Bank of
£40,000 proved, as such things usually do, to be
the very mildest kind of accident. The Gent.
One vanished from his usual haunts over a year
ago, and (though he is a conspicuous figure any-
where) all the combined Sherlock Holmes of Great
Britain failed to trace him. But one day he was
sunning himself on St. Kilda pier, when a Scotch
lady who had known him in his old pasture at
Kilmarnock passed by and recognised him. She
told the detectives, and the rest consisted in
cabling to Scotland for the warrant and keeping
an occasional eye on the truant through the key-
hole in the interim.
* * * *
The recent sentencing of English black-
mailers to imprisonment for life, while probably
administering a severe blow to a flourishing
industry, suggests that its abolition need not,
after all, be such an urgent concern of the Social
Purity people, since “our dear brother in the
Loard ” could never have been found—like the
prosecutor in this case —talking to the Man in the
Street at midnight, and couldn’t therefore have
accepted a hospitality which was to cost £4OO in
hush-money. Extremes meet; the Blackmailer is
as much a numble instrument ”as the Gimleteer.

Bananaland is essentially a land of strange,
unholy bargains. Even the joyous Kanaka occa-
sionally forms himself into a company for financial
purposes. Billy Mallicolo is a-weary of his bride,
and Tommy Tanna forms a syndicate to purchase
the lady. Eight' boys at £5 apiece put their
dollars in the pool and so scoop in the dusky
damosel, who loves them all duly in turn and
divides her charms
among the enrap-
tured eight.
Recently a col-
ored man on Thurs-
day Island bought a
native woman and
two children. The
piccaninnies lie hab-
itually ill - treated
until a native boy,
acting in collusion
with the mother,
stole a dingy and
carried off the
youngsters. During
a storm the boat up-
set, but the boy
swam on with the
children until one
of them died of ex-
posure. A casual
shark relieved him
of this burden but
did not cool his
courage, for the
dusky hero swam
on until he struck
the mainland and
was rescued. And
no medal—-no purse
of sovereigns not
even his uncouth
name figuring in the
papers!
N. : “Vainglorious statements are a chal-
lenge. Bishop Goe and his colleagues, in Melb.,
have been indulging in puff. In reality, the
Church of England is far behind the times.
Bishop Moorhouse was a refreshing indraught.
Before him was moss, and after him is the green
mantle of the standing, stagnant pool. The cathe-
dral is virtually insolvent. Imagine how Moor-
house would have vivified it ! Cabinet Minister
Best was put up at Bishop Goe’s luncheon function
last week to do slaver, but played Balaam, and
rung in about the folly of appointing English
prelates to Australia, cutting off the chances of
promotion and systematically keeping down the
tone and status of the men who enter the pro-
fession.”

In matrimony, as in chess, the end is very
often stale mate.
Will the damsels who ogled him shyly.
Or worshipped the god from afar,
Be startled to learn that their R.yley
Was hardly as young as “ Papa ” ?
Will Youth, which is said to be golden,
Feel tempted to get in a rage
When thinking how Charley—an old ’un—
Could pass for two-thirds of his age ?
Rich merchants, who prattle.so drily . ■
Of wisdom which cometh with years,
Ye are fools in the sight of a R.vley.
Your wives may address you as “ dears,”
But your aspect is quite uninviting,
Your manners lack softness, or dash—
And ye know not the art of delighting
The darlings who play with your cash.
What a “ jar ’’ for the opulent, thrifty
Dull fellows who govern the mart
To reflect that a “ hoy ” nearing fifty
Can reign in their womankind’s heart t
The “mash ’’-time for Fatman is over
Ere the autumn of life draweth nigh,
And he envies the evergreen lover
Those triumphs which money can’t buy.
Young life is a period measured
By moments of rapture sublime ;
Their memories carefully treasured
Are safe from the ravage of time.
In the trade-making world of the wily,
Whence love has been driven away,
One may live and be younger than Ryley,
Yet deader, much deader, to-day.
Melb.

Mrs. Barney, the Yankee W.C.T.U.
anostle, who has come to Australia chiefly .
to force the police authorities to keep
matrons in the lock-ups for the handling
of run-in women, has sense at her back.
In most lock-ups there is a policemans
wife but she has no official standing, and
the large-footed male tenant plays monarch
of the show. Why a reputedly sane com-
munity waited for an apostle named
Barney to come all the way from Bull s
Run to show the egregious blunder of
such a method is harder to answer than it
is to end the system outright.

The story goes that the proposers
of a certain amateur warrior—now tip-
topping it abr- 'ad—for • membership of a
fashionable Australian club had to with-
draw his name in a mighty hurry, be-
cause, no sooner had it been put up than
there came to every member of the club
a circular soliciting his patronage for the
firm of which the aforesaid warrior is an

In a certain coast-town of Northern
Mongrelia the wardsman of the local hos-
pital is also grave-digger to the com-
munity. Thirty shillings is the local price
of excavating a sepulchre. Consequently
the wardsman’s interests clash somewhat
with those of the grave-digger. . .
An empty hospital is ruin to the former
functionary, but may spell substantial
profits to" the latter. Probably they
whack the dividends. Should they form
a combine and play into each others
hands one shudders to contemplate the
consequences.
The utter futility of N.S.W. diseased-
meat arrangements appears again in the case of a
cow condemned the other day at Canterbury
(Sydney) as tuberculous, and thereupon sold to a
person who promptly proceeded to diffuse the in-
fection to the greatest advantage by cutting up
th ■ carcase for pig-and-poultry feed, and who—
.-•rini joke !—is to be prosecuted for carrying on a
noxious trade, ! Inspection, without power to des-
troy, is a splendid example of “ how not to do it.
N.S.W. Veterinary Medical Association
has probably made a huil’s-eye with the suggestion
that phthisis-poison is disseminated by the im-
ported tinned-milk which is so largely used, and
about the antecedents of which we know nothing.
Also wbat’s to show that imported tinned-meat
isn’t’often tuberculous ? The phthisis perspective
widens-out fast.
An instructive fact vouched for by a death-
certificate given by Dr. Atkins, of Wilcannia,
N.S.W. Johnston, a teamster there, was drenching
a pleuro’d bullock when the animal coughed into
the teamster’s open mouth. The teamster felt
something go down his throat, and was taken ill
next day. Dr. Atkins, who was called to the
case, pronounced the man suffering from pleuro-
pneumonia, and, on Johnston dying, certified to
that effect, attributing the disease to the bullock s
sputum. Moral: Don’t keep your mouth open;
breathe through the nose.

The slovenly cableman !
London, Ist July.
The survivors of the Aden disaster, despite their fort-
night’s misery, celebrated the Jubilee on board the
wrecked vessel whilst awaiting succor, and the
occasion by singing the National Anthem. Her Majesty
has cabled her condolences with them under the terrible
sufferings they were obliged to endure.
Does H.M. really share The Bulletin’s opinion
of the Unnatural Anathema?

The funds of St. Andrew’s Cathedral,
Sydney, are so low that there Is serious talk of
again cutting the salaries of organist, choir, verger,
and little fry. The stipends of the Bishop, Bean,
and Precentor will presumably remain intact. The
special service arranged in connection with the
Jubilee celebrations when all the potentates
Sydney elbowed each other to get in—secured a
few extra pounds, which was a perfect godsend to
the Chapter.

The youthful bride of an elderly widower
had an unpleasant quarter-of-an-hour on her wed-
ding-day recently. When she reached the church
where the ceremony was to be performed the
bridegroom was not there to receive her, but after
a long wait his youngest son rushed in with the
intelligence that “ Pa couldn’t find his teeth, but
he’ll be here directly ! ”
A LAMENT.
He journeyed ’Ome the other week
An ardent democratic learner :
How soon the Knight of Labor meek
Became a greasy butter churner !
Poor Turner
He joined the Tory toadies, who
Around the British Throne were flocking,
And gave the Queen a kangaroo —
A jest for watchful cynics mocking.
It’s shocking?
The emblem of our Austral land
She took, and, as a further favor,
She proffered him a royal hand,
Which Turner kissed, and liked the savor
(or flavor).
Of class distinctions in the past
An honest, unrelenting spurner,
He turned against himself at last,
And played the part of title-earner.
O ! Turner.
The kangaroo cried “ What a drop !
No principles than his were sounder ;
They caught the man upon the hop
Who might have been a nation-founder.
Vain bounder."

mainly) are not “ the largest producers.” The countries
that use mainly paper currency are leaving them far
behind. (3) Silver is no more variable than gold. A
while ago you bought a sovereign for 4oz. silver or for
201 b. wool or 6 dollars Argentina paper. Now, that
uncertain coin costs Boz. silver or, say, 401 b. wool or
12 dollars Argentina paper. Silver has kept about, the
same relation to tea and Argentina paper dollars all
through ; it is gold which wobbles all the time and won’t
keep still. (4) You say prices fell when silver was
demonetised. It never was demonetised. There is
more silver money afloat than there ever was before. (5)
Argentina is a paper-currency country. (6) About 40

A WOMAN’S LETTER .
Sydney, Jiily 13,1897.
The Juvenile fancy-dress ball in aid of the Con-
sumptive Hospital Fund happens on Thursday
(to-night) in the Town Hall. A committee has
energetically worked off tickets to a large extent,
and the hopefuls of several hundred families
arrayed in fancy garb will disport themselves for
flir;£ d i* aus ?\ To kee P d °wn the average of
fairies and cricketers and white-capped cooks,
prizes are to be given to those arrayed in original
and striking costumes. g
There is much disgust among the lady members
who took part in the recent Gymkhana held‘>by
the Hampden. Riding and Cycling Club over the
report of the high-toned sports meeting in a daily
paper Instead of figuring in a double-headed,
half-column notice, the itein was lumped in among
cycling notes along with doings of professionals
and other common folk.
r~, k ' rom 1 point of view of the spectators, the
iempe ball, at the Town Hall, on Wednesday
wa . s n< ?t nearly as amusing and exciting as
the function has been in former years. As regards
numbers the affair was much below preceding
averages, and the comfortable dancing-space
wmch each couple had to gyrate in did away with
the furious scenes of jostling and confusion which
the onlookers naturally expect at a smart sub-
scription dance. The loaves-and-fishes arrange-
f n£ Tfcof five men and two small boys to every
half-hundred girls seems to be the only one pos-
sible ; at hops now-a-days the girls have learnt to
make the best of it. An adroit plan whereby
some dozen otherwise attractive girls placed
themselves hors concours, by attiring them-
selves m weird night-gown-like wrappings
of cheap muslin and top-heavy velvet
head-pieces, worked successfully, for only males
of considerable courage were bold enough to dance'
With the maidens so attired, and this arrange-
ment left more partners to go round among the
normally-clad girls. Smartly-dressed coiffures,
smothered with violet-powder, made some fresh
faces look younger and fresher and added a score
of years and advertised wrinkles on features which
had seen harder service—-the invariable effect of
powder and patches. Sudden of steel
sequins on a black moire gown drew observation
to its fair wearer as imperatively as her own high
soprano, and one tiny woman offered to the specta-
tors interesting speculation as to how she managed
to keep her slight bodice from wilting entirely
away from her creamy shoulders. The ball was
rounded off by a plain, unvarnished supper, and
was kept a-rolling by Re Groen’s excellent band,
lne popular Les Fiances ” valse, with its sickly-
sweet refrain, reminds one at this date of the
flavor of stale wedding-cake icing, but it is the
most danceable measure the local talent has turned
out for many a day.
Ibsens “Doll’s House ” and other things were
discussed at Mme Juliette Henry’s conference at
her rooms in Hunter-street- last "Wednesday. A
fairly large crowd was entertained, notwithstand-
ing the attractions of the Teinpe ball, which was
going on at the same time. The lecture was in
T rench, and consequently, for many, the vagaries
°f the erratic Helmer and wife were not disen-
tangled of any of the mystery surrounding them,
and the problem of the door-slam was still un-
solved when the meeting rose.
Mr. Harry Hyndes farewelled the fair Cora B
Potter at Paling’s rooms on the Saturday before
she sailed. Mrs. P. rather underacted her partin
a blue gown remarkable for nothing in particular
but her hat was characteristic. K. Bellow was
attired as usual in casually-cut clothes. He is not
a tailor-made man in real life, reserving all his
sartorial triumphs for costume plays. Even on
the stage his modern costuming isn’t quite satisfac-
tory. His greatest social effect is in his conversa-
tion. On Monday the Potter following of devoted
maidens turned up at the mail steamer and waved
handkerchiefs enthusiastically, while their goddess
tore her bunch of flowers apart and distributed it
around, a violet apiece.
Not long ago Westralian Parliamentary sta-
tionery was distributed around Sydney wholesale
by a close relative of a Westralian M.P. The lady
brought over with her about lcwt. of gratis writ-
ing-paper with the arms of the province stamped
upon it. Even her orders to butcher, baker, and
draper were all written on stationery bearing the
L.C. brand. And many W.A. inhabitants wonder
at the enormous amount of stationery annuallv
consumed by Parliament. y
A well-known Darling River identity, who was
lately made a J.P., is more at home with sheep
than in polite society. While travelling to Syd-
ney he dined at the railway refreshment-room.
His neighbor was a new-chum dressed in latest
London style. The J.P. reached across the table
for the mustard, jostling the new arrival as he did
so The swell glared fiercely at the back-blocker
and said, indignantly, “Sir, if you had asked me,
1 would have passed the mustard.” When the
J.P. could speak, he burst out—“ Why, how was
a cove to know you was the blanky waiter? ”
Among the latest draws to make church attrac-
tive, High-Church parties are now holding first
Elaee. The youthful celibate curate, who is
ecoming too prevalent to please altogether the
adoring maidens of his flock, is being drawn into
this latest net under cover of “church work.”
On a moonlight water-picnic, many vows of
celibacy have been known to halt, and “ High
Girls ” are usually social somebodies, and blessed
with plenteous coin—worth making monkish vows
for.
The theatre entr’acte is going to be filled by
something else besides band for women who have
become tired of seeing their escorts regularly file
out between acts In one of the Sydney theatres,
a few nights ago, a fashionable-looking damsel in
the stalls whipped out needle and thread during
the interval and mended a glove. After this thin
end of the wedge, why not bring along the family
darning to the play, and “ the missis ” can execute
the necessary repairs while the old man makes the
usual exit ?
Now that Sydney street loaferdom has become
used to the bicycle woman, it has become tired of
the absorbing occupation of watching her pedal
twisting. But a considerate Transit Commission
has given it a new object in life by allowing women
to mount the perilous iron ’bus ladders and sit on
the roof, and loiterers can glue their eyes to the
steps as passengers alight—
‘Bor it’s not considered shocking
Just to indicate a stocking,”
as Deoima Moore used to sing.

Wheres the blanky logs?” A Govt.-educated
