A friend, writing from London, tells me that
tulle has entirely taken the place of chiffon on
smart gowns this season, and that hardly a yard
of the once-popular “ rag ” is used now except for
lamp-shades and sun-veils. This change, she
explains, is merely a wile of the drapers, for,
despite the fragile transparency of chiffon, it
stands a good deal of hard wear, not to speak of
washing and dyeing. . On the other hand, there is
an unostentatious stiffness about tulle which the
slightest crushing or damp will dispel, and with
it goes the whole virtue of the fabric. With the
most careful handling tulle stands only one wear,
whereas chiffon would freshen up cheerfully many
times during a long season.
During festivities at a Govt. House over the
water a meteoric person of foreign title ap-
peared at the free feed and jubed so charmingly
that all the other jubers were eclipsed. Her Ex.
was particularly gracious and' thawed under the
genial glow of the Baron’s presence, while the
suite took charge of the overlooked company and
quietly jubed with them in the background. And
it _is darkly alleged that the reason why
the fascinating foreigner felt so much at home in
swallow tails is because he wears ’em all day long
as he hands round corned beef and carrots.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“At the Princess Theatre these nights it’s edi-
fylll!? to see rows of church-goers gloating over
Mary Weir’s dance, slashed skirt and all, as
unctuously as if it were a revival sermon from a
popular preacher. And to think how these very
saints have hitherto carried on about the immo-
rality of the theatre, the immodesty of stage
dancing, and the indecency of the dialogue. Now,
just because there’s a flavor of sentimental theo-
logy thrown in, they complacently countenance a
production with fully the average amount of
naughtiness (as they would know if they had ever
seen any other play), and applaud unblushingly
midnight love-making, drunken buffoonery and
epileptic royalty. Undeterred by these seeulari-
ties, elders and deacons bring along their olive-
branches to hear the juvenile martyr screech, and
the early Christians sing hymns in first-class style
and unrestrained voice at forbidden midnight ser-
vices.
“In the ‘Sign of the Cross ’ the so-called ‘Mel-
bourne beauty ’ is a delightful vision as a slave, in
a delicious yellow robe and fillets of gold coins in
her hair. In the pose of a lute-player, however,
she falls short of perfection owing to the reed-like
proportions of her arms. ‘No woman with thin
arms was ever unreservedly attractive to a man.’
Though the dressing as a whole was admirable,
the Christians even at that early stage were
evidently remarkable for their dowdiness. Nine
out of every ten women who have seen the play
condemn Mercia’s flowing style of coiffure as
unsuited to the sparseness of her tresses, and yearn
for a gold-and-crimson evening gown, built on the
lines of Miss Elliott Page’s (Berenis). Ladies’
schools are being taken to the Princess’s to get up
their Roman history. So far the chief result of
the object lesson seems to be joy at the discovery
that the Latin Johnnies wore high-laced boots,
with heels, instead of floppy sandals.
“Dr. Clifford, the London Baptist pr'eacher now
witn you in Sydney, is in appearance a frail, shabby
ancient with a straggling, grey beard, a creased
navy-blue tie twisted round his limp shirt collar,
and, altogether, not a particle of ecclesiastical
foppery in his get-up. In the old country, a suc-
cessful dissenting divine is, or was, a bird of another
feather from his colonial brother. The former,
Radical in politics, more or less socialistic in
opinions, would speedily have barked his shins
against the watchful Nonconformist conscience,
had he personally indulged in man-millinery. On
this side of the water, the higher-salaried Baptists
and such, unchastened by persecution, school-
board and established church grievances, wax fat
and sleek. Their dress outherods Herod in its
immaculate sacerdotalism, and, so far from defy-
ing the 0. of E. parson, their chief ambition is to
be recognised by him as a fellow professional.
“As is the new world pastor so is his flock.
Local Wesleyans and members of kindred denomi-
nations, instead of glorying in their old tradi-
tions of humility, fearlessness, &c., constantly tilt
at the popular delusion that only the lower classes
worship in the minor tabernacles. They point
with pride to this or the other rich warehouseman
as a prominent communicant, and dwell fondly on
the social elegance of his daughters and the ex-
pensiveness of his sons. In fact, convention, pro-
perty, and the powers that be, are the idols of the
so-called dissenting bodies in Australia, no less
than of those religious institutions which lay claim
to the monopoly of the title * Church.’
“However, old-man Clifford, as a relic from
the former Nonconformist dispensation, puts on
no frill, and has a good deal of fire in him when
he warms up. Gov. and Lady Brassey, the
Mayor and Mayoress, Judge Hodges, and the
most prominent of local ‘ dippers,’ sat on the plat-
form m the Town Hall during his lecture in aid
°f the fever hospital. The wives of wealthy
wholesale drapers are always remarkable for the
solid excellence of their furs, mantles and such-
like, while the salient point in the outward ap-
pearance of successful softgoodsmen themselves is
that in their own raiment they never spare the
stuff.
. * The application of many Sydney women to
witness the strangulation of murderer Butler re-
calls the efforts of several Melb. ‘lydies’ to see
Deeming turned off. One prominent member of
the Austral Salon was particularly energetic to
obtain a pass, but without avail, •_ and another
alleged woman had hopes'right to the end; even
nasty remarks from Sheriff Ellis himself failed to
damp her craving. The very morning of the
execution she was at the gaol gates importuning
the warder on duty to admit her.
“ Each suburb of any pretensions is at present
trying to outdo its rival ‘ city ’ in everything save
the sewer-rate, and every aspiring Mayor seems
bent not only on going one better than his neighbor
but on rivalling the Melb. dignitary in style. The
civic hop-gatherings this year have been mounted
on a Eland Holtean scale of impressiveness. Haw-
thorn started with a fairly expensive display, and
others followed suit with added splendors, but
when Richmond took the field all the glory of
these preceding efforts departed from them. The
interior of the Richmond hall positively blazed
with hundreds of colored electric-lights, not to
mention flags, garlands of greenery, ferns, and the
inevitable V.R. Male creatures were accommo-
dated with a quite luxurious smoking marquee
by way of supplement to the card-room, in
which was a bar evidently well-equipped with
breath-perfumeries. The weaker sex, as some-
body remarked in a plaintive soprano, could
get nothing stronger than sherry. As a matter of
Jubilee course, there was a private reservation of
one refreshment-room for the numerous Mayors,
together with their family belongings, and such
members of the “ hupper suckles ” as had been
successfully implored to leaven the function were
provided with a card admitting them to the sacred
retreat, the door whereof was guarded by a brawny
waiter of chueking-out proportions. One was
amused to observe the foreign Mayors taking stock
of the fixings and mental notes of matters to be
unproved upon at their approaching shivoos. The
dressing, as a rule, was rather above the civic
mark, although, of course, the crowd included
many who emerge from the Ark on these particu-
lar occasions, and seem to return to Noah’s care
when the curtain falls.
“ Lady Brassey has shed the light of her coun-
tenance several times upon Miss Annie Mau
since that ‘scharmine ’ prima-donna’s unfortunate
retirement to the Homeopathic Hospital. Also,
floral offerings from Lady B. and the warbling
Earl have brought sweet memories of stage-fever
into the invalid’s dozy daily life. Miss Mau was
so seriously ill that her doctor refused to attend
her at her home, and insisted on her removal to a
hospital. She now lies in a private room in the
St. Kilda-road Institute progressing slowly.
Siiiellbourne is a blaze of wattle-blossom this
week. The * golden harbinger of spring 'is intro-
duced into everything in the way of decoration
without the least regard for artistic effect. A
Collins-street hostess spoilt the look of her two
handsome reception rooms and supper-table at an
‘ -AJ Home,’ last week, by too lavish a display of
the bush bardlet’s pet weakness. Another wild
enthusiast sent up a bouquet of the blossom over
the footlights to a Roman lady (Miss Elliot Page)
on Saturday night, and a third spoilt a dainty
wedding set-out by having bridesmaids’ posies of
wattle.
“Lindsay Gordon’s warmest enthusiasts have
gone West, and ’Ome, and even heavenwards, and
so scattered are they that the anniversary of his
death passed off without the merest shake of the
cypress. A few pedestrians down Brighton way
strolled into the cemetery to see that the poet’s
head-stone was still there ; but that was all. Even
his old friend Mount was so busy bringing out a
daughter at the Govt.-house ball that lie minded
not to come before the footlights and maunder of
the bard’s decease.
“ Marshall-Hall’s vanity is the most inflexible (to
use a paradox) of human frailties. It covers him
like a coat of mail, so that the whizzing arrows of
various critics glance past and hurt him not. The
‘Hymn to Sydney ’ is, Prof. Marshall-Hall
thinks, the finest thing (of its kind, be it said
parenthetically), in the new language. ‘ Did
you read my new poem ? ’ said the prof, to a lady
in a Collins-street bookseller’s, the other day.
‘No,’ said she, ‘ do you think I miyhtV (dubiously).
“Marshall Hall begins his orchestral concerts for
the season on Saturday, 17th, which recalls that
at one of the Wagnerian bang-and-jingles last
year a discordant note among the violins caught
the director’s ear. He descended from his perch,
and made straight for a guilty-looking person in
the fourth row. ‘ Tune your instrument! ’ said
the great man. The audience, who had been
pressing into the hall of Jove on the heels of Wag-
ner, came down to earth, with a great gasp, at the
sudden interruption to the proceedings, and gasped
again when they saw the Prof, detect the jarring
instrumentalist in a band of 40. Of course, it
7/iight have been a put-up job, but the incident
looked spontaneous enough.
“The prisoners at Pentridge (Melb.), who par-
ticipated so far in the Jubilee revels as to have
their sentences shortened a few days, sent up the
Hanthem in the prison-yard when they were
hned-up to hear the good news. Truly the reduc-
tio ad absurdum! One felonious bird blubbered,
and would have broken down but for his stoic
companions. And yet his emotion was wasted,
for he got no further reduction of his term in
H.M.’s gaol.
“Government House (with theaspirate)recovered
soon enough after the bloominations to give a
private dance to its friends (numbering, seemingly,
250 in Victoria) on Monday night last week. Mrs.’
Freeman-Thomas, who origiiiates all these side-
shows, kept things humming, and made the select
gathering, which had threatened to be deadly dull,
one of the brightest events of the Brassey regime.
“ The grief felt in many circles for Lady Clarke’s
bereavement is not mere surface sorrow. The
little fishes in the social net mourn sincerely that
the hostess of Cliveden’s retirement into the back-
ground as an entertainer deprives them of their
only chance of playing in sassiety circles. Lady
C. has always been miles ahead of the limitations
of snobdom, and her invitation-lists are long and
generous. She is the patron saint of all who can
get her name on their business-books—gives the
advt. of a nod to her tradespeople, and cranes her
neck from her carriage-window to bestow a bow
on her frock-artist. Whereas most fashionable
dames pass with a stony stare the woman whose
life, an hour or two before, they have been worry-
ing out over ruches and gathers.
“At the Bijou, on Saturday evening, as the
Victorian Operatic Society neared the end of its
three-night season in aid of the Fever Hospital,
the large and friendly audience acquiesced for the
billionth time in the hopeless inferiority of
amateur mummers to the “real Mackay.” * For
one thing, non-professional make-up, so far from
helping to make a heroine younger and
prettier, or a hero more martial and im-
pressive, is merely an unbecoming disguise,
adapted, apparently, out of compliment to
stage tradition. Then the raw recruit rarely hits
upon an effective costume. Remember the frisky
farmer’s wife, for instance (in the ‘Hermit’s Bell ’)
whose red skirt and green velvet Swiss bodice
made her look as round-backed as if hubby habitu-
ally drove her in the plough. And, to add to the
liigh-shouldered effect, she wore her hair in a
frizzed, roundabout mop, hiding every vestige of
neck. The dashing dragoon of the cast
had adopted an aggravating tinkle - tinkle
laugh, and constantly posed in profile for his
frequent kiss-tableaux, an error in judgment
for one by no means nasally deficient. The part
of Sylvan, the ample country swain, was taken
by an individual suitably named ‘ Pett,’ with a
sweet-enough singitug voice, but talking tones so
tearful as to excite the laughter of the well-
behaved gods. Peasant Rose Moineau, though
livelier than most amateurs, was handicapped by
her facial make-up, which gave her a staring hair-
teeth-and-eyes sort of expression, surprising in a
young lady who, in private life; is described as a
prepossessing damsel.
“ Here is one of the little things that make the
democracy hate the sight of a soldier. A party
was crossing in the military launch from the
Franklin to the Queenscliff Battery (Vic.) and
when it came on to rain ‘ cats and dogs, ’ the ladies
on board at once rushed the cabin, and among
them was the wife of a ‘common soldier,’ holding
rank no higher than a sergeant. The swash-
buckler in charge of the boat noted the offence,
and, immediately on reaching barracks reported
the presumption of the non-commissioned man in
not letting his spouse get wet through. The ser-
geant was hauled before the commanding officer
and, though allowed to depart without being
drummed out or shot for insubordination, an order
was posted in the barracks stating that in future
the saloon of the launch would be exclusively for
the use of officers’ wives.
Yours affectionately,
CLEO.
Out in the desert, far from apparatus, a
certain Westralian doctor administers enemas
with a bicycle pump. Patent applied for.
With the withdrawal of the protection
which made N.S.W. sugar a white man’s industry,
the colored races step in. It looks as if Reid’s
“ fiscal reform” will not affect the Sugar Co.,
while it will rum white labor—will, in fact, pro-
tect black against white.
Further information does not place the
purchase of the Parkes second-hand “not first-
class ” statue in a better light. It seems that the
late G.O.M. had inspected and pronounced it not
good enough even for St’Enrial Park. But here
is where the Parkesian element comes in. With
this statue were others of Gladstone, Disraeli, and
Dickens, by the same artist. These old man
Parkes bought as good enough for the Centennial.
Ex-Premier Jennings has died at Brisbane
of Bright’s disease. Although he had bei n
head of the State in N.S.W., no one regardfd
him seriously as a politician, and it is doubtful, in-
deed, if he ever took a solemn view of himself in
that capacity. He was of high education, refined
tastes, and unimpeachable honor, and one of the
most generous and compassionate men who ever
owned a big bank-account. He married a
Victorian heiress and was at one time of great
wealth ; but he mortgaged one huge property to
buy another, and then he invested in a third and
bought no end of land to get rid of selectors, and
in the end—well, many a 640-acre selector dies
more wealthy and more happy. Thus departs
another leading light of Sydney’s old “ cabbage-
tree ” club, the Reform.
The late N.S.W. Railway Commissioner
Eddy did many good things for the public, and
this in particular : he firmly set his face against
political influence in the railway service. And
the malicious spleen of the little lodge bosses and
mouthy politicians who had “ lived on the game,”
and whom he defeated and discredited, vainly
spits venom over his open grave. To those
who had the least acquaintance with Mr. Eddy
the printed slander that he indirectly suc-
cumbed to the effects of liquor is merely
ludicrous. But those who did not know
him may be told that it would be hard to find a
more abstemious man. His doctors constantly
told him that he must live more generously. At
official banquets he honored the toasts by sipping
at one glass of wine. Por seven years he had cold
luncheon with a cup of tea or milk in his office.
His work was his life ; it alone interested him ; he
allowed himself no time for recreation, exercise, or
leisure. And this high tension, coupled with a
temperament which worried incessantly, was what
broke him down. The story that employees con-
sidered him a tyrant is refuted by the fact that some
3000 of these sacrificed a day's pay and came, long
distances to attend his funeral, while practically
every country station sent either a wreath or ex-
pressions of regret. This sorrow was not show ;
it was sincere and spontaneous—nothing was to
be feared or gained from a dead Caasar.

acceptable.”

Muswellbrook Chronicle, June 26, says
! that N.S.W. Chief Justice Stephen has been made
a K.C.M.G. It is just 25 years since Judge
Stephen was Chief Justice, and he has been dead
now some three years. News travels slowly to
Muswellbrook.
Hartley Williams (Vic.) is the only knight
in Australia whose father was a knight before
him. Baronets, of course, don’t count, because
their sons can’t help that sort of thing. Kingston,
whose father was be-titled as Speaker of S.A.
Assembly, has had a narrow escape.
N.S.W. Opposition-leader Lyne objects
to “P.C.” as much as to “K.C.M.G.” Lyne
may some day become Premier and be in as tight
afixasG.H.R. A wise politician always keeps
open at least one avenue of escape as it is so hard
for Australian Premiers to “ refuse a lady.”
Frank Farnell, M.L.A., the rosy Recha-
bite who publicly scouted the flowing bowl and
still wags a monitory finger at the sinful pew-
ter, is the great-grandson of the man who first
brewed ale in Australia. The brewer, Squire, was
a soldier, and his brewery was at Kissing Point.
Federal Conventionist “Jack” Gordon,
ex-Chief Secretary of S.A. and the sweetest-
tempered man in local politics, will be 47 on July
26. Kingston, Holder, and Cockburn, with whom
Gordon was a colleague before the wretched Hos-
pital dispute separated them, were all born in 1850.
Ricardo : “Sir Cuthbert, as the French
put it, is the richest Vic. knight. He was the
prime legal milkman and oyster-opener in the
prodigious Ballarat mining litigation that used to
come off before Judge Molesworth the first, who
created the provincial mining law on such solid
foundations that litigation died out —and Cuth
bert became politick.”

ist, was travelling to Sydney per steamer and,

passenger to the man of basins. “ Yes,” remarked

soon.”
A sni-generis barrister, brother of an illus-
trious Englishman, was “run in” the other day as a
noisy “drunk ”; he had been hammering “after
hours” at the door of a sixpenny lodging-house.
“Discharged ! ” cried the beak, and immediately
the name of a Chinaman was called as the next
case. “I appear for the prisoner,” said the bar-
rister, as he meandered from the spikes-compart-
ment to the lawyer’s table. He had “improved
the shining hours ” of incarceration !

S.A. Democrats tremblingly wonder
whether Premier Kingston is wearing in London
that faded holland suit which has nobly stuck to
him through good and ill, and, further, whether
the shabby terai hat, so prominent on the day of
his departure, has now, alas, been discarded and
forgotten amidst the vanities of the Jubilee cele-
brations. “ Don’t you worry, boys, I won’t for-
get you,” said Kingston in April. But what of
July? The Right Hon. C. C. Kingston, P.C.,
D.C.L., in a terai hat! What would Albert
Edward say?
Many good stories, mostly Rabelaisian, are
told of the late George Hill, the Sydney sporting
man. One day a flash person, who had just ac-
quired a huge diamond ring, sought to have Hill
admire it, and twiddled it under his nose; but
Hill declined to take notice. At last, in despera-
tion, the diamond man said : “ Fine stone, isn’t it,
Mr. Hill—what do you think of it ? ” “I think,”
said old George, very deliberately, “that it’s like
a brass knocker on the door of a ” (com-
pound Saxon word).
“ Rev. G. D. Buchanan, parson-elect of
Chalmers Church, Sydney, made no idle boast
when he recently declared that one of the most
encouraging features of his work in Brisbane had
been the influence of his preaching on the com-
mercial travellers. Last night the members of
the Brisbane Commercial Travellers’ Association
were to entertain Mr. Buchanan at a farewell
social.” Pastor Buchanan is the one cleric in all
Australia eminently fitted to act as ghostly com-
forter to the average drummer.

S.A. Gov. Buxton’s married daughters
have all wedded parsons, and now Conybeare,
the assistant private secretary, who is en-
gaged to the youngest daughter, has departed
for England to take “ holy orders.” Capt.
Guise, who is engaged to the other available
daughter, and has almost covered S.A. with tracts,
is also ambitious to become a pulpit-thumper.
Harold and Leland Buxton, who have gone Eng-
land wards with Conybeare, are also similarly des-
tined. It must be indeed saddening to Gov.
Buxton to think that with all this piety the Bux-
tons will be best remembered in connection with
the family beer.

A S.M. Herald scribe jeers a solemn jeer
at the absurd iteration of “ honorable ” in parlia-
mentary procedure. But the Herald herself
never fails in her cablegrams to set forth, with
true yellowplush unction, “ The Right Hon. G. H.
Reid, P.C.,” and so on with the rest of the junket-
ing premiers. What’s the matter with, for in-
stance, “Premier Reid? ” A turnip by any other
name!

A Falstaffian officer of N.S.W. khakee
soldiery puffed his way through the crowd at the
close of a Sydney review' lately. “ See him ! ”
said a Stars-and-Stripes man ; “in my country it
is the law that no fat man shall wear a commission.
Obesity is a standard disqualification. When fat
shows, the man is retired.” A good rule, too, in
general; but if The Bulletin was in a beleagured
city it would like the commandant to be fat.
Henry Ayers, the S.A. knight who re-
cently died, lai d the foundation of his wealth
by playing the harp in Adelaide hotels in the
early days. He made a “ rise ” out of the
S.A. copper-boom in the forties, and was
the largest shareholder in the Burra mine.
There was once a politician who was
cited as co-respondent in divorce, and was
so conclusively bowled-out that there was
no getaway. So, the lady having “cleared,”
he put the best possible face on matters by
calling his constituents together and pub-
licly asking forgiveness. Armed with a
stack of Hansard, he fired off one after
another the beneficial acts which he had
been instrumental in placing on the statute-
book, and asked in broken accents that all"
these good deeds should weigh against his
one error. As the last word left liis lips
the politician reeled and fell back into his
chair sobbing bitterly, his face hidden
with his hands. “ That ought to fetch the
blankers,” he whispered to his chairman
as he staggered into his seat. And it did.
Bishop Meyers, of Melbourne (bishop of
Nat. Kinsman’s church—own make), has been lec-
turing on “Drunken Parsons I have Met.” He
tells of one minister who was so tight that he
went to sleep in the middle of his own sermon.
Bishop Meyers shouldn’t jump to conclusions.
We have often seen good teetotallers fall asleep in
the middle of a sermon—why should the parson
always escape the influence ?
By promptly transferring to the deceased’s
family the property which an enthusiastic Queens-
land devotee had willed to the Theosophical
Society, President Olcott puts up a recond. No
other religious body, as far as The Bulletin
knows, has ever disgorged a penny of any bequest
it could legally stick to—no matter how gross the
moral injustice involved. Christianity always
makes a bad break at the scent of hard cash.

Eastern newspapers, when commenting on
things Westralian, often refer to the “Six
Families.” Properly speaking there are seven—
viz., Lee Steere, Parker, Hammersly, Burgess,
Hassell, Leake and Burt.
Re Butler’s brain. Doesn’t this case come
within the common-law jurisdiction of the coro-
ner ? and can’t he order a post-mortem ? This is
certainly the case in Victoria, and the N.S.W.
provision for a certificate of the due carrying out
of the choking process hardly seems to be intended
as a substitute for, but as a complement to, the old
law. Both in N.S.W. and Vic., after an execu-
tion, the coroner holds an inquest, with a jury
and all the paraphernalia, in addition to a gazet-
ted statement of the fact of judicial hanging,
signed by all persons present.
Minister Ghould’s high-chested assurance
of clemency in the Thomas Suffield case is merely
what Chief Justice Griffith used to call “flap-
doodle.” Two years ago, and again before Reid
went to England, Suffield was informed that if he
petitioned for clemency the petition would receive
favorable consideration ; but this man demanded
release, not as a clemency, but as a right. It is
probable he will again refuse to petition according
to prison stereotype, unless, indeed, as a gaol-
warder whispered a few days ago, the gaol officials
make things so hot for him between now and
Christmas that a man will do anything to get out
of that Gehenna.
The admission of the budding poet, con-
cholouist, sociologist and ex-footballer, Collins,
to Vic. bar, subject to an easily-fulfilled condi-
tion about reciprocity, seems to remove, if the
case is correctly reported, the absolute bar which
has appeared to exist against the admission of
English and other barristers to Victoria. The
absence of articles in the English regulations tiaa
been hitherto thought an insuperable objectiqn.
But, judging from this case, all that is necessary
for an English, and presumably a N.S.W., barris-
ter to do, is to get admitted in Maoriland also,
when, without fulfilling articles, he can, on satis-
fying the Vic. court that he has got through
a course of study equivalent to the Vic. standard,
be admitted there without difficulty.
“ Henry Lawson has settled at Kaikouri
(M.L.) as schoolmaster at the Maori pah school.”
A REGRET.
The restless rover has left the track
Of “dust and flies” in the far “ out-back” ;
He has dumped his swag: by the Maori sea,
And we’re lonely here in the old Gum-tree.
For the green leaves quivered to hear his song
Of the right of Right and the wrong of Wrong,
And the dead leaves turned from their misery
To hear that hymn in the old Gum-tree.
Will he find new themes in the Maori land—
A league of sea for a league of land 1
Then his pent-up notes will be all too free,
Till he come back and sing in the old Gum-tree.

“ What Ails You ? ”
You are looking, perhaps feeling, badly. You
have something wrorg, but think it will soon pass
away ! That is one of the fatal delusions •such as
disease always weaves about its victims. If you
are wise you will send reinforcements to nature.
She can pull you through if you give her a little
timely aid. [V|
“DON’T KNOW WHAT TO DO!”
Let us tell you. If you have a weak, languid
feeling, periodical headaches, dizziness and vari-
able appetite, skin eruptions, bad taste in the
mouth on rising, disagreeable breath, yellow,
spotted, pimply skin, blood corruption, are easily
tired without known cause, have chills, fevers,
and more frequent “off days,” are restless at
night, troubled with dreams and startings in sleep,
are prematurely debilitated, are nervous, and ra
always in dread of some indefinable event I—*
which never happens, are irritable, peevish, easily
dispirited, have aching in the back and around
and above the hips, belching of wind, distress after
eating, distention of the bowels, which are irregu-
lar—mostly constipated,—if your whole system
lacks tone and vigor, then we knoiv what ails you 1
ARE YOU HELPLESS?
No; neither are you necessarily in a dangerous
condition yet, but you certainly will pr: be soon if
you don’t help nature. HOW ? L-i

POLITICAL
POINTS
About 40 per cent, of N.S.W. Assembly
now consists of miserable wretches whose fear of
divorce from their £3OO a year impels them to beg
from the Treasury blankets for the poor of their
electorates, and then to have the ghastly fact para-
graphed in all the daily papers. The Blanket-
party !
Labor-member Prendergast is shoving
through Vic. Assembly a useful little bill to fix
the fees chargeable in servants’ registry offices,
and to abolish all the swindling registry people by
compelling them to take out licenses, which can
be promptly cancelled in case of fraud or other
misconduct. As the measure won’t personally
damage Sargood or Cornelius Job Ham it has a
better show in the Council than most democratic
ideas.
A HARDENED OFFENDER.
O’HARA, J.P.: “ Ye say. Constable Raffardi, the prisoner was whistling
in a loud strain this morning. I can’t see any harem.”
CONSTABLE R. (in whisper) : “But, yer Washup, it was a sthrain of
the Wather.”
O’H.: “ What Wather?”
C. R.: “Boyne Wather.”
O.H.: “Dare, oh dare! It’s a good job for ye, M‘Mannis, it’s a free
and enlightened country. Ye’re let off with ten shillings
and costs.”
Queensland politicians, supposed to be
Able to “ read between the lines ” the meaning of
recent party movements, profess to see that the
peculiar personal appointments made to the
“ Royal Commissions ” on lands and mining have
temporarily averted the general political slump
that threatened to rearrange the various Parlia-
mentary sections.
The recent case of garnisheeing a tem-
porarily-crippled debtor’s pay in a Queensland
coastal town recalls that, in ’94, a bill to prevent
•workmen’s wages from being garnisheed was
passed by Q. Assembly, but blocked by the
Council. If the. Tozer crowd does not re-introduce
the measure some Labor-member should, at least,
table a motion about it.
H. : “ Bravo, Ileid ! He stands up to
Gosehen, gives it him square from the shoulder,
and knocks out a tooth or two. A delicious cold
douche. Here is Gosehen, in his gold-plastered
coat, expecting the usual oratoric suit of Dittoes,
in reply. His eyebrow's are pretty high, but they
must have been elevated almost to the roots of his
hair, as he peered over his glasses, at this
Audacity.”
N.S.W. Police Superannuation Fund has
gone broke. There was <£5300 left of the fund on
March 31, and on June 30 it was down to £2OOO.
The outgoings are about £24,000 a year, so unless
the State comes to the rescue with a large sum of
money, theold,broken-down “cop.”must needs end
his days in the Benevolent Asylum, or be run in
for vagrancy by his younger and more active
successor.
Constitutional history is sometimes made
whole pages at a time. But a few years ago
Parkes and Reid denounced the Acting-Premier-
ship of Dailey during the Stuart illness and of
Barton during Dibbs’s trip to “ Moscow ” as an
office unknown to the Constitution. And here,
to-day, every division of the Australias and every
other self-governing dependency of the empire
flourishes a full-blown, officially-gazetted and par-
liameu tardy-accepted Acting-Premier.
It appears that one of the N. S. W. Minis-
ters keeps up the dignity of his office by declining
to travel per penny-ferry, and uses a Govt, launch
(all to his gorgeous self) to take him to town, most
mornings.
Higgins’ Referendum Bill again intro-
duced—this time by Outtrim and Higgins, with
detail much more specific —into Vic. Parliament.
Its substance (containing a latent prophecy of its
chances of success) may be summed up thus:
Be it enacted by Her Gracious, by and with the
consent of the Leg. Council and Assembly of Vic-
toria, that said Council do forthwith commit
felo de se.
Melb. : “It is not hard to imagine how a
rumor arises that Turner and Reid have not been
quite ‘ hitting it ’in England. They do not show
together. Surely nobody can imagine London is
so dazzled as to be unable to bear the effulgence of
such a twin star in any one banquet. May be, it
is because Reid has so much the advantage in the
oratoric, but evidently Turner is reckoned the
bigger man For one thing, there is the polar
difference between Freetrade and Protection.”
Lord Mayor Ives, presiding at a Sydney
public meeting:—
The Australian colonies had reached their present stage
of development under the divine wisdom and guidance of
the Supreme Being, yet they were endeavoring to create
a nation without a recognition of that Supreme Being.
According to Ives’ Gospel, the Supreme Being
took matters very easily until Cook arrived, or,
rather, until Hargreaves discovered gold. But
hold, The Bulletin has it! God and J. Bull
arrived here together. In a word, Bull is God.
Keir Hardie, who
went with a red Republi-
can cap on his head to
take his seat in the Com-
mons years ago, continues
to show reason why the
“great newspapers ” held
him in awful contempt.
The other week he had
the bad taste to tell an
ignorant audience that
any monarch is neces-
sarily in co. with the
aristocrats and title-pur-
chasing classes. “ The
Crown and the coronet,”
said Keir Hardie, “ are
so inseparable that any
interference with the one
must weaken the position
of the other. The heads
of the enemies of the
people are all fixed on
one neck, which will
make a saving of labor
when the chopping pro-
cess sets in.” In other
words, destroy the Crown
symbol, then you abolish
the accursed aristocratic
and plutocratic ambition
to bask in its reflected
glory and bring the
hupper classes more into
sympathy with the lowah
ordahs. Suppress the
monarchy, and the foun-
tain of titular distinc-
tion, for one thing, is
turned off at the main
tap.
And has it come to this—that on one day of
the week, in Sydney, an honest man with sixpence
in his pocket may not go into a shop and buy a
bottle of milk and a few cakes for his wife and
child w r ho are sitting on the beach?
Labor - party prospects seem hopeful in
Collingwood, Vic. Several men spent jubilee
week distributing bills for a meeting the following
week, one man alone having posted up 300. Re-
sult, an attendance of seven, including two avowed
opponents.
IRREPRESSIBLE!
The English Court of Appeal has decided that
cash-betting on racecourses is legal.
Two more, making four in all, of Victoria’s
little cockspur railways—the scrap of line from
H-orsham to Norad juba and the Ilpmile branch
from Mount Moriac to Wensleydale—are to be
closed in August. Gillies’s policy of giving every
member about miles of line in his electorate to
keep him quiet caused the country to be littered
over with these odd twopenn’orths of useless rail-
way. Now that Duncan is posing in view of the
election as the “ one wise head ” of the Vic. Tory
party, the closing of four of his most insane rail-
ways is a very inopportune advertisement.
Wensleydale, by the way, has a population of 18
souls—yet Duncan thought even its support worth
buying with a railway.

A Northern pastoralist on his grievances :
“ Bill to adjust matters from the Queen-street
gang ! You will get a bill to adjust losses due to
the tick plague when the tick reaches Brisbane,
and not a day earlier ! ” Savage, but true.
A very reputable M.P., who has' until
lately, been a high Rechabite official, startled
the House the other morning by turning up in
an aggressive state of drunk to take part in
debate. Usually a silent member, he talked on
the occasion for an hour and a half; but the Han-
sard reporters were merciful to him.
An Australian visitor to Japan can only
buy goods there per medium of a Jap agent.
Also, white men are not permitted, except in
treaty ports, to hold land in Japan. Why, then,
should Australia hesitate about restricting Jap
privileges here, and prohibiting the little brown
man from making his direful scoop?
Glassey’s Queensland. One-man-one-vote
Bill, after Tozer had vigorously rallied up
the Government forces against it, was only de-
feated on the second reading by 35 votes against
29. Compared to the majorities which used to
back the old Griffilwraith Government this is very
weak.
Premier Reid’s straightest and boldest
statement up to date was his intimation to John
Bull in general that Australia had no intention of
giving up its semi-independence in order to be a
hopeless, helpless minority in an Imperial Federal
Parliament, and a mere joint in the tail of the
empire. By comparison with this honest and
courageous utterance it was a painful thing to
find Forrest, Braddon, and Seddon —the once
democratic Richard of Kumara —all doing a mild
crawl in favor of Imperial Federation and the
“ tightening of the silken bonds,” and the
other stereotyped bosh.
The person who doesn’t know when
to burn incriminating documents has a lot
to answer for. A few months ago a lady
left her traveling-bag in a railway-carriage.
It went to the lost-luggage office, and was
sold by auction in the usual course, another
lady buying it for a song. Among the con-
tents were a lot of letters, the writer of
which mentioned that he enclosed certain
drugs for an unlawful purpose, as instructed
by a person whose name was given in full.
Naturally the buyer read these horrifying
disclosures to her friends at afternoon-tea,
and the contents filtered through many
shocked females till the social purity society
Eassed them on to the police. And now a
ighly-respected citizen is in very deep
water indeed.
Britishah . “I have just written Home to
my honored mother the glorious news that there is
not a single Republican in Australia except about
three Irish, more or less.”
The man chosen to saw off the “one
wise head ” of the Conservative party in Victoria
(Duncan Gillies) at the Toorak election is W. H.
Lewis, whom the Age seemingly has adopted as
its Liberal candidate and supporter of the Turner
Government. Lewis is an enlightened critic of
insolvency law in the southern province.
The general feeling up North is that
Queensland isn’t coining in to the Federation
movement. The Toztr crowd still insists on the
nomination of Convention delegates by Parlia-
ment ; the Opposition demands election by the
people ; and the general feeling is that the TLozei
Ministry hates election by the people a great deal
more than it loves Federation. The debate practi-
cally means that the Govt, party is talking against
fate in the hope that two or three Oppositionists
may drop down dead or something. There is an
anxious counting of heads now and then, and, as
the numbers are against the Government, the
House adjourns without a division. As a result,
the Federation Bill has hardly got started yet.

Chairman of Committees M‘Court is the
only M.L.A. in N.S.W. history elected on a writ

Convincing argument from the Austra-
lasian :
Why on earth should Victoria want to invest capital in
N.S.W., and then follow after it in thousands, if all that
is claimed for Protection be true? A colony that is
alleged to be only creeping along under Freetrade ought
to be no place for the investment of Victorian money.
Quite so. Or, to put it another way, why should
British people want to invest millions of capital
in all manner of protected countries if all that is
claimed for Freetrade be true? It is a poor
argument which won’t bear being inspected on
both sides.
The solidarity of the Queensland Labor-
party was strikingly exemplified, the other day,
when that same party, during discussion on
Glassey’s amendment to Tozer’s Federal pro-
posals, missed defeating the Government simply
because four of their number (the leader among
them) voted with the Ministry for an adjournment
of debate. A more humiliating position than
Glassey’s cannot well be conceived. Nominally
leader, he is usually accompanied, on divisions, by
a third or a fourth of his party, to the confusion of
Labor and the huge delight of the local Fats.

At Collingwood (Melb.) police-court last
week, the evil effect of statuary in public gardens
was brought under the Bench’s notice by Johanna
Mussaline, spinster. Johanna, who happened to
be present on a charge of uttering obscene lan-
guage, stripped herself suddenly of her various
habiliments, and besought the “beaks ” to testify
that her figure was at least lovelier than the
common or garden kind of plaster Venus. “I’m
no artist’s dream, or sculptor’s study, but a live
woman ; and, if you’re judges, say so,” shrieked the
nude Johanna. It was the cry of a woman whose
modesty had been undermined, whilst her spirit
of emulation was excited, by the weather-beaten
visions that flaunt their chipped outlines anigh
the benches in the park. The cry rang with
artistic feeling, and created an awful sensation in
court. “ The magistrates stared in blank sur-
prise ” —having lost for the moment the power to
blush and turn away their heads; whilst the
living Venus was hustled away (with great diffi-
culty) by three policemen. Then the Bench in-
flicted a sentence of three months’ imprisonment
on account of the obscene language, but preserved
a strict silence as to whether Johanna had proved
her case against the effigies mit nodings on.
Probably the fact that she didn’t get more than
three months was expected to speak for itself.
Both in Sydney and Melbourne on J übilee
night it was noticed that where “ God Save the
Queen’’was outlined in flaring gas-jets “God”
kept going out most frequently, and “Queen”
was the next hardest to keep alight. The two
unimportant words in the middle gave no trouble.
The ten S.A. delegates to the Federal
Convention —six out of the ten oppose payment of
members !—are still drawing their two guineas a
day, including Sundays, and will continue doing
so till after the next Convention meeting is over.
This means that Kingston is Jubilating in Eng-
land at £l4 14s. per week and his Ministerial
salary in addition.
Majah Lassettah said “the volunteers in
the colonies looked upon England as their home,
and gloried in what had been called her ‘ isola-
tion.’ ” The first statement may pass as the mere
post-prandial prancing of a tin warrior—but the
second is unutterable fool-talk for a tradesman.
The ‘ ‘ isolation ” of England spells ultimate ruin
to her hucksters.
The beautifully abject Jubilee address of
Maoriland Parliament —the thing with the 26 or
189 “Your Majesty’s” in it—came to a bad end.
A gorgeous copy was prepared and Premier Sed-
don was to present it personally while he wiped
up the floor of Windsor Castle with his stomach.
But to his horror it wasn’t among his baggage,
nor did it arrive by- post, and after much indig-
nant cablegramming it was found on a carrier’s
premises in Wellington detained for non-payment
of Is. 6d. cartage. What a fall is there ! Now
the illuminated grovel will arrive six weeks late,
and Agent-General Reeves will take it round to
the kitchen entrance, and for all the glory there
will be it might almost as well be an address to
G, Washington.

A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of 660 ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic: 1, New Books; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery; 4, Music ; 5, Fancy
Goods ; 6, Toys ; 7, Perfumery ; 8, Pictures ; 0,
Artists’ Materials ; 10, an Ornamental Exhibition ;
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland ;
13, a Smiling Gallery ; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department,“containing half-a-million books. The
main walks of the establishment are half-a-mile
long. *
Dear Bulletin, —You asked who was going
to pay for the “ grand illuminated arch ” in Bris
bane (Q.), near the main approach to Excellency
Lamington’s fireworks show. Well, it was made
public that it was the Governor’s gas-bill ; but,

Good for Producers.—Competition, when
not overdone, is bracing, and it is pleasant to ob-
serve those veteran stock-salesmen, Pitt, Son &
Badgery, Ltd., making good headway in the wool
and produce selling, which they took up a while
back. No doubt stock and wool selling have the
same essentials for success, hence the productive
results. #
Mr. H. E. Stoye, who until recently held
an important position in the Hotel Australia, has
taken over the management of the Grand Central
Coffee Palace, Clarence - street, Sydney, and,
being a man of many years’ experience in the
hotel business, he is confident that he will make
the Grand Central a place of entertainment second
to none in the seven provinces. The culinary
department will be under his own direct super-
vision, which should be a guarantee that even the
most fastidious student of the art of dining will
have no cause of complaint. The above expression
is used advisedly. Dining was meant to be an
art, though too often it is a mere casting of under-
done mutton into the open countenance.*

Bulletin certainly mentioned Hayseed among its five

Hayseed’s achievement ranks high among G.N
Steeplechase records. His weight, 10 stone, was quite
enough for a 4-year-old to carry, the course was pretty
sticky, and the time was nearly 4sec. faster than the pre-
vious record When Fearless 11., in 1890, won the same
double-event, Edges gelding carried five pounds less
and took 20 seconds longer in doing the long journey.
Out of 17 G.N. Steeple winners only the first
one Sussex, was a stallion. The mare Ruby scored in
1880, and in fifteen other cases the prize has gone to
geldings. Racing over sticks certainly can’t be advo-
cated on the ground that it tends to improve the breed of
horses. The successful lepper is almost invariably an un-
sexed animal.
The Open Hurdle Race, at Flemington, last
Saturday, strengthened the prejudices of those who
doant like to see money joomping oop in the air.”
Soult and So-and-So, on the strength of their performances
on July 3rd, were reckoned safe conveyances this time
Backed at fours and fives to one, they were the only com'-
petitors that fell. J
Doctor put up a record, last Saturday, by going
five miles over the big sticks. He did the Grand Nat
distance without making the slightest mistake, and 75
minutes later, was brought out again for the Open Steeple-
chase. Although a bit stiff at the commencement of his
second journey, Doctor jumped all right, and struggled
into third place at the finish. What Harry Rickards
would call “ a dear, kind Doctor.”
Patriot’s victory in the six furlongs Lawn Han-
dicap. at Flemington, seems to give him a chance of
taking classic honors next season—which, by the way
starts next month. At any rate, he gave a lot of weight
to other youngsters in the race, of whom Eugene, carry-
ing 221 b. less than Patriot, was backed freely. ’Among
the unbacked ones in this flutter good judges took note
of Bayonet, a 3-.vear-old, by Carbine, that may win some-
thing hereafter.
* * * *
Mark Whitty, father of the lad who steered
Buzzi through the G.N. Hurdles, is a brother-in-law of
the owner, J. Farrell, and in ’96, when the horse got
home, his residence at Flemington was thrown open and
all-comers were treated ad lib This year no such’pre-
parations were made, the stable not reckoning that the
horse had a big chance, but nevertheless the same old
crowd, augmented with all the “beats” of the course
who remembered the story of last year’s luck, rolled up
rushed Whitty’s house, and “scoffed” everything drinkl
able and eatable under the roof.

Had the members of the English Jockey Club
seen Mr. Hagerty jump his nineteen horses off in the
Welter Handicap, at Moorefield, last Saturday, level as a
die, the use of Gray’s machine at ’Ome would be assured.
♦ * * *
Larrikin’s untimely end will, no doubt, warn
owners of top-notch steeplechasers not to risk their
animals over the smaller obstacles. It was assuredly an
error of judgment to start hurdle-racing with a horse
like Larrikin, whose people considered him, even with
the maximum weight up, as holding a mortgage for some
time to come upon cross-country events.

If Merman, after wintering well, couldn’t run
forward in a handicap at Newmarket, with only 7st. 51b.
on his crack Australian back, what was the good of send-
ing Newhaven to Hingland?
* * * *
One of these days it may occur to an Australian
turfman to import English thoroughbreds from France,
where the stock is reared under somewhat Australian
climatic conditions. The French edition of the thorough-
bred is apt to be bigger-boned and hardier than the
English original, and should be better suited to the com-
paratively rough career of a racehorse in this country.
Considering that market prices run lower in France than
Britain, the idea seems worth an experiment.
• « • •
The owners of a horse sued a Tas. country race
club, the other day, for wrongful and malicious disquali-
fication. Damages claimed, £250. It transpired in evi-
dence that the horse was protested against by the rider
of the second horse for having run inside a post. The
stewards, at least one of whom had backed the winner,
dismissed the protest. Subsequently, a week or so after
the meeting, they met and disqualified the horse for a
year and the jockey for two years. Without enquiry, the
T.R.C. endorsed the disqualification, which, of course,
blocks the horse throughout Australia. In the opinion of
Judge and jury plaintiffs failed to prove malice on the
part of the club, though ignorance of racing rules and
carelessness stuck out far enough for a Judge to han<>- a
caustic comment upon. Verdict for defendant club, with
a recommendation from the bench that the disqualifica-
tion be removed. The paradoxes of the law 1

_W. R- Wilson is negotiating for the purchase
of Torrens Park, the estate of R. Barr-Smith, the S.A.
millionaire, about three miles from Adelaide. Years ago,
when Wilson first established his racing-stud, he tried to
buy the same place. He intends, they 7 say, to give up
horse-racing altogether—which will be a pity, for the turf
has had few such clean sportsmen.

As Goldspur and one or two other cracks are
likely to visit Australia, it is as well to remember that
Maoriland horses, after coming across, have been of two
kinds. The Carbines, Trentons, Nordenfeldts and Martini-
ilen rys have upheld their reputation, and proved better
than Australia’s best. On the other hand, the more
numerous class, such as Tirailleur, Cuirassier, Medallion,
Man ton, St. Hippo, &c., &c., through climatic or other
reasons, never came near their island form and became
veritable milch cows for Ikey Mo.

* * * *
~ defeat of the M.L. footballers by 22—8 in
trie return match with Sydney was due partly to the
nome team s improvement in personnel, partly to its
scrummers playing in shin-pads, partly to its making the
game fast and open, partly to its surprising unselfishness
and combination. M.L. had done considerable railway
travelling, had played at Bathurst and Orange, and had
eaten, drunk, and knocked about considerably during the
week, ouch things, however, do not condone M.L.’s de-
feat, since to be a footballer is to plav hard, drink hard,
eat hard, sleep hard. When the English football team,
for instance, was in Aus. its drunks and disorderlies used
to be stirred up by the policemen in the morning from
door-steps, bridges, footpaths, etc., etc., used to have a
bath, a rundown and feed—and then file out to the battle
and win. The yarn that the Maorilanders weren’t on the
job is very improbable, however. The only evidence in
its favor is that the game was not rough : for, although a
couple of men got kicked on the head, the only consider-
able stoppage was for “ ripped pants.”

,A far-seeing Melb. business man started to run a
cycle-insurance agency a while back, accepting 10s. a year
to cover a machine to £3O. By the time he had raked in
half-a-dozen premiums, a big smash occurred through two
riders meeting face to face in the dark. Both bikes were
reduced to old metal, and, by an extraordinary coinci-
dence, both nders were clients of the underwriter '

slump on record just now, and several agencies for in-
ferior makes will snuff-out altogether before winter is
over, bpring is expected to revive the trade, but people
are beginning to know a cycle from a perambulator, and
only first-grade wheels will stand a chance next season

Dear Bulletin, —Just a word in reply to vour
correspondent as to weights of rowing men. A man isn’t
good or bad simply because he is either big or small
There are good Ing men and good little ditto, and also
darned bad uns of either sort. Has “ M‘G.” ever heard
the rowing expression “ Pull your weight» ? This sums
the whole thing up in a nutshell. Whatever a man’s
weight, he must pull it and a bit over—viz., his share of
boa * a ?d c ox. Now, as by the laws of proportion a
12st. man is to a 9st. man as 4 to 3, he is consequently
likely to pull more overweight in that proportion. As for
University crews, you simply can’t take them seriously
They are composed principally of youths who come down
to the sheds for about three weeks previous to a race and
not at all afterwards. “ M‘G.” talks of list, as a light
weight. It takes a fair-sized man to scale list, trained
and in rowing costume. If “ M‘G.” is going to barrack
for little men, let him do so by all means, but don’t let
him include list, stripped in the category.—W.

Fitzsimmons cannot have been over-confident

ii, nl!ih b U iv-lt P r‘' IZ l a r. d ■ the for a certain surti

Amusing to read that anybody exhibiting bogus
cinematograph pictures of the Fitzsimmons-Corbett fight
wul be prosecuted by the holders of the copyrighted
films. It is quite certain that no reproduction of a brutal
spectacle ought to be permitted by the police. Fitz-
simmons and Corbett couldn't get permission to fight in
an Australian theatre. Why sanction a demoralising set-
to between their photographs ?
* * * •
. Looking at photographs of Corbett and Fitz-
simmons in the buff, above the waist Corbett shows
poorly as compared with the muscular blacksmith,
ritz., square built to a fault, and looking hard as nails,
makes Corbett appear soft and washy—in fact, the latter
reminds one very much of poor old Mick Dooley. A week
before the fight Fitzsimmons’ chest-measurement was
42in. in repose, and 46J expanded, all bone, muscle and
smew. Corbett, round and soft-looking as a girl,
measured 39in., with an expansion of 43in. The pair
mutely showed their respective youthful implements of
training—the sledgehammer and the pen.

drawback. Then both lines are not nearly so remunera-

have drawn as a pugilist, as his victim (Choynski)did, but

* * * *
League of Yic. Wheelmen, as reconstructed,
should prove a less flabby power than before. A new
racing rule provides that “competitors in handicap
events shall not start from a mark distant from the pole
or inner circle of the track, except in cases where a
number of competitors are allotted the same mark”
Hitherto a competitor has been at liberty to start from
any part of the track he pleased, and the result was to
give a great advantage to men who were able to go from
off the high banking and thus get “ wav ” on from their
run down the incline. Good judges consider the banking
start worth 15yds. in the mile.

THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to tneir advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*
That Young Australia will have the good
soft-drink is evidenced by the surprising trade
now done this winter weather at Washington H.
Soul’s counter. As many as 30 people—ladies, bar-
risters, solicitors, business men—can be seen at
the one moment waiting for a pull from the big
fountains.*

“The Retrospect,” issued by D. Jones &
Co., is an interesting account of an interesting
firm, showing how the business began in ’37 with
a shop no larger than a dining-room, and grew
and grew until, to-day, it occupies three acres,
and is one of the business sights of Sydney.
“The Retrospect” shows the past 60 years’
changes in dress and fashion, by good illustration
and description, while the pithy memoirs concern-
ing the evolution of Sydney and N.S.W. institu-
tions and other public matters, make “ The
Retrospect ” of interest to all classes of the com-
munity. *
A Boon to Sufferers.— A well-known stock-
dealer and storekeeper announces that, if written
to confidentially, he will reply and state the plan
he pursued by which he was restored to health
and vigor after years of suffering from Nervous
and Physical Weakness, Loss of Vigor, etc. He
writes : “ I have no scheme to extort money from
anyone whomsoever. I was victimised by quacks
and empirics until I lost faith in mankind ; but,
thank heaven, I am now well, vigorous, and
strong, and anxious to make this certain means of
cure known to all. Having nothing to sell, I
want no money.” Address—J. KILIAN, Store-
keeper, Daveyston, S.A.*

TERRIBLE CASE OF
ECZEMA
CORED BY
Ayer’s Sarsaparilla
Mrs. E. Wyatt, of Port Road, West
Hindmarsh, So. Australia, writes of
the sad condition of her little daugh-
ter, whose portrait she also sends:
“My daughter was afflicted with
Eczema of the most aggravated
type. The disease first appeared in
eruptions on her head, then her hair
began to fall out, and in spite of
the best medical advice and treat-
ment she grew steadily worse. The
sores were full of matter and were
extremely offensive. Her eyes be-
came affected, and she was, in truth,
in a terrible state. My neighbors
were very sympathetic and took
great interest in the case. They
persuaded me to try Ayer’s Sarsa-
parilla, and I am most thankful to
be able to say that this wonderful
medicine completely restored my
daughter’s health. She has now as
good a head of hair as anyone could
wish, her eyes are perfectly well,
and she is a fine girl of eight years
with every prospect of growing up
to be a strong and healthy woman/’

The young men who stroll around in long
hair and soft felt hats talking largely about their
intention to lead a purely literary life, should
seriously regard James Hill, a frowsy, shirtless
person who received a year “under the vag.” from
Melb. City Court the other day. Hill described
himself in the watchhouse book as a ‘‘ man of
letters ” and, when brought before the court, he
offered to write the bench a “ good book ” if they
would give him time. But James had been living
in a nua-mia on Yarra banks, and he had been
lightingfires in dangerous proximity to T. Brassey’s
back-yard, so the bench disregarded his offer and
gave him 12 months hard. So much for the out-
look of literature in Australia.
Sydney land-tax officials valued the big
city estate of John Cooper at .£495,000, and de-
manded £2OOO a year tax ; and John Cooper
values it at £95,000, and offers about £4OO. The
difference being irreconcilable, a joyous fight is in
progress. Cooper claims, among other things,
that the 1896 tax is wholly illegal because the
assessment was issued before the assessment-books
were completed, or the court of review appointed,
or various other necessary things done. It will
be a big case, and it is reckoned the court will be
covered with the hair of both parties before it is
settled. Cooper, by the way, complains that the
tax demanded is half the income of the estate,
which is quite probable, and also not at all unde-
sirable. When a large portion of an estate is kept
lying idle and yields nothing, because the owner
is waiting for the unearned increment to come
along, then a land-tax is apt to produce surprising
results like this.

Kangaroo tendons are in huge demand for

only one death and but three relapses.

Are Women Degenerating ?
„ .. Sydney, June 13, 1897.
To the Editor,
Sir, In these days of ever-increasing competi-
tion, when the struggle for existence has become
more intense and earnest than it has ever been
before in the world’s history, the question as to
whether women should enter into the pursuits,
and assume the vocations, hitherto regarded as
exclusively manly, is one of the utmost importance
to both sexes. It is not my intention in this short
letter, however, to go into this question again, for
it has already been discussed in all its aspects, and
with great, freedom, by many of the greatest
thinkers of the day. That women do compete,
and successfully too, with men is a fact most em-
phatically proved. But the question which is of
the greatest importance to us at the present
moment is : Are women physically capable of
sustaining the great efforts now required of them
both in their home life with its worries and cares,
or in the outside world of business and pleasure ?
Alas ! we have not to seek for the answer. It is
written too plainly in the faces and forms of the
women and young girls we meet every day in our
streets. Pale faces which should bear the bloom
of health, and dull, expressionless eyes, which
should beam with life and hope, tell their own
tale; and we know our women are paying the
penalties due to diseased constitutions, either in-
herited or resultant from the artificial modes of
living and the unhealthy foods of the day. The
climate of this country, too, with its sudden
changes from heat to cold, its dust storms, and its
enervating winds, is in itself a great strain on the
female constitution. It is, then, an undoubted
fact that the women of the day are physically
degenerating, and, therefore, the interesting par-
ticulars contained in the following letter, received
recently from Miss Adeline Bell, of Napier, prov-
ing as they do the marvellous curative powers of
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills in all ailments of the
female sex, should prove interesting to all readers.
This lady gives the following graphic description '
of her ailments. She says: “I was troubled
with cold hands and feet, palpitation of the heart,
constant feelings of weariness, headaches, loss of
appetite, lack of energy, and also swollen feet and
legs. The symptoms first appeared about three
years ago. Two doctors were consulted about my
case, and medicines were used, but without avail.
I now wish to thank you for the benefit I have
derived from your highly-valued Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills. They have done me every good, and
I am now quite another person in health. I can
recommend your pills to anybody. ” In conclusion
it may be added that this wonderful medicine has
cured numbers of people, not only in Australia,
but all over the world, of Rheumatism, Sciatica,
Lumbago, Dyspepsia, Indigestion, and other ail-
ments.—I am, Sir, Yours, etc.,
“PRO BONO PUBLICO.”

Mre. LILLIS (late of Bundaberg).
Naturalist A. J. Campbell avers that that
fine bird, the Cape Barren goose, has but a few
more years to exist before it follows the moa, the
great auk, and the dodo to the spirit-land.
George Weatinghouse tried to sell his patent
railway brake for .£6OO, but could not find a pur-
chaser. In the subsequent ten years it brought
him in three millions of money. There are many
other things still to be invented just as valuable as
a railway brake. But many inventors have lost
their patents through defective specifications,
claims, etc. This risk can be avoided by inventors
consulting Mr. Arthur Griffith (M.L.A), Patent
Agent, Sydney, who will not only do the work with
exactitude, but at rates within the reach of every
inventor. For instance, £2O will secure pro-
visional Protection in the whole of the seven
colonies of Australia and Great Britain, ana
Foreign Patents can be obtained through him at
similarly reasonable rates.*
The little Champion, which was not the
worst of Melb. weeklies, has merged its struggling
identity into the Sun, devoted to society
and the household,” a chronicle of small tea "
parties and other social engagements. The Cham-
pion had one hopeful moment in its career, and
the sanguine radical whose name was writ large
on the wrapper decided to celebrate the gleam or
sunshine with a little dinner to the staff. 0,. what
a night—what a night! as Paulton remarks in the
new farce. Warmed by the juice of local grapes,
boss Champion painted a glorious political future
for the company at large. His election to Parlia-
ment and prompt capture of the Premiership were
set down as sure things. Then the staff were to
waltz into the Legislature, one at a time,
or simultaneously if possible, and get pro-
moted to seats in the Ministry. Ihe
Champion would direct popular opinion and
its editor would rule the House assisted by all the
admiring intellects present on this historical
occasion. Even the canvasser was to have a
Cabinet position—without portfolio, perhaps, but
a position all the same. The staff sat revelling m
a vision of knighthoods whilst they harked to the
chairman’s oratory, sweet as the murmur of low
fountains that gush forth in the midst of roses,
and lofty as the Blue Alsatian Mountains of am-
bition. Led by such a champion they never
dreamed of leaving their bones to bleach in the
Sun.' The world was their oyster, and they had
swallowed it from the half-shell. And now—well,
what there is left of the Champion records that
Miss Smith “ looked nice in pink.” It is melan-
choly to remember that this, is the man who
waged war so long, and (all things considered) so
well, against the boodlers of society. He is now
chronicling the fact that Mrs. Secret Composition
held a delightful reception on Saturday—at which
painful persons wore smart gowns bought on
credit Yet that first issue of the Champion
shadowed H.H.C’s destination. It was a demo-
cratic paper, and it had a block of the newest
spring shape hat ” and of a “ becoming gown.
Also, there was a picture of the Beaconsheld
statue in Westminster, and of the Salisbury medal-
lion H. H. Champion did a plucky thing at the
end of ’95, when he cynically published, vn his
Christmas number, a list of the boom secret com-
positions, which list caused more heart-burning
down South than any other article ever published
in the city of smells —or, perhaps, in Australasia.
Champion was born in India 40 years ago to swell-
society parents. He was reared in England in the
usual style of a “gentleman’s son. 1 hen he
went into the army—but threw up his commission
when ordered into active service against the
Mahdi, as he regarded that noisy person m the
light of a patriot. Possessing keen literary tastes
and no small amount of ready ability he took
up journalism, and soon was sub-editor of the
Nineteenth Centuby, to the disgust of struggling
professional journalists without. his luck. He
spoke at Trafalgar Square in ’B6, in the month of
the riots, and had to stand in the dock with John
Burns and Hyndman, on a charge of sedition.
He conducted his own defence, earning the praise
of the Judge, and called the magnificent Joseph
Chamberlain as a witness. Chamberlain was
made to prove speeches delivered against
the Government, which were more “seditious”
than Champion’s own—inference: the Govern-
ment was afraid to tackle Chamberlain. The
seditious persons were found not guilty. Then
Champion took a prominent part in the big Hock
strike, and became known to Australia through
the cablegrams. He came to Australia while the
’9l maritime strike was in course, and he got
much censure from the Trades Hall for alleged
actions then. Champion’s old habit of thinking
with the Liberals and dining with the Tories
stood him in bad stead in Australia, though it was
his right bower in England. When he again
returned to Victoria he joined the Age staff, but
had a quarrel with David Syme. That is why he
started the Champion, with an open letter to
King David as the main feature. In May last
year Champion stood for South Melbourne against
Mauger and Ben Tucker. Champion nearly lost
his £5O deposit. Since then his paper has ap-
peared in several shapes, with intermittent spasms
of boldness, and has now disappeared without a
gurgle. The late H. H. Champion now edits a
paper for the households of Society. Lists of wed-
ding-presents a specialty.

Dave’s Snakebite.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
One hot day, as we were finishing dinner*
a sheriff’s bailiff rode up to the door. Dad left the
table, munching some bread, and went out to him.
Mother looked out of the door; Sal went to the
window ; little Bill and Tom peeped through a
crack ; Dave remained at his dinner; and Joe
knavishly seized the opportunity of exploring the
table for leavings, finally seating himself in Dad’s
place, and commencing where Dad had left off.
“Jury-summons,” said the meek bailiff, extract-
ing a paper from his breast-pocket, and reading
“Murtagh Colin Joseph Duncan M‘Gregor Ross,
selector, Shingle Hut . . . Correct ? ”
Dad nodded assent.
“ Got any water ? ”
There wasn’t a drop in the cask, so Dad came in
and asked Mother if there was any tea left. She
pulled a long, solemn, Sunday-school face, and
looked at Joe, who was holding the teapot upside-
down, shaking the tea-leaves into his cup.
“ Tea, Dad ? ” he chuckled—“ by golly ! ”
Dad didn’t think it worth while going out to the
bailiff again. He sent Joe.
“ Not any at all ? ”
“ Nothink,” said Joe.
“ H’m! Nulla bona, eh ? ” And the Law
smiled at its own joke and went off thirsty.
Thus it was that Dad came to be away one day
when his great presence of mind and ability as a
bush doctor was most required at Shingle Hut.
* • * •
Dave took Dad’s place at the plough. One
of the horses—a colt that Dad bought with the
money he got from Red Donovan for Farmer —
had only just been broken. He was bad at start-
ing. When touched with the rein he would stand
and wait until the old furrow-horse put in a few
steps ; then plunge to get ahead of him, and if a
chain or a swingle-tree or something else didn’t
break, and Dave kept the plough in, he ripped
and tore along in style, bearing in and . bear-
ing out and knocking the old horse about till
that much-enduring animal became as cranky
as himself, and the pace terrible. Down would
go the plough-handles, and, with one tremend-
ous pull on the reins, Dave would haul
them back on to. their rumps. Then he would
rush up and kick the colt on the root of the tail;
and if that didn’t make him put his leg over the
chains and kick till he ran a hook into his heel
and lamed himself, or broke something, it caused
him to rear up and fall back on the plough and
snort and strain and struggle till there was not a
stitch left on him but the winkers.
Now, if Dave was noted for one thing more
than another it was for his silence. He scarcely
ever took the trouble to speak. He hated to be
asked a question, and mostly answered by nodding
his head. Yet, though he never seemed to prac-
tise, he could, when his blood was fairly up, swear
with distinction and effect. On this occasion he
swore through the whole afternoon without once
making a mistake.
Towards evening Joe took the reins and began
to drive. He hadn’t gone once around when,
just as the horses approached a big dead tree that
had been left standing in the cultivation, he
planted his left foot heavily upon a Bathurst-burr
that had been cut and left lying. It clung to him.
He hopped along on one leg, trying to kick it off ;
still it clung to him. He fell down. The horses
and the tree got mixed up, and everything was
copfusion. Dave abused Joe remorselessly.
“ Go on ! ” he howled, waving in the air a fist-
ful of grass and weeds which he had pulled from
the nose of the plough ; “ clear out of this alto-
gether ! —you’re only a damn nuisance.”
Joe’s eyes rested on the fistful of grass. They
lit up suddenly.
“ L-l-look out, Dave,” he stuttered ; “ y’-y’ got a
s-s-snake. ”
Dave dropped the grass promptly. A deaf-adder
crawled out of it. Joe killed it. Dave looked
closely at his hand, which was all scratches and
scars. He looked at it again ; then he sat on the
beam of the plough, pale and miserable-looking.
“ D-d-did it bite y’, Dave ? ” No answer.
Joe saw a chance to distinguish himself, and
took it. He ran home, glad to be the bearer of
the news, and told Mother that “ Dave’s got bit
by a adder—a sudden-death adder —right on top
o’ th’ finger.”
How mother screamed ! “My God ! whatever
shall we do? Run quick,” she said, “and bring
Mr. Maloney. Dear, oh dear !oh dear ! ”
Joe had not calculated on this injunction. .He
dropped his head and said sullenly:
“ Wot, walk all the way over there ? ”
Before he could say another word a tin-dish left
a dinge on the back of his skull that will accom-
pany him to his grave if he lives to be a thousand.
“ You wretch, you ! Why don’t you run when I
tell you ? ”
Joe sprang in the air like a shot wallaby.
“ I’ll not go at all now—y’ see ! ” he answered,
starting to cry. Then Sal put on her hat and ran
for Maloney.
Meanwhile Dave took the horses out, walked
inside and threw himself on the sofa without
uttering a word. He felt ill.
Mother was in a paroxysm of fright. She
threw her arms about frantically and cried for
someone to come. At last she sat down and tried
to think what she could do. She thought of the
very thing, and ran for the carving-knife, which
she handed to Dave with shut eyes. He motioned
her with a disdainful movement of the elbow to
take it away.
Would Maloney ever come ? He was coming,
hat in hand, and running for dear life across the
Eotato-paddock. Behind him was his man. Be-
ind his man—Sal, out of breath. Behind her,
Mrs. Maloney and the children.
“ Phwat’B th’ thrubble ? ” cried Maloney. “ Bit
be a dif-adher ? O, be the tares of war ! ” Then
he asked Dave numerous questions as to how it
happened, which Joe answered with promptitude
and pride. Dave simply shrugged his shoulders
and turned his face to the wall. Nothing was to
be got out of him.
Maloney held a short consultation with himself.
Then—“ Hould up yer hand ! ” he said, bending
over Dave with a knife. Dave thrust out his arm
violently, knocked the instrument to the other
side of the room, and kicked wickedly.
“The pison’s wurrkin’,” whispered Maloney
quite loud.
“ Oh, my gracious ! ” groaned Mother.
“ The poor crathur,” said Mrs. Maloney.
There was a pause.
“Phwhat finger’s bit?” asked Maloney. Joe
thought it was the littlest one of the lot.
He. approached the sofa again, knife in hand.
“Show me yer finger,” he said to Dave.
For the first time Dave spoke. He said :
“Damn y’—what the devil do y’ want? Clear
out and lea’ me ’lone. ”
Maloney hesitated. There was a long silence.
Dave commenced breathing heavily.
“It’s maikin’ ’m slape,” whispered Maloney*
glancing over his shoulder at the women.
“ Don’t let him ! Don’t let him ! ” Mother
wailed.
“ Salvation to’s all,” muttered Mrs. Maloney,
piously crossing herself.
Maloney put away the knife and beckoned to
his man, who was looking on from the door. They
both took a firm hold of Dave and stood him
upon his feet. He looked hard and contemptu-
ously at Maloney for some seconds. Then with
gravity and deliberation Dave said: “Now wot
’n th’ devil are y’ up t’ ? Are y’ mad ? ”
“ Walk’m along, Jaimes —walk ’in along,” was
all Maloney had to say. And out into the yard
they marched him. How Dave did struggle to
get away ! Swearing and cursing Maloney for a
cranky Irishman till he foamed at the mouth, all
of which the other put down to snake-poison.
Round and round the yard and up and down it
they trotted him till long after dark, until there
wasn’t a struggle left in him.
They placed him on the sofa again, Maloney
keeping him awake with a strap. How Dave
ground his teeth and kicked and swore whenever
he felt that strap ! And they sat and watched
him.
• * • *
It was late in the night when Dad came
from town. He staggered in with the neck of a
bottle showing out of his pocket. In his hand
was a piece of paper wrapped round the end of
some yards of sausage. The dog outside carried
the other end.
“ An’ ’e ishn’t dead ? ” Dad said after hearing
what had befallen Dave. “ Don’ b’leevsh id—
wuzhn’t bit. Die ’fore shun-’own ifsh desh ad’er
bish’m.”
“ Bit! ” Dave said bitterly, turning round to
the surprise of everyone. “I never said I was
bit. No one said I was —only those snivelling
idiots and that pumpkin-headed Irish pig there.’’
Maloney lowered his jaw and opened his eyes.
“ Zhackly. Dia’n’ I (hie) shayzo, ’Loney ?
Did’n’ I, eh, ol’ wom’n ? ” Dad mumbled, and
dropped his chin on his chest.
Maloney began to take another view of the
matter. He put a leading question to Joe.
“He muster been bit,” Joe answered, “ ’cuz he
had the d-death-adder in his hand.”
More silence.
“ Mush die ’fore shun’own,” Dad murmured.
Maloney w T as thinking hard. At last he spoke.
“ Bridgy,” he cried, “ where’s th’childer?” Mrs.
Maloney gathered them up.
dust then Dad seemed to be dreaming. He
swayed about. His head hung lower, and he mut-
tered : “ Shen’l’m’n, yoush disharged wish shanksh
y’cun’ry.”
The Maloneys left.
* * * *
Dave is still alive and well, and silent as
ever; and if any one question is more intolerable
and irritating to him than another, it is to be
asked if he remembers the time he was bitten by a
deaf-adder.

STEELE RUDD.
A Sydney suburban Mayor declared on
his election to the c 1 air, the other day, that he
felt an inward religious monitor urging him to
accept the position. We shall next hear, probably,
of a nuisance inspector having a spiritual call to
remove the defunct william-goat out of the alley.
Words that burn : “God Save the Queen.’
“ Billy ” Brookes has retired from the Q.
Leg. Council at the age of 72. He was for years
a sort of standing member for N. Brisbane, until
the plethorio M‘II wraith downed him in ’BB over
the “ Hopeful ” prisoners’ cases. Billy was an
obstinate “ Liberal,” as the party to which he
belonged was called, but he was, at any rate, con*
sistently Australian in opposing “black labor.” His
great misfortune was to have to creep under- the
wing of such a slippery opportunist as Griffith.
How Billy hated (politically) M ! llwraith ! What
he must have thought of the subsequent coalition
of Sam and Mac —of the M'llgriff, or the Griffil-
wraith—could not be adequately expressed except
in words of burning blasphemy. And Billy never
swore. He was a clean-living man—an under-
sized, parchmenty-skinned, spectacle - wearing
chap, with a forehead much larger than its depen-
dents ; and, withal, with a determination of chin
that suggested pugnacity and gave a misty, men-
tal apparition of a light-weight prizefighter. He
is old and feeble now, and animosities against him
have long since been forgotten. He was in the
hard-goods line, the name of his old firm having
been Brookes and Noble. The only political scan-
dal ever breathed against Billy was something
about an iron contract (Govt.) in connection with
one of the Brisbane River bridges (since swept
away); but there was probably little truth in it.

mised to try Australian horses at the Cape before
taking any definite action with regard to cavalry
remounts. They state that the purchase in the
Argentine Republic was only an experiment. Sir
Ocorcje Turner is satisfied with this explanation.
