Great Britain’s officers of War
(A stuck-up crowd, by all accounts),
They sent to Argentina for
A lot of cavalry remounts.
Australia’s horsey soul was stirred ;
Our land was wounded in its pride—
Until the cable brought us word
That Turner, knight, was satisfied.
1-Ie might have risen, full of wrath,
To cut the silken bonds in twain,
Brushing the Lion from his path,
And breaking up the Record Reign ;
But, no ; an explanation first
He sought, and meekly they replied.
War’s threat’ning clouds at once disperst
When Turner, knight, was satisfied.
Now Lady T., contented, greets
Her Gracious when she calls at Court,
And if she casually meets
The Prince of Wales, she doesn’t snort.
Joe Chamberlain says “All is right”—
And then he adds, in stage-aside—
“ I'll pull the silken bond more tight
Since Turner, knight, is satisfied.”

tired, my appetite failed, and my color was deathly. My father

anaemia, and sent me a bottle of medicine. I found that the medi-
cine did not agree with me and discontinued it. I was constantly
growing worse, and was subject to weak spells, and was fast

the grave,' and I strongly urge other girls who may be in a condi-
tion similar to mine to try them.”

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Look here, doctor,” said the man with the
scrubby, red moustache and the piggy eyes, “ it
ain’t no further use. This little matter has been
a-standin’ between you and me for two months
now, and I want the stuff.”
The doctor was looking out of the window of
his “surgery” past the other’s head. He saw
what he had seen so many times before, a sandy
space of ground, bleak in winter, burning in
summer, with its few anaemic trees struggling
bravely amid adverse surroundings, a disreputable
wire-fence or.two, and a background of State-
school—the last a thing of beauty formed of lime-
stone rubble and tin.
In the playground the children were occupied
in languid attempts at frolic. He knew most of
them. They were all “Rechabites” to a child,
and thus, in his club practice, he had many chances
of studying them. Their faint, raucous shouts and
yells came floating through the open window of
his office, and made a fitting accompaniment to
the scarcely less agreeable tones of the man with
the scrubby moustache.
The doctor sighed. He did not dispute the
debt. It was a very small one, but one of many,
and it had “ had to wait.”
“ And see here,” said the man, “ I ain’t a-goin’
to wait no longer ; so that’s flat. Folks as can’t
pay up shouldn’t indulge in luxuries.”
The luxury responsible for this debt was poultry
for the doctor’s invalid wife. The amount was
one pound. “ And so I tell you,” went on the
other, “ that unless I’m paid to-morrow I’m
a-goin’ to take out a summons —that’s straight.”
The summons would not have mattered so much
in itself, but it would have entailed consequences :
other summonses and possibly a meeting of
creditors.
And still the doctor looked vacantly out of his
window at the school-ground and wondered if any
one there could work out a little sum in arithmetic
such as the following : “Given cash in hand, nil ;
overdraft at bank, £lOO ; further debts, £150;
And JBI sterling.” He saw two boys mount the
tall swing in the school-yard and commence swing-
ing, standing-up. A curious fancy flitted across
his mind that they looked like the old-fashioned
“ monkeys on sticks ” with which he had played
as a boy. The man with the red moustache con-
tinued half-bullying, half-complaining, and the
children’s voices crept across the sandy space to
his half-attentive ear. And he recognised the
boys on the swing. One was the son of the man
opposite him—a Rechabite, of course. And the
swing went higher and higher, to and fro, to and
fro; very high now. But still the boys stooped and
straightened themselves and urged it higher and
higher—and—ah ! The doctor drew a long breath
and started up. The swing had “kicked,” and one
of the boys, his companion’s son, had lost his hold
and fallen, and he almost fancied he heard the
sound of the lad’s body as it struck the earth. The
children yelled and shrieked a little louder than
before, and then were silent. The doctor moved
to where his hat was and laid his hand on his bag.
“ Now, no sneaking off ! ” said the man with the
scrubby moustache, “Are you goin’to pay it or
not? That’s what I want to know before I leave
you. ’Cos if you’re not, so ’elp me gawd ”
“ I haven't the money in the house, man,” an-
swered the doctor, speaking for the first time in
many minutes, “ but will you take an order on the
Attorney-General ? •”
“ Oh ! ” responded the other, somewhat taken
aback. He had wanted the excuse for that sum-
mons very much.
“ I’ll take it if it’s goin’ to be honored ; but is
there anything cornin’ to you ? ”
“ There will be,” rejoined the doctor, in a day
or so. But if it is not honored you can summon
me then.”
“ Humph ! ” grunted his creditor ; I suppose
it’s better than nothing.”
The doctor made out the order dated that day
week, and handed it to the man, saying, “ And
now I must go. There’s been an accident at the
school. Perhaps you’d better come with me.”
As they left the house—the man with the mou-
stache wondering much what the accident had to
do with him—they were met by a breathless boy,
who gasped, all wide-eyed and pale: Please,
doctor, they wants yer quick at the school ! A
boy’s been and fell off of the swing, and they
think he’s dead.”
They hurried to the play-ground- Pushing
through the crowd of children and women from
neighboring houses, they found the schoolmaster
supporting the boy’s head on his lap. The man
with the moustache gave a hoarse ory and fell upon
his knees beside them.
The doctor waved aside the crowd, and after a
moment’s examination said, “Yes; I was afraid
so. He is quite dead.” They carried the body to
the school, and the doctor went slowly home.
* • * *
A week later the man with the scrubby red
moustache sent in his order on the Attorney-
General for collection. He got his money and
signed the following receipt
Attorney-General to Dr. .
Attending and giving evidence at inquest
touching death of George i. 14 (i !>
Received per 1 1 "
It referred to the death of his own son.

Comp.
The Vie. P.O. Commercial Directory for
1897-98, a biennial production, invaluable as a
book of reference and for banging anybody on the
head, has been published by Wise a.nd Co., of
Melbourne (and elsewhere in Australasia). It is
a great book, in the strictest sense of the term.
Germany has now the strongest artillery of
all armies—she lately spent on it <£15,000,000 in
one act. “ Completion of the war stores ” was
the official title of the item in the extraordinary
estimates, in which the colossal sum named was
demanded and unanimously voted without debate.
Only a few months ago the first intimations about
the real object of that considerable sum were
made in a clerical paper, and although they could
do no more harm the publication was stigmatised
as unpatriotic on all sides.
Southland (M.L.) Times states that at
Waikaha the highest wages of working men are
paid to rabbiters (trappers for the exporters). A
wages-sheet for one man s work for one month
totted up to <£3l odd ; and there are quite a number
who are making from =£2s to £2O a month. It is
computed that during the five months for which
the season lasts about <£130,000 is distributed in
wages, and this to a large extent among a class of
people who otherwise would find it hard to obtain
employment during winter, even at much lower
wages than they are making at the rabbit indus-
try. And yet it is proposed to insist on rabbit-
poisoning in Southland !
Dies Irce.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was some millions of years after the great Judg-
ment Day. Satan had been sleeping, and when
he awoke he felt somewhat chilly.
“ Confound those imps ! ” he exclaimed, “ I be-
lieve they’ve let the fires down a bit. A more
careless set of devils I never had to do with in all
my eternity. I’ll give them particular sheol tor
this.”
As he spoke he touched the electric bell, and a
few seconds afterwards a trapdoor in the floor fell
open, and a devil, burnt to a rich terra-cotta by
the eternal fires, shot into the presence of ttie
Lord of Hell.
“ How now, sir ? ” thundered the potentate ;
“ do vou think hell was meant to be an ice-house,
that you let the fires down so low ? You seem to
have mistaken your vocation. The whole stoking
department ought to have been angels with white
robes and crowns of glory. What do you mean
by it, anyhow? .
The stoker-devil trembled like a leaf. May it
please your majesty,” he said, “ it isn’t realty our
fault; the fact is, we are running out of fuel.
“ What ! ” roared Satan, “ running out of fuel,
with the whole universe to draw on? ”
“ We’ve been using ten constellations a minute
for the last three million years,” explained the
stoker ; “and now the supply has given out. We
kept the Milky Way till the last, hoping against
hope, but this week we had to use it. We are
running the furnaces now with the Magellan
Clouds, but the quality is none of the best, and
we’ll want a fresh supply in a few hours.”
“Search the ends of Space then!’ exclaimed
Satan, “ there must surely be any quantity of
constellations left.” m ,
The stoker-devil shook his head sadly. We ve
used ’em all up,” he replied. „ “Our cinder heap
reaches to the farthest depths.” _
“Search that cinder heap then'.’’cried Satan,
“ plenty of suns and constellations may have been
thrown carelessly out.” .
“No,” replied the stoker-devil despondently,
“ during the last million years the ashes have been
carefully sifted, and all burnable parts have been
returned to the furnaces.”
Satan ground his mighty teeth in despair.
“ This comes of damning too many souls, he ex-
claimed. “ I begin to think that I was far too
active in the old days. If I had had any foresight
at all I’d have let the parsons save all the souls
they wanted. Here I am brought to beggary in
my old age—burnt out of house and home by a
lot of miserable souls who have not the common
decency to thank me for giving them the chance.
But I will not submit, I’ll strain the universe
through a hair sieve before I give up the game.
Here, you, turn out ten thousand of those Aus-
tralian miners we damned in the nineteenth cen-
tury. Tell ’em to prospect all Space. Tell them
I’ll give threepence each for planets, and nine-
pence each for suns—in asbestos orders good for
whisky at the refreshment-room. And, while
they are routing around, I’ll see what I can find
on my own account.”
At once the message clanged throughout hell,
but before the order could be carried out Satan
took a header into the depths, on a quest of Ins
own. In a few seconds thousands of eager souls
swarmed out on to the clinker-heap. There they
were, gaunt and grim, seared by millions of years
of searching fires, grilled for ages on the unmelt-
ing fire-bars of the inferno, racked and torn by the
tireless torturers, and hideously disfigured by a
thousand endless and unimaginable torments.
But there they stood with the old prospecting
fever still strong within them, and before them
lay the biggest prospecting field ever offered to the
sons of men. There lay an apparently duffered-
out universe, with just a chance that somewhere in
it were constellations every sun of which was
worth a drink in the private bar of hell, and so
with a whoop that shook the grim portal of the
inferno they dashed into Space.
But little they recked of the task before them.
For days they wandered, that troop of discon-
solate diggers, and many a billion miles they
prospected without success. At last, when the
search seemed almost hopeless, something huge
and filmy hove in sisrht on the great black wall of
starless Space. With a simultaneous rush the
prospectors went for it, and, hooking themselves
on by every available point, they dragged it, with
a wild hurrah, through billions of miles of empti-
ness till they triumphantly dumped it at hell’s
gate, and sent it bodily down into the furnaces.
A stoker-imp rose up and thanked them, with
tears in his eyes.
“Just in time, oh, just in time !” he exclaimed,
in broken accents. “ The weather prophet in the
third gridiron has prophesied a white frost.”
But suddenly a mighty form spun up from the
exterior darkness. It was Satan, returned from a
fruitless search.
“I hear the roar of the furnaces,” he said;
“ what have you found ? ”
They told him.
“ ‘A filmy thing, floating in Space.’ Oh, fools ! ”
he cried, “you have gone and burnt up Heaven.
It won’t last ten minutes. We are lost! ”
And, sure enough, fifteen minutes later Hell
froze over.

IT
was said of the first Napoleon that one of the canons by which he regulated
the correspondence of his high office was this : “A letter that is not answered
answers itself.” A.nd in many instances this is, no doubt, true. “ Silence,”
we say, “ gives consent. ” Often, again, it means refusal. Much depends on
the form of the appeal ; and much on the relative positions of the two parties
to the communication.
REALLY
business-like people, however, in business affairs, will seldom adopt any of
these formulae of indifference, whether of princely or popular origin. A
neglected letter is, in some respects, like a neglected chill, or a neglected head-
ache, or a neglected physical ailment of any kind. Each of these, indeed, is a
message—a message from Nature. And it
POESN’T
do to trifle with her communications. She is too powerful, and we are
too puny ; and when our indifference ruffles her she has a way of calling us
angrily to account. A postcard will often answer a long letter; “ A stitch in
time saves nine,” and attention to a slight physical ailment, or apparently
trivial obstruction, may alike save unnecessary suffering and useless regret.
MATTER
in the wrong place is a famous definition of “ Dirt.” Equally is it true (very
often, at all events) of Disease. A sluggish liver, an impaired circulation or
recurrent indigestion ; a bilious attack or a sick headache; nervous disorders
and functional irregularities, each of these is often simply caused by “ Matter
in the wrong place.” Of each how often has one heard it said, “It really
doesn't matter ! ” And yet they all do : they are “ Matters that do matter ! ”
They are Nature’s MESSAGES ; and one of the best specifics—one that
is always found to ANSWER—is a timely purifying dose of BEECH AM’S
PI LLS. They are to be had of all Chemists ; so that for the purpose, at all
events, of obtaining a supply, wherever you may happen to be
